Summary 0:
EU rules on collective investment undertakings and marketing communications to investors
Umbrella Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — marketing requirements and fees and charges for collective investment firms
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 752/2019 — marketing rules for collective investments and on marketing communications addressed to investors and common principles concerning fees and charge levied on managers of collective investment companies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out uniform rules on the publication of national provisions concerning marketing requirements concerning collective investment organisations (AIFs and UCITS) and on their marketing communications, as well as common principles regarding fees and charged by managers for cross-border activities.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must publish national rules on marketing requirements for AIFs, UCITS and their managers on the websites of the competent authorities of the EU countries in which the marketing takes place. EU countries must also publish a central database containing summaries of national requirements for marketing communications and hyperlinks to the information published on the national websites of competent authorities.
The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) is responsible for ensuring that EU countries’ competent authorities publish the national rules and fees on managers’ charges on their websites.
Directive 2009/65/EC sets out common principles for the disclosure of the fees charged by collective investment funds and managers of UCITS. These principles are set out in Directive 2011/61/EU.
EU governments must ensure that the marketing communications sent to investors are clearly identifiable as such and that the risks and rewards of purchasing units or shares of an AIF or UCITS are clearly disclosed in an equally prominent manner.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 2 August 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s collective investment framework is designed to ensure that investors have access to a wide range of investment products and services across the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Collective investments’ on the European Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of entry into force

Summary 1:
Ensuring the safety of products on the EU market (EU harmonisation regulation) (European Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — market surveillance of products subject to the EU harmonisation legislation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to ensure that products covered by the EU’s market surveillance legislation are safe when they are placed on the market in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all manufactured products other than food, feed, medicinal products for human and veterinary use, living plants and animals, products of human origin and products of plants or animals relating directly to their future reproduction.
It does not deal with the protection of intellectual property rights (IPR).
Manufacturers must ensure that their products comply with the requirements of the legislation.
An interlocutor must be established in each EU country to act as a liaison point with the market surveillance authorities.
EU countries must also ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities have the necessary investigative powers to check whether a product complies with the legislation and to take appropriate action if it is found to be a threat to public health or safety.
National authorities must also be able to identify non-compliant and unsafe products and take appropriate measures to prevent them from being placed on their market.
The legislation does not apply to:
the manufacture of the product;
the distribution of the products;
market surveillance authorities’ powers to carry out market surveillance checks.
Implementation of the regulation
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation. It is assisted by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and the European Medicines Agency (EMSA).
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘The European Union’ is a single market for goods and services.
For more information, see:
‘‘The EU: the single market’’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 2:
Cross-border retransmission of television and radio programmes
Transmission of TV and radio programs
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/26/EU on the exercise of copyright and related rights by operators of online services that are ancillary to broadcasts
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that operators of internet-based retransmissions of television (i.e. live and on-demand) and radio (e.g. radio) programmes have the necessary rights to communicate to the public and make available to them their programmes.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all operators of retransmissable online services which aggregate broadcasts of television or radio programmes into packages and provide them to users simultaneously with the initial transmission of those broadcasts, unaltered and unabridged, using various techniques, such as cable, satellite, digital terrestrial, and mobile or closed circuit IP-based networks, as well as the open internet.
Ancillary online services include services that give access to material that enriches or otherwise expands the television or the radio programmes broadcast by the broadcasting organisation, including by way of previewing, extending, supplementing or reviewing the relevant programme's content.
EU countries must ensure that the operators of these services have the required rights to works and other protected subject matter for all the relevant territories, which further increases the complexity of the clearance of such rights.
Rights of broadcasting organisations
To retransmit initial transmissions of television programmes, operators must obtain an authorisation from the holders of the exclusive right of communication to the general public of works or other protected subjects.
The authorisation is valid for a period of 5 years from the date of receipt of the authorisation. The authorisation must be renewed at least once every 5 years.
Operators must also ensure that they respect the rights of the collective management organisations (collective management organisations) responsible for the distribution of the retransmitted programmes and for the collection and management of the revenues from those retransmits. They must also inform the public of the identity of the operators and of the rights they hold.
Collective Management organisations are responsible for:
collecting and managing the revenues generated by retransmitting programmes;
ensuring that the rights are exercised correctly and in a manner that is consistent with the rights covered by the directive;
monitoring the performance of the services they manage and ensuring that they comply with the directive’s requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
    —  
It has applied since 20 June 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  —
For more information, see:
‘Online retransmations’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 3:
Registry for the.eu.eu domain name
Registry of the.EU.eudomain name
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 — registry for the domain name ‘eu’
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a registry in the European Union (EU) to register domain names that are deemed to be defamatory, racist or otherwise contrary to the law of an EU country.
It aims to promote the use of, and access to, internet networks in accordance with Articles 170 and 171 TFEU by providing registration complementary to existing ccTLDs and to the global registration of generic TLDs.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The registry is responsible for the registration of domain names in the EU.
The regulation sets out the conditions for its implementation, including the designation and characteristics of the registry.
Eligibility
The registrant must be an EU citizen, independently of their place of residence;
a natural person who is not a EU citizen and who is resident in an EU Member State;
an undertaking that is established in the Union without prejudice to the application of national law.
Requests for a domain name must be made by the registrant on the basis of the following criteria:
the registrant’s identity;
the identity of the registrants’ business or organisation;
their contact details and contact details.
Reservation
The registration of a domain is subject to a number of conditions, including:
a minimum level of protection for the user;
protection of intellectual property rights;
promotion of the EU and the EU countries’ interests;
access to the internet for the purposes of the prevention, detection, investigation and prosecution of crimes, as provided for by EU or national law;
ensuring that the registry complies with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
Procedure
The Registry must:
ensure that domain names are registered in a manner compatible with EU law and with the principles set out in Article 11 of the regulation;
provide the public with access to the registry and to its services;
implement a system of dispute resolution (ADR) to settle disputes between registrants and registrants without resorting to judicial proceedings.
Dispute resolution
Registers may:
revoke or block domain names on their own initiative, without submitting the dispute to an ADR or judicial procedure;
suspend domain names if they have outstanding unpaid debts owed to the Registry;
non-fulfilment by the registry of the eligibility criteria pursuant to Article 3;
failure to comply with the registration requirements for registration requests;
breach of the registration procedures for domain names.
EU countries may also:
ban domain names they consider to be offensive or racist;
restrict domain names from future registration if they are considered by a final decision of a EU country court to be:
defamatory;
racist; or
contrary to public policy or contrary to public security under EU law or EU law under EU country law, or if they comply with national law with their reasonable justification;
ban or restrict domain names for a period of 5 years if they constitute a threat to public order, security or public policy, or are considered to be contrary to national law or to the public security of the Member State concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   ...
It has applied since 1 January 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The.eu TLD is part of the Internet governance multistakeholder model.
For more information, see:
‘Registry’ on the European Commission's website.

Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 4:
Cohesion Fund for Energy and Transport (CEF) 2021-2027 — guidelines for the TEN-T guidelines
Cohesion fund for energy and transport (CET) 2021/2027 – Guidelines for the CET
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — guidelines on the European Commission’s framework for the Cohesion Fund 2021-2020
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down guidelines for how the European Union (EU), through the European Regional Development Fund (ERCF), will finance transport projects in EU countries eligible for financing from the European Social Fund (ESF).
It sets out how the CEF will support the European Transport Network (ETN) and how it will contribute to the decarbonisation of all modes of transport in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The European Commission is responsible for developing and implementing the guidelines.
The regulation sets out the guidelines for funding the European Network for Energy & Transport (ENET) which aims to:
ensure the accessibility and connectivity of all EU countries and regions by 2020;
facilitate the transport of goods, goods, capital and services across the EU, including remote and outermost regions;
improve the efficiency of transport infrastructure;
encourage the use of new technologies and innovation in the transport sector;
support the development of smart mobility solutions;
promote energy efficiency and energy security;
increase the environmental impact of transport;
provide the EU countries with the necessary resources to meet their climate and energy efficiency targets;
enable the development and use of alternative fuels;
contribute to the creation of a low-carbon transport network.
Funding guidelines
The CEF is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Stability Mechanism (ESM).
The funding will be allocated on a competitive basis to the greatest possible number of projects located in the Member States eligible for funding from the EEF, with priority to cross-border links and missing links. The EIB will also support the ETS and the ESM.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Energies between the transport, energy and digital sectors enable the EU to meet its climate objectives.
For more information, see:
Transport and energy (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
  The European Commission adopts the guidelines of the European Community for the Transport Network 2021/2020 on behalf of the EU (Cet) (OJ L 183, 1.4.2019, pp. 1-24)

Summary 5:
Terrorist content: hosting service providers’ obligations (EU directive on the removal of terrorist content) (European Commission). (European Parliament and Council Directive on the prevention and combatting of terrorist offences)
Terrorist material: hosted by a hosting service provider
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/849 on the hosting of terrorist material
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DIRECTIVE?
The regulation aims to improve the detection and removal of content online that incites or solicits someone to commit, or to contribute to the commission of, terrorist offences, or glorifies terrorist activities including by disseminating material depicting a terrorist attack.
The directive is part of the EU’s digital single market (DSM), which aims to ensure that all EU citizens have the same access to the internet.
KEY POINTS
The legislation applies to all providers of relevant services offered in the EU, irrespective of the country of their main establishment. It does not apply to providers of information society services which store and disseminate to the public information and material provided by a user of the service on request.
Hosting service providers must:
ensure that their services are used by a significant number of users in one or more EU countries, or that their activities are targeted towards one EU country;
remove or disable access to terrorist content following an assessment by the competent authorities;
provide the competent authority with information on procedures and applicable deadlines at least 12 hours in advance of issuing for the first time a removal order.
They must ensure that the removal order is authentic and that the information provided by the host is accurate. They must also ensure that:
the order is issued in a language easily understood by the user;
the removal order has been executed in accordance with the law of the Member State in which the hosting service is located;
there is a written record of the order and of the date and the time it was issued.
In addition, the regulation lays down a number of other safeguards to protect users’ personal data, including:
protection of personal data in the event of a breach of an order;
impartiality of the decision to remove the content;
ensuring that the host does not infringe the rights of the user in the course of its removal procedure;
protecting the personal data of users who have been the subject of a temporary or permanent removal order and who have not been informed of the reasons for their removal;
taking all necessary measures to ensure the protection of their personal data.
Directive 2014/33/EU sets out a framework for cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities in the fight against terrorist offences.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    —
It has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Hosting service provider: a service provider whose main purpose is to host terrorist material.
* Terrorist offences: any act or omission which incites, solicits or participates in terrorist activities.

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027 (FPRI 2021-2020)
Framework Program for Research & Innovation 2021 - 2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/2027 establishing the framework programme for research and innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE PROGRAMME?
The regulation establishes the framework for the EU’s investment in R&I.
The programme aims to support the creation, better diffusion and transfer of high-quality and excellent knowledge and technologies in the EU, attract talent at all levels and contribute to full engagement of the EU's talent pool, facilitate collaborative links and strengthen the impact of R&Is in developing, supporting and implementing the EU.
It sets out the objectives of the programme, including:
the creation of jobs and growth in the European Union (EU);
the transfer of knowledge and technology to the European economy;
the development of innovative products and services for the benefit of the European society and the world.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The FPRI is funded for the duration of the multiannual financial framework (MFF) 2021–2027 as laid down in Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2020/2093 (see summary).
It is open to all EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA), as well as non-EU countries.
Funding is available for research, innovation and technological development (R&I) activities in the fields of science, technology and innovation (S&T) and in the areas of culture and creative sectors (Culture, Innovation and Entrepreneurship (CiE), Education, Science and Technology (STEM) and the Environment).
Projects are eligible for funding under the 'widening participation and spreading excellence' component of the FPRIs. Projects must meet the following criteria:
contribute to the development of knowledge-based solutions for the common challenges of the future;
promote the creation of knowledge based on the principles of open science, open innovation and open to the world, while safeguarding the Union's interests.
Applicants must clearly show that projects are linked with national and/or regional research strategies to be eligible to apply for funding.
They must also demonstrate that they meet the criteria set out in the programme's strategic planning and that they are compatible with other EU programmes, such as Horizon 2020, the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Social Fund (ESF) or the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The European Commission is responsible for administering the programme.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The framework programme runs from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2023.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘Framework programme’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 7:
EU Space Programme
EU Space programme
SUMMEMBER OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — rules for the EU’s space programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for implementing the EU Space Programme (ESA) and lays down the procedure for establishing and implementing the budget through grants, procurement, prizes and indirect implementation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
ESA supports autonomous, reliable and cost-effective access to space, especially as regards critical infrastructure and technology, public security and the security of the EU and its Member States.
The programme is funded by the European Union (EU), the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom) and the European Space Agency (ESA).
Objectives
ESA aims to:
ensure that the EU has a high level of autonomy in the use of space;
provide the necessary resources and expertise to meet the new needs of users;
facilitate the development of new technologies in the space sector;
promote cooperation between EU and non-EU countries in the area of space research and development;
support research and innovation in the areas of space and space-related fields such as space tourism, space tourism and space security;
develop partnerships between EU countries and international organisations such as the United Nations Space Agency, the International Space Agency and the Organisation for the Prohibition of Chemical Weapons (OPCW).
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the ESA programme and for the management of the budget.
the programme is managed by the Commission, which is assisted by the Agency for Safety and Security at Work.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
  
Regulations (EU/EU) 2017/853 and (EU, Euratom and Euratom) 2019/856 have applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The ESA is one of the most innovative and innovative organisations in the world.
For more information, see:
ESA (European Space Agency).

Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Hubs (EDIH)
European digital innovation hubs (EDIIH)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on the European Digital Innovation Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE PROGRAMMING?
   
 
The European Digital Investment Fund (EDIH) is the European Union’s (EU) innovation programme for the period 2021-2027.
The EDIH aims to support the development of innovative, high-quality and secure solutions in the areas of data infrastructure, connectivity and cybersecurity.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme is funded by the EU budget. It is designed to support EU countries’ efforts to implement smart growth in areas such as:
high-quality data infrastructure;
connectivity;
security;
cybersecurity.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote the adoption of advanced digital technologies by industry, in particular by SMEs and by other entities that employ up to 3,000 people (mid-caps), by public organisations and by the academic community.
To achieve these objectives, the EIIH supports:
the development of high-performance computing (HPC) and high performance computing (HPPC) solutions;
the creation of a high-performing data infrastructure and cybersecurity solutions for the European supply chain;
research and development (R&D) activities;
innovation in the area of information and communication technology (ICT);
the establishment of a European digital innovation hub;
facilitating the exchange of best practice and best practice in the field of information, communication and communication technologies (ICT) in the EU;
exchange of best practices in R&D;
creating a network of EU-wide digital innovation centres.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 			
The EU has been investing in the digital economy for more than a decade.
For more information, see:
‘Digital Europe’ on the EIBM website.

Summary 9:
Sustainable EU internal market
Sustainable Union internal market	SUMMARY OF:
Programme for the implementation of the European Union’s (EU) internal market policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE PROGRAMME?
The programme aims to improve the functioning of the internal market by:
improving the application of the principle of mutual recognition in the area of goods and services;
ensuring the protection of consumers’ rights when goods are imported from economic operators based in non-EU countries, including when they are sold online.
KEY POINTS
Mutual recognition
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve mutual recognition between EU countries’ authorities. These include:
the harmonisation of the rules on the import and export of goods by EU countries (Regulation (EC) No 223/2009);
the creation of a single market information system (SIS) for EU citizens and businesses;
the establishment of a European information society (EISA) to support the development of information and communication technologies (ICTs) in the EU.
The EU has also adopted measures to:
ensure that EU legislation is fit for purpose and, when new risks emerge, identifies potential areas of action;
promote the proper implementation and application of EU legislation.
SIS
The SIS is designed to help small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) to grow and diversify their businesses. It aims to support SMEs in the following areas:
finance;
access to finance for SMEs;
supporting SMEs’ growth and diversification of their activities;
encouraging SMEs to take advantage of the opportunities offered by the SIS.
FROM WHEN DOES THE PROGRAMMENATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The internal market has contributed to the creation of jobs and has offered greater choice of high-quality products and services at lower prices for consumers.
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable European internal market’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 10:
Firearms and essential components — traceability of firearms and essential parts (EU directive on firearms and their essential components)
Firearm and essential elements — tracing firearms and components
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/849 on the tracing of firearms, essential components and ammunition
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities are able to trace the ownership, ownership and destruction of firearms*, essential components* and ammunition.
It codifies and repeals Directive 1999/47/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
firearms designed for military use, such as the AK47 and the M16, and which are equipped to operate on the basis of selective fire, where they may be manually adjusted between automatic and semi-automatic firing modes, and are classified as category A firearms and should therefore be prohibited for civilian use.
Firearms which use rimfire percussion, including those with a calibre of.22 or smaller, should not be classified in category A unless they have been converted from automatic firearms.
EU countries may, however, authorise the acquisition and possession of firearms classified in the category A when necessary for historical, cultural, scientific, technical, educational or heritage purposes, provided that such museums and collectors demonstrate that they have taken the necessary measures to address any risks to public security or public order, including by way of proper storage.
National authorities must ensure that the firearms are marked with a clear, permanent and unique marking and registered in the data-filing systems of the EU countries and that they are destroyed after the destruction of the firearms or essential components concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCEVIDENCE
   
Directives (EU countries had to incorporate the directive into national law by 1 January 2018.

Summary 11:
Maritime National Single Window (EMSWe)
Maritime Network of Single Windows (EMSW)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment of a maritime national single window
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to simplify and harmonise the reporting procedures for shipping companies by EU legal acts, by international legal acts and by national law of EU countries.
It also aims to improve the efficiency of maritime transport.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to shipping companies that:
make a port call in an EU country;
provide one or more categories of port services listed in Article 1(2) of the European Parliament and of the Council Directive 2002/59/EC (see summary);
have at least one vessel registered in the EU.
EU countries must:
establish a maritime single window by 15 February 2020;
ensure that the information provided by shipping companies to the single window is in the language of the EU country in which the ship is registered;
designate a national authority responsible for the operation of the national window.
National authorities must ensure that the national national single windows provide the same information to the European Commission.
The national authorities must also:
set up and maintain a centralised reporting interface module;
establish an electronic data-processing system for the data set and define definitions, categories and data specifications for each data element;
inform the Commission of any reporting obligations stemming from national legislation and requirements, as well as of any new reporting obligations adopted by the European Union legal acts.
Each EU country must designate a national focal point for the implementation of the regulation. The focal point must be located in the port of the country where the ship makes its port call and must be accessible to the national authorities responsible for its operation.
Reporting
The directive sets out a set of reporting requirements for the shipping companies. These include:
the information that is required for port calls;
the number of items that are to be reported to each maritime national window;
a list of the port services that must be provided for operational purposes when a ship makes a port visit;
information that is to be provided by the ship’s operator when authorised by the port or terminal operator, and taking into account confidentiality, commercial sensitivities and legal constraints.
In addition, the directive sets up a list of hazardous and polluting goods that are required to be notified to the EU single window, based on the relevant data elements from the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) Convention and Codes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
    —
It has applied since 15 February 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 15 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Maritime national single-window: a single window that provides the information that a shipping company must provide to the Commission when a vessel makes a maritime port call.
* Harnessing: a means of transferring information between the vessel and the operator of the vessel.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —  
Commission Implementing Decision 2018/1825 of 14 February 2018 on the establishing of a European Maritime National Window (EMN-2020) (OJ L 183, 15.3.2018, pp. 1-8)
last update 15.02.2019



Summary 12:
Interoperability of electronic road toll systems (EOTs)
Interoperation of electronic roads tolls (EUTS)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on the interoperability of European Union electronic toll systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to make electronic road-based toll (OE) systems interoperable across the EU.
KEY POINTS
The directive sets out the rights of EETS providers (e.g. toll chargers) and EETS users.
EETS providers have the right to:
set up an EETS domain and to publish all the necessary information relating to access rights in an EET domain statement;
provide EETS services in accordance with the principles set out in the directive.
The EETS provider must:
ensure interoperability with other EETS domains and with the existing interoperability constituents of the EETS service provider.
Provide EETs services in line with the EU’s ‘user pays’ and ‘polluter pays principles’.
Ensure that the EET providers’ invoices are sent on their behalf and in their name, since invoicing directly in the name of the toll charger can, in certain cases, have adverse administrative and tax implications.
Safeguard personal data
EETs providers must ensure that:
personal data are protected;
the EET service provider is responsible for the processing of personal data;
they have the power to verify that the remuneration offered by the toll-charger to the Eets providers respects the principles laid out in this directive;
their EET services are free of charge to EET users and that they are free to use the services of other EET operators.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Electronic road tolls’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Electronic road toll system (OE): a system that allows users of light-duty vehicles to pay a toll on the road by means of an OBE (radio-based electronic communication system).
* EOTs: a system where the road fee due is linked to the time spent by the vehicle on the tolled infrastructure (for example, time-based systems such as vignettes) or to the vehicle passing a specific point.

Summary 13:
European network of immigration liaison officers (ELECOSCE)
European network OF IMMIGRATION OFFICERS (EOLs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — European network of migration liaison officers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a network of EOLs to support the EU’s migration policy in the field of migration.
KEY POINTS
The ELCO network is designed to:
ensure that EU countries, the European Commission and the European Union Agency for Fundamental Rights (TFEU) more systematically coordinate the tasks and roles of their liaison officers deployed in non-EU countries;
optimise the utilisation of the network of EU officers deployed by EU countries and the Commission and EU agencies to third countries in order to implement EU priorities more effectively while respecting the national competences of EU countries.
It also aims to reduce information gaps and duplication of work and maximise operational capabilities and effectiveness.
The regulation repeals Regulation (EC) No 377/2004, which had been in force since 1 October 2018.
EOLS’ responsibilities
EU countries have the responsibility to deploy ELCOs within their territory and to ensure that they carry out their duties in accordance with the relevant international and European Union (TEU) and EU countries’ national laws.
They are responsible for the prevention and tackling of irregular migration and for preventing, investigating, detecting and prosecuting smuggling of migrants or trafficking in human beings, as well as the trafficking of human beings.
trafficking of migrants and human beings is punishable by death or serious injury.
In addition to their duties, EOLOsCE officers are also responsible for:
collecting, analysing and analysing information on irregular migration;
carrying out investigations and investigations on human trafficking of migrants;
ensuring the protection of the rights of asylum seekers and of applicants for international protection;
monitoring and assessing the impact of EU action on migration, particularly in the context of the Schengen acquis.
OLCOsCELECOs’ duties
The OLCOCCOs have the following responsibilities:
carry out their tasks within the territory of the EU country in which they are deployed;
collect, analyse and analyse information on the migration situation in the country of origin or transit;
assist EU agencies in their functions and tasks;
act as facilitators and providers of information from third countries to support EU agencies, in particular where EU agencies have not yet established cooperative relations with third countries, and in particular in the areas of security and border control;
share personal data processed in the course of their duties with law enforcement authorities and within networks of EU liaison officers, provided that the personal data in question is necessary either for preventing and tackling irregular migration or for the investigation, investigation, detection and prosecution of smuggling of migration or human beings;
transfer personal data to the competent national authorities of the countries of origin and transit of migrants, if necessary for the purpose of the prevention or investigation of smuggling, trafficking in migrants, trafficking of smuggled migrants, human beings or trafficking for the purposes of the prosecution of trafficking for human beings and for the provision of assistance to the victims of human trafficking;
participate in the activities of the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCA) and the Directorate-General for Migration and Integration (DGMI).
OOCOs must be able to carry out the following tasks:
monitor and analyse irregular migration flows;
take appropriate action to prevent, investigate, detect and prosecute irregular migration, including by means of information sharing and cooperation with other EU agencies and other EU instruments or structures, including the work of local immigration cooperation groups;
report to the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of EU migration policy;
coordinate their work with the Commission, the TFEU and other relevant EU agencies;
operate in line with the requirements of the Treaty on European Union and the EU countries' national laws and regulations;
provide information to the relevant national authorities and the competent authorities of other EU countries on the operational aspects of their work;
cooperate with each other and with other relevant national and international organisations, such as the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR), the European Migration Agency and the Organisation for Security and Cooperation in Europe (OLAF).
The European Commission is responsible for coordinating the tasks of the OLCOs deployed in third countries and for ensuring that they fulfil their duties and that they do not duplicate the responsibilities of their counterparts in the EU.

Summary 14:
Security of identity cards and residence cards
Security of identities and residence documents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — security standards for identity cards
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the security of EU citizens’ identity cards by introducing enhanced security standards.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that EU citizens have access to biometric identification and authentication when using their identity cards or residence documents for identification purposes.
The regulation applies to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality.
It does not apply to non-EU citizens who do not have the necessary travel documents or do not live in an EU country and therefore do not qualify for the right of free movement.
EU citizens have the right to use their identity card or residence card to travel within the EU without having to register with the relevant authorities in the EU country in which they live or work.
This regulation does not require EU countries to issue, under national law, other residence documents which fall outside the scope of EU law, for example residence cards issued to all residents on the territory regardless of the nationality. However, EU countries may require EU citizens and their family members who are not nationals of the EU to register in the relevant EU country for the purpose of verifying their identity.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The European Commission has to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat fraud in the area of travel documents. These include:
Directive 2004/38/EC on travel documents (see summary);
EU rules on identity cards, residence cards and travel documents for non-national citizens;
EU guidelines on the use of biometric data for the verification of identity and residence card data.
For more information, see:
‘Biometric data verification’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management (EES, VIS, ETIAS, Eurodac, EuroDAC, SIS and ECRIS-TCN)
Interoperation of the European Union Information System for Security, Border and Migration Management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — interoperability of the EU information system for security and border and mobility management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to improve the interoperability between the EU’s information systems to ensure that border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities have the necessary information at their disposal to prevent, detect or investigate terrorist offences or other serious criminal offences.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the European European System for Electronic and Border and Movement Management, the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBSA), the European Information System (EIS) and the European Security and Border Control Agency (ESBCA).
It sets up a European search portal (ESP), a shared biometric matching service (shared BMS), a common identity repository (CIR) and a multiple-identity detector (MID) as interoperability components.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 552/2004.
The EBSA and EES are used by EU countries’ border control authorities to check the validity of travel documents and for the purposes of preventing, detecting, detecting or investigating terrorist offences and other serious crimes.
They are also used by Europol and Interpol.
Eurodac is used by the European Criminal Police Organization (Interpol is used for the investigation of terrorist offences in the EU and by the Interpol database of Stolen and Lost Travel Documents (SLTD).
The CIR is used to store the personal data that are necessary to enable the more accurate identification of the individuals whose data are stored in those systems, including their identity data, travel document data and biometric data, regardless of the system in which the data were originally collected.
EU countries must ensure that the authorities using those systems are able to conduct sufficiently justified or unjustified verifications of the identities of the data stored in different systems to verify that a person has not been recorded in the different systems.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 			
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
For more information, see:
European Union information systems (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interoperability: the ability of an EU country to exchange information between its national information systems and those of other EU countries.
* Identities: a person's identity and travel document information.

Summary 16:
Renewable energy targets for 2020
Renewables for 2020 — EU targets
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1999 — EU renewable energy targets
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
It sets out EU countries’ targets for the share of renewable energy in energy consumption and in the transport sector to be met by 2020.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
they set a baseline renewable energy share by 2020 of at least 50 % of their total energy consumption in the energy and transport sectors, i.e. the total energy consumed by households and businesses in the EU by that year;
they calculate the percentage of total renewable energy used by households, businesses and other end-use sectors by the end of that year.
They must also ensure that the calculation methods take into account:
the energy consumption of heating and cooling systems in all EU countries;
the share of energy used in all other end use sectors;
energy consumption by households in the food and beverage sectors; and
the amount of waste generated in the waste sector.
The directive also provides for a partial exemption from the calculation of the final consumption of final energy consumption for the transport and waste sectors by EU countries that do not meet the EU’s greenhouse gas (GHG) reduction targets.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
Directive 2009/28/EC law sets binding national targets for renewable energy consumption by 2020 for energy and the transport sectors.
It repeals and replaces Directive 2009/27/EC.
For more information, see:
‘Directive on renewable energy’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 17:
Electronic Communications Directive (ECD)
Electronic Communication Directive (ECD)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/852 on the European Union’s electronic communications directive
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that electronic communications networks and services provide a high level of protection for end-users and their rights.
It repeals Directive 2002/19/EC, 2002/20/EC and 2002/22/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all electronic communications services, whether they are fixed-line or wireless. It does not apply to the content of services delivered over electronic communication networks, such as broadcasting content, financial services and certain information society services, and is without prejudice to measures taken at EU or national level in respect of such services, in accordance with EU law, in order to promote cultural and linguistic diversity and to ensure the defence of media pluralism.
The scope of the directive does not cover the use of radio spectrum by all electronic networks, including the emerging self-use of the spectrum by new types of networks consisting exclusively of autonomous systems of mobile radio equipment that is connected via wireless links without a central management or centralised network operator, and not necessarily within the exercise of any specific economic activity.
Rights and obligations
EU countries must ensure that the rights and obligations of the end-user are effectively and equally protected when using e-communications services. The directive sets out a number of rights, including:
the right to use of the radio spectrum and the right to be treated as an end-to-end user of e-communication services, i.e. the right of access to e-services, e.g. access to the internet, and services not covered by this directive.
the rights of the provider and end users when using a service.
National authorities must ensure the protection of the rights of end users and the protection for their rights and interests when using an electronic communications service, including by:
ensuring that the service is provided in a manner that is compatible with the legislation;
providing a service that is free of charge and that is accessible to all users;
protecting the privacy and the security of personal data;
encouraging the exchange of information between providers and users, including through the sharing of information on the service;
promoting the harmonisation and harmonisation of the rules on the use and use of electronic communications;
supporting the development of a single market for e-commerce, e-business and e-government services.
Regulatory framework
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the ECD. It is assisted by the European Telecommunications Regulatory Agency (ETRA) and the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (EASW).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The ECD has applied since 1 July 2017 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Electronic communications directive (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  ‘Directive 2009/9/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 2009 on the electronic communications Directive (OJ L 183, 1.7.2009, pp. 1-12)
last update 07.10.2017



Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of CO2 emissions and fuel consumption of new heavy-duty vehicles in the EU (Regulation (EU) 2018/858)
Monitoring, reporting and collection of data on new heavy and light commercial vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 595/2009 on the monitoring and reporting obligations of manufacturers of new light and heavy commercial vehicles and their trailers in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the requirements for manufacturers to monitor and report to the European Commission data on the registration of all new heavy vehicles and all new trailers, including data on powertrains as well as the relevant bodywork.
KEY POINTS
Manufacturers must:
monitor and report the data to the Commission by 1 January 2019;
report the results of on-road verification tests by 31 December 2020;
pay for the data by 1 February 2021.
The data must be collected by the competent authorities of the EU countries and the European Environment Agency (EEA).
The EEA is responsible for the exchange of the data with the competent national authorities of EU countries, manufacturers and the EEA, and for the management of the final database on behalf of the Commission.
EU countries must ensure that the data are accurate and up-to-date. They must also inform the Commission of any deviations from the required data.
If a manufacturer deviates from the data recorded in the framework of the regulation, the manufacturer may be subject to administrative fines where the manufacturer fails to deliver the data within the applicable deadline or if the manufacturer does not comply with other EU rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION APPLY?
Implementation has started since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from heavy and medium commercial vehicles in order to meet the EU’s 2030 target of reducing GHG emissions by 40 % compared to 2005.
For more information, see:
Heavy-duty vehicle emissions (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Heavy-duty: vehicles with a gross tonnage of more than 1,000 kg.
* Light commercial vehicle: vehicles weighing less than 10 tonnes.

Summary 19:
EU solidarity and coordination in the event of gas supply disruptions and crises (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
EU solidarity & coordination in case of gas disruptions and crisis (Council Regulation (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 852/2014 on solidarity and cooperation in the case of natural gas supply and crisis
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to ensure that all the necessary measures are taken to safeguard an uninterrupted supply of gas throughout the EU, in particular to protected customers, in order to avoid severe disruption of the gas supply.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
EU countries must ensure that:
they take all necessary measures to ensure the protection of protected customers (e.g. healthcare, essential social care, emergency and security services) from the effects of a gas supply disruption or crisis;
they ensure that their national authorities have the necessary resources to deal with the situation; and
they have the capacity to respond quickly and effectively to any crisis or crisis.
Protected customers must be treated as solidarity protected customers. They must be guaranteed uninterrupted gas supply to all essential services provided by the public administration of the EU country in which they live or work, including:
healthcare,
emergency services,
education,
essential social services, and
security services.
services provided by a public administration,
transport,
information and communication,
including information exchange,
communications,
telecommunications, and information exchange and
information exchange.
They must be provided by:
the national authorities of EU countries and the European Commission;
the EU countries’ national authorities and the Commission.
National authorities must:
identify the main gas supply sources and routes;
assess the risk of a disruption of supply;
prepare preventive action plans and emergency plans;
coordinate with each other and with other EU countries;
ensure that the relevant EU countries have the resources to cope with the impact of a supply disruption;
provide information to the public and the public authorities of the affected EU countries.
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring the implementation of the regulation and ensuring that it is implemented correctly and effectively.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘The EU solidarity and crisis response’ on the EU’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 20:
Labelling of energy-related products in the EU (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Labelling in the European Union (Regulating (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/922 on the labelling of products in energy-intensive sectors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that consumers can make informed choices based on the energy consumption of energy related products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
products in the following product groups:
electrical products;
electromagnetic products (e.g. light bulbs);
space and water heating products; and
air-conditioning and heating systems.
It does not apply to second-hand products, unless they are imported from a non-EU country.
Suppliers must provide a printed label together with each unit of a product.
The label must contain the following information:
the product’s energy consumption;
the name of the supplier and the number of units of the product in each class;
how the product is being used;
where the product falls into the top classes of energy consumption, the type of label shown on the label.
If a product group is rescaled, the supplier must replace the label on the affected products displayed in shops within a short timeframe, and by organising adequate consumer information campaigns clearly indicating that a new version of the label has been introduced.
Labels must be clearly visible and clearly legible. They must be printed in a colour that is easy to read and can be easily identified.
Disputes between suppliers must be settled by a dispute resolution procedure.
EU countries must establish a product database for suppliers to check that they comply with the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
Regulation(EU) No 852/2017 has applied since 1 August 2017.
Regulations (EU), EU countries and the European Commission have to incorporate it into national law by 1 August 2018.
BACKGROUND
 Â  Â
     
For more information, see:
‘Energy labelling’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at European Commission's website.

Summary 21:
Protecting personal data in the digital age: European Union (EU) 2017/1821 — data protection in the EU (European Union) (European Commission)
Protecting the personal data of EU citizens
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 521/2017 — EU rules on the protection of personal data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU citizens’ personal data are protected when they provide their consent to the processing of their data by third parties.
It sets out the rights of data subjects and the obligations of those who process and determine their personal data.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality or place of residence, in relation to:
the processing of data by a third party (e.g. a processor or a controller) in the context of a payment, irrespective of whether the payment is connected to a payment;
the use of the processing by the third party for the purpose of providing goods or services to such data subjects irrespective of the payment.
EU countries must ensure that:
data subjects are not deprived of the protection to which they are entitled under this regulation, even if they are in an EU country other than their own;
they have the right to be informed and to have their data processed in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation;
third parties are subject to the same rules as third parties when processing their own personal data;
processing of their own data must be authorised by the competent authorities of the EU countries.
The protection of data is not restricted or prohibited for reasons connected with:
personal data processing;
data processing of third parties is not prohibited for the purposes of the prevention, investigation, detection or prosecution of criminal offences or the execution of criminal penalties,
the safeguarding against and prevention of threats to public security, so that the processing for those other purposes, in so far as it is within the scope of EU law, falls within the regulation’s scope.
Rights and obligations of the third parties
The third party responsible for processing personal data must:
ensure that personal data is protected when processing third parties’ business activities;
provide a clear, understandable and understandable explanation for the processing and the use of their business activities.
Third parties must also ensure that third parties respect the following rights and obligations:
they respect personal data subjects’ rights, including the right of access to information, including:
privacy and security;
access to personal data, including access to their names, addresses and contact details;
information security, including information security measures;
identification of persons who have been identified or identifiable;
protection of the identity of persons whose personal data has been used for third party purposes;
use of third party services, such as payment processing, for purposes other than providing goods and services to third parties;
transparency, including by setting up a central register of third-party service providers;
security and data protection measures to protect third parties and third parties, including with regard to third party service providers.
In addition, third parties must ensure:
that third parties comply with the data protection rules of the regulation and with the relevant national data protection legislation;
that they respect the rights and responsibilities of the persons whose data they process or determine to process third parties' business activities, including their right to access and use personal data and their rights to have access to the information they process and use third parties', and
the protection of the data that they have processed or used.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The European Commission has adopted the regulation on behalf of the European Parliament and of the Council.
BACKGROUND
Directive 95/46/EC (the European Union data protection framework) aims to harmonise the protection requirements of fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons in respect of processing activities and to ensure the free flow of data between Member States.
For more information, see:
‘Data protection’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 22:
Harmonised indices of consumer prices and of the house price index (HPI) at EU and national level
Harmonomically-based consumer price indices
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — harmonised indices for the development, production and dissemination of harmonised consumer prices (HICP, HICP-CT, OOH price index) and the harmonised index for the construction of the home (HCP-CT) at European Union (EU and national levels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to establish a common framework for developing, producing and disseminating harmonised national indices of the consumer price index and the housing price index.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
The indices are based on the price changes and weights of products included in the household final monetary consumption expenditure (FMC) of households.
The data are collected by Eurostat, the European Commission’s statistical office.
EU countries must:
provide the Commission with the sources and methods used by EU countries to calculate the indices;
ensure that the national indices meet the comparability requirements for the harmonisation of the indices at international level in accordance with changes to the United Nations Committee on the Internationalisation of Consumer Prices (UN COICOP);
implement implementing measures and delegated acts to ensure that the indices comply with the regulation.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts concern the following:
sampling and representativity;
collection and treatment of prices;
replacements and quality adjustment;
index compilation;
revisions;
special indices.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Consumer price index: a measure of the price of goods and services in a given EU country.
* Household final monetary expenditure: the total value of all household goods, services and capital that is spent on the purchase of a product.
Household final consumption expenditure: a sum of all the household goods and capital which is used to purchase a product, such as a car, a house, a mortgage or a loan.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, Euram) No 2352/2009 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 23:
Anti-dumping — rules for the EU’s trade policy in the field of trade and investment
Anti-Dumping — Rules for the trade in goods and investments
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — rules on the application of the rules of the 1994 Anti-Dump Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It lays down the rules for applying the rules on trade and investments in the fields of trade, investment and investment in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the conditions under which the EU may impose anti-dump duties on imports of certain goods from non-EU countries. These duties may be imposed on the basis of:
the normal value of the imports;
the average weighted average costs for the period of investigation;
provisional duties.
Provisional measures may also be imposed in cases of suspected violation or where further investigation is necessary to supplement the findings of the investigation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
  The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat dumping, including:
rules on the import and export of certain agricultural products and services;
rules for the investigation of imports of agricultural products into the EU;
measures to combat the dumping of agricultural and forestry products and agricultural services in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Dumping’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 24:
Countervailable subsidies — EU rules on trade policy
Countervailing subsidies — Union rules on Trade policy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — countervailable subsidy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the application of EU trade policy in the context of the EU’s anti-subsidy policy.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ trade policy is based on the principle of ‘fair competition’.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the principles and procedures for determining whether a subsidy is ‘countervailable’ (i.e. that it has caused material injury or is threatening to cause injury) and the amount of the subsidy. It also sets out the procedures for the rejection of complaints or the initiation of proceedings.
In addition, the regulation defines the conditions for the imposition of provisional and definitive duties. These are designed to protect the interests of EU countries against unfair trade practices.
Provisional and definitive measures
EU countries may impose provisional duties on imports of certain goods from non-EU countries if they believe that they have caused injury or are threatening to do so. They may also impose definitive duties if they consider that the imports have caused serious injury or threaten to cause serious injury.
Duties may also be imposed on the export of certain products to the EU.
Implementation of the regulation is governed by a number of rules, including:
provisional duties may be imposed in cases of suspected violation of the rules and in which the import of a subsidised product is likely to cause material injury;
definitive measures may be adopted in cases where there is evidence that the subsidy is causing material injury and that it threatens to cause such injury. These may be based on:
the nature of the imports;
the quantity of the goods imported; and
the type of goods imported.
Definitive and provisional duties can be imposed where:
a product is considered to be subsidised if:
it benefits from a direct or indirect subsidy;
it is imported from the country of origin but is exported from an intermediate country; or
it has been exported from the EU from an EU country other than that of origin.
A product is not considered to benefit from a subsidy if it does not:
benefit from a government guarantee, unless there is a difference between the amount that the firm receiving the guarantee pays on a loan guaranteed by the government and on a comparable commercial loan in the absence of the government guarantee.
The amount of a subsidy must be calculated according to the following criteria:
whether the subsidy has been granted specifically or not;
whether it has been made legally contingent upon export performance;
how the subsidy was granted;
if the export performance is in fact tied to actual or anticipated exportation or export earnings.
An investigation may be launched if the facts demonstrate that the grant of a government subsidy, without having been made legal contingent on export performance, is in relation to export earnings or that the granting of such a subsidy was in fact linked to export performance.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Countervailable: a product is deemed to be ‘subsidised’ if it benefits from an ‘unlawful’ or ‘undue’ subsidy.
* Imports: imports of a product which are intended to be used for the production, export or transfer of the product, but which are not intended for that purpose.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 852/2017 of 21 December 2017 on the countervailing policy of the European Union (codified version) (OJ L 183, 29.12.2017, pp. 1-8)
last update 14.01.2019

EU financial assistance for the COVID-19 crisis recovery and resilience plans (European Facility for the Cohesion and Integration of the European Union (Euratom)
EU financial aid for the cohesion and integration of the Eastern Mediterranean region (EUROM)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2094 — EU financial assistance to the European Economic and Social Fund for the response to the crisis in the eastern Mediterranean region
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE FUNDING?
The European Union’s (EU's) Euratom financial assistance facility is designed to support EU countries’ efforts to recover and strengthen their economies and social structures following the crisis caused by the crisis, known as COVID–19.
The EUROM Facility is the main source of EU financial support for EU countries in the wake of the crisis.
It aims to help EU countries to:

KEY POINTS

make the necessary investments to meet the milestones and targets of the plan.

Summary 27:
European Union (EU) rules on rail passengers’ rights (Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007)
European Union rules on passenger rights (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — European Union rules for rail passengers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that rail passengers have the same rights as passengers on other modes of transport in the EU.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EC), which had been in force since 2007.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all rail passengers taking international and domestic journeys. It does not apply to rail services offered strictly for historic or touristic use.
Passengers have the right to:
have a seat on the train;
have their ticket validated;
make and cancel their reservation;
be assisted by a ticket vendor or a station manager.
The rights and obligations regarding carriage of bicycles on trains should apply to bicycles that can be readily ridden prior to and after the rail journey.
EU countries must ensure that passengers with disabilities and persons with reduced mobility have access to information concerning the accessibility of rail services, access conditions of rolling stock and the facilities on board.
In addition, EU countries should ensure that no discrimination occurs during the process of accessing online interfaces or purchasing tickets.
Railway undertakings and station managers must:
ensure that passengers can make and cancel reservations on their own initiative;
establish joint customer services and complaint-handling mechanisms;
take appropriate enforcement measures;
provide a system of compensation for passengers in the case of delay, including in cases where the delay is caused by a cancellation of a service or a missed connection.
A railway undertaking may not be obliged to pay compensation if it can prove that the delay was caused by extraordinary circumstances such as extreme weather conditions or major natural disasters endangering the safe operation of the service.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2018/722 has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Passenger rights’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger: a person with a disability or a reduced mobility who wishes to travel by rail or by air.
REFERENCES
Act

Summary 28:
Pre-accession assistance for the Western Balkans
Pre-Accession aid for the western Balkans
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/947 — preaccession support for the beneficiaries listed in Annex I for the 2021-2027 period
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a dedicated instrument for pre-accessION assistance to the beneficiaries of the EU’s Western Balkans programme.
It aims to support the efforts of the countries listed in the Annex I of the IPA III programme to prepare for their accession to the EU.
KEY POINTS
The beneficiaries covered by the regulation are:
the Western Balkans (Albania, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Croatia, Kosovo, Montenegro, Macedonia, Serbia and Montenegro);
the candidate countries for the accession process (e.g. Albania, Bosnia, Croatia and Kosovo) and their Member States;
the countries that have applied for EU membership.
The objectives of the assistance are to:
promote democracy, human rights and the rule of law;
ensure that the beneficiaries meet the minimum standards of performance indicated by the EU Gender Action Plans;
support the development of a social market economy in line with the key principles and rights set out in the European Pillar of Social Rights solemnly proclaimed and signed on 17 November 2017 by the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission;
develop regional, macro-regional and cross-border cooperation and territorial development, including through the implementation of EU macro-Regional strategies.
Support
The European Commission and the Member States responsible for providing the assistance must ensure that the assistance is consistent with, and complementary to, the general objectives of EU external action as laid down in Article 21 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU), including respect for fundamental rights and principles as well as the protection and promotion of human rights, democracy and the law.
In order to achieve these objectives, the European Commission must:
encourage the participation of the beneficiaries in the decision-making process;
provide incentives and guidance to them to pursue the necessary far-reaching reforms;
encourage the participation and participation of civil society and the private sector in the political dialogue;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice in order to improve the quality of governance and the transparency of the aid provided;
assist the beneficiaries with the necessary financial resources to meet their objectives.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2021 and had to become law in the Member State concerned by 1 January 2022.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The Western Balkans’ on the European External Action Service's website.

Summary 29:
European Union Instrument for Development Cooperation (EUIDC) 2017-2021
European Union Initiative for development cooperation (UIDC 2017-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2017/2021 establishing the European Union Instrument on Development Cooperation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE INITIATION?
   
The decision establishes the EU’s Instrument on development cooperation, which aims to support the sustainable economic, social and environmental development of developing countries.
The objective of the EUIDC is to contribute to the achievement of the 2030 Agenda and the Addis Ababa Action Agenda.
KEY POINTS
The objectives of the instrument are to:
promote sustainable development in developing countries;
contribute to achieving the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs);
support the implementation of the European Neighbourhood Policy (EPC) and the African Union (AUS);
strengthen the capacity of partner countries to respond to crises;
ensure the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms;
encourage the sustainable development of the environment and the sustainable use of natural resources;
support good governance, democracy, the rule of law and human rights;

promot the fight against youth unemployment;
improve the quality of life for people living in the most deprived areas of the world;
provide the necessary financial resources to implement the EPC and the ACP-EU Partnership Agreement;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice in the field of development cooperation; and
support political dialogue and cooperation.
In line with the objectives set out in Article 21(1) and (2) TEU of the Treaty on European Union (TEU), the EUI is funded by the European Development Fund (EDF), the European Social Fund (ESF), and the European Investment Bank (ESB).
The EU is responsible for providing the EU countries with the financial resources they need to achieve the SDGs.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTING DECISION APPLY?

Summary 30:
Whistleblowers’ protection in the EU — common minimum standards for EU acts, policies and regulations
WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
ACT
Directive (EU) 2017/1371 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2017 on the protection of whistleblowers in the European Union (EU).
SUMMARY
EU countries must ensure that whistleblowers are protected when reporting breaches of EU law that are harmful to the public interest.
The directive applies to all EU acts and policies, including those relating to competition, the environment and the financial interests of the EU.
It does not apply to certain EU policies, such as the EU’s common foreign and security policy, which are not covered by the directive.
Scope of the directive
It applies to the following acts and policy areas:
the enforcement of EU competition law, including concerning State aid;
the protection of the environmental acquis, including relating to environmental protection;
public procurement, including the supply of products or the provision of services.
In addition, it applies to breaches of the public procurement rules, which relate to the procurement of defence-related products.
Reporting of breaches by whistleblowers can be key to detecting and preventing, reducing or eliminating risks to public health and to consumer protection resulting from breaches of these EU rules.
EU country authorities must:
ensure that whistleblowers’ reporting is effective and proportionate;
take all necessary measures to prevent, detect and eliminate breaches of those EU rules;
establish a procedure for investigating and prosecuting breaches of such EU rules, including by means of criminal proceedings;
impose sanctions on those who fail to comply with the directive;
use all appropriate investigative and prosecutorial tools to investigate and prosecute breaches of their EU obligations.
National authorities must also:
establish procedures for investigating, prosecuting and punishing those responsible for breaching EU law;
identify the persons responsible for the breach and inform the public and the European Commission of the reasons for their failure to act;
assess the extent to which the breach has been committed and take appropriate action to prevent it from happening again;
investigate and prosecute the perpetrators of the breach;
report to the Commission, the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) or the European Court of Auditors of the Union (ECAD) of any information that has been obtained by a person who has been a victim of a breach of EU or national law.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE ENTER INTO FORCE?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
EU law is one of the most important instruments in the fight against fraud and corruption in the world.
For more information, see:
EU whistleblowers (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Whistleblower: an individual who has the right to expose and prevent breaches of Union law and policies.
* Public procurement: the supply or provision of goods or services by a company or a person acting on behalf of a company.
