Summary 31:
European integrated border management (EIBM)
European integration of the external borders
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — European integrated border-management — European Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s European Integrated Border and Coast Guard (EUBM) to support EU countries in their efforts to manage the crossing of their external borders and to combat serious crime with a cross-border dimension.
KEY POINTS
The EIBM is responsible for the management of operational cooperation at the European external borders of the EU and the Schengen area. It has a mandate to:
support EU countries’ efforts to protect the EU external borders;
provide technical and operational assistance to EU countries to reinforce their capacity to implement their obligations with regard to external border control and to face challenges resulting from an increased number of arrivals of irregular migrants;
ensure the effective and sustainable return of migrants and return of returnees;
support the fight against transnational organised crime.
The regulation sets out the responsibilities of the EUBM and the role of the national coordination centre to coordinate and exchange information among all EU countries with a responsibility for border control at national level.
It also sets up the European border and coast guard standing corps (the ‘standing corps’).
The standing corps will be responsible for:
providing operational support to the EU countries and EU countries on the ground in the event of an increase in the number of unaccompanied minors crossing the external border;
carrying out border surveillance and border checks;
conducting rapid border interventions;
contributing to the creation of a standing corps and technical equipment pool.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDING DIRECTIVE APPLYORK INTO FORCE
The implementing act entered into force on 1 May 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has a comprehensive approach to managing its external borders. The aim is to prevent and combat serious cross border crime, such as migrant smuggling, trafficking in human beings and terrorism, where it is appropriate for it to act and where it has obtained relevant information through its activities.
For more information, see:
European Integrated border management — European Union (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Migrant smuggling: the illegal crossing of the borders of an EU country by a non-EU country to enter the territory of another EU country.
* Cross-border crime: the movement of persons, goods or services across borders by means of illegal means.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council implementing act (EC) No 852/2017 of 14 April 2017 establishing the European Agency of the Management for the European Integration of the External Border and of Operations at the external Borders (EibM) (OJ L 183, 15.5.2017, pp. 1-8)

Summary 32:
Cybersecurity in the EU: strengthening the role of the European Network and Information Assurance Agency (ENISA) (European Cybersecurity Agency) (EnISA)
The European network and information assurance agency is responsible for protecting the European Union (EU) against cyber threats.
ACT
Regulation (EC) No 552/2014/EU of the Council of 26 February 2014 on the strengthening the functions of the EU Network and IT Assurance Association (Enisa)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes the role and responsibilities of ENISA, the European network security agency, to protect the EU’s information and communication technology (ICT) systems against cyberattacks.
It sets out the tasks and responsibilities that ENISA has to fulfil.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out ENISA's tasks, including:
ensuring that cybersecurity is a priority for EU countries’ cybersecurity policies and programmes;
providing advice and expertise on cybersecurity at EU level and at EU country level;
assisting with the development and updating of strategies on the security of networks and information systems at EU-level and, upon request, at EU State level, in particular on cybersecurity;
promoting the dissemination of cybersecurity strategies and following the progress of their implementation.
ENISA also provides technical assistance to EU countries in the event of cybersecurity incidents and crises.
EnISA is responsible, on its own initiative and on behalf of the Commission, for:
coordinating the response of EU countries to cybersecurity incidents;
coordination between EU countries;
informing the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council on cybersecurity issues;
supporting the Commission in the implementation of the Cybersecurity Strategy and the EU Cybersecurity Action Plan;
facilitating the exchange of best practices and best practices on cybersecurity.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 March 2014 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 March 2016.
BACKGROUND

Summary 33:
Gross national product (GNI) and gross national income at market prices — verification of the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of the EU’s gross national product
Gross National Product (GNDP) and Gross national income (GNOI)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — verification and comparability of gross national products (GNP) and GNI at market price
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the procedure for the verification and assessment of the reliability, reliability, exhaustiveness and comparable quality of the Gross National Product* and Gross National Income* data collected by EU countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with an inventory of the sources and methods used to produce GNI aggregates and their components in accordance with ESA 2010.
The Commission must verify the sources, their uses and the methods in the inventory to ensure that GNI data are reliable, exhaustive and comparable.
Each year, EU countries must send the data to Eurostat, which then verifies that the data are comparable across EU countries and that they comply with the relevant definitions and accounting rules of ESA 2010 and with the rules of the European System of Accounts (ESA 2010).
The data must be sent to the Commission by the EU countries at the same time.
National authorities must notify Eurostat of the data they have received and send them to the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC), which is responsible for verifying and assessing the quality of GNI statistics.
Eurostat may request the assistance of experts representing other EU countries’ national accounts.
In addition, Eurostat must notify the Commission of any objections it receives from EU countries to the data it has received.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLACE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union (EU), as a whole, has a system of national accounts (ESA) which aims to provide a common framework for the collection and analysis of national statistics. ESA 2010 is the European system of accounts for the period 2014-2020.
For more information, see:
‘The European system for national accounts’ on Eurostat's website.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of new vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units following the withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the European Union (EU)
Implementing act
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2014 on the application for EU type approval
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedure for manufacturers to obtain new EU type approvals for their vehicles and systems in the event of the withdrawal from the EU of the UK.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies from 30 March 2019.
Manufacturers must apply to the European Commission for type approval from the competent EU authority of the EU country in which they have their vehicle or system of manufacture registered or placed on the EU market.
The manufacturer must submit the application to the competent authority of an EU country that is responsible for granting EU typeapproval for the same type of vehicle, system, components or separate technical unit (e.g. engine, transmission, brakes, exhaust system, etc.).
The competent authority must decide whether to grant type approval on the basis of the test reports already presented for the purposes of obtaining the UK type approval, or on test reports that have not changed.
If the manufacturer does not receive type approval within 2 years of submitting the application, the manufacturer is liable to pay adequate fees, laid down by the new authority, for the time when the EU type of approval takes effect.
When submitting an application pursuant to this regulation, manufacturers must provide all the documentation and information necessary to obtain type approval in accordance with the regulation’s rules, including:
the vehicle type certificate and type certificate;
the type certificate for the vehicle or systems of the type covered by the type certificate,
the technical unit for which the type approval has been granted and
the number of type certificates for the type of components covered by this regulation.
EU type approval is valid for a period of 5 years from the date of entry into force of the regulation or, failing that, 2 years after that notification, i.e. from the day when the regulation ceases to apply to and in the UK, unless the European Council, in agreement with the EU countries, unanimously decides to extend that period.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDINGREDUCATION APPLY?
The European Commission adopted the regulation on 30 March 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Types of vehicles’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Vehicle: any vehicle or component of a vehicle that has been approved in the EU for the purpose of putting on the market, registration, sale or entry into service in another EU country.
* System of a component: any system or component that is intended to be used for the operation of a motor vehicle, including the transmission and brakes, the exhaust system and the brakes.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2014 of 30 February 2014 on the approval of new types of vehicles (OJ L 157, 30.3.2014, pp. 1-18)

Summary 35:
Europol — rules on data protection and personal data protection at Europol (European Police Office (Europol) (European Union).
Europol – rules on personal data Protection and Personal Data Protection
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2009/371/JHA on the rules on the management of Europol
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
It sets out Europol’s data protection rules.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the management and data protection of personal data at the European Police Office, Europol.
It also lays down the principles underpinning Regulation (EC) No 45/2001 on the protection of data.
EU countries, EU bodies, non-EU countries and international organisations must ensure that personal data are only transferred to Europol if necessary for preventing and combating crime that falls within Europol's objectives.
Data protection rules are based on the principles set out in Regulation (EU) No 5/2014 on the use of personal information by EU countries and non-European Union countries.
The management board of the Europol is responsible for Europol and is chaired by the Executive Director of the European Union (EU). The Executive Director is assisted by the European Data Protection and Security Policy Directorate-General (EDPS).
The European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council are represented on the Management Board.
Each EU country must designate at least one liaison officer to work closely with Europol to ensure a continuous and effective exchange of information between the national units and Europol, and each national unit must designate a liaison officer who is attached to the management board.
In addition, EU countries must provide Europol with a copy of bilateral and multilateral exchanges of information with other EU countries on crime falling within its objectives, as well as with other international organisations.
All EU countries are required to ensure that Europol has access to all relevant information, including personal data, to the extent necessary for the performance of its tasks.
Access to EU countries’ data must be limited to information falling within the respective mandates of those EU bodies. Any access to data available by technical means must, by means of technical means, be limited within the EU bodies’ respective mandates, and must not exceed the scope of their respective mandates.
Member States and EU countries have the right to restrict access to personal data provided by Europol for:
the purpose and purpose for which Europol may process the data they provide and to restrict the access rights;
the use of the personal data for the purposes for which the data are provided; and
the protection of the privacy of individuals.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
  * KEY TERMS
* Europol: the European police office responsible for investigating, prosecuting and prosecuting serious crime in the EU.
* EU countries: the law enforcement and judicial authorities responsible for the prevention, investigation and prosecution of serious crime, including terrorism, organised crime, organised fraud, corruption, money laundering, organised drug trafficking and organised crime.

Summary 36:
EU financial assistance to countries affected by the COVID-19 outbreak
EU financial aid to countries involved in the CoVID-2019 outbreak
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 — financial assistance for EU countries experiencing, or is seriously threatened with, a severe economic disturbance caused by the outbreak of the communicable diseases of the Central Mediterranean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions and procedures enabling the European Union (EU) to provide financial assistance in the event of a serious economic disturbance in an EU country.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries which have been affected or are seriously threatened by a severe outbreak of communicable disease of the central Mediterranean (COVID-20).
EU countries may request financial assistance from the EU for the financing of short-time work schemes or similar measures aimed at protecting employees and the self-employed and thus reducing the incidence of unemployment and loss of income, as well as the financing, as an ancillary, of some health-related measures in particular in the workplace.
The assistance is guaranteed by the EU countries and is irrevocable, unconditional and on demand, while additional safeguards are in place to enhance the robustness of the system.
EU loans
The loans are guaranteed by EU countries on behalf of the EU. The loans are provided by the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EBRD).
Liability
The EU guarantees the loans to the requesting EU country on the basis of the following criteria:
the amount of the loans is the sum of the gross gross domestic product (GDP) of the requested EU country;
the guarantee is guaranteed on a case-by-case basis, with the exception of calls on the guarantee by the Commission for additional loans;
in the event that the guarantee is not paid out, the EU country has the right to call on the EIB or the EBRD for an additional amount of loans.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘The EU’s financial instruments’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 21 December 2018 on the financial assistance provided for by the Instrument for the Prevention and Control of the Human Immunodeficiency Virus in the Community (TFEU) (OJ L 183, 28.12.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 37:
Recovery and resolution of central counterparties (CCPs) — common conditions, powers and procedures for the recovery and resolution in the event of a failure of a central counterpartie (CCP) (Regulation (EU) No 648/2012)
Recovery, resolution and recovery plans for Central counterparties
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 — common rules for the resolution of CCPs
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common conditions and procedures to be followed by national authorities and resolution authorities when dealing with the failure of the Central Counterparties* of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
CCPs set out measures to recover from financial distress, to maintain the critical functions of a CCP which is failing or likely to fail while winding up the remaining activities through normal insolvency proceedings, to preserve financial stability and to avoid a significant adverse effect on the financial system and its ability to serve the real economy while minimising the cost to taxpayers of the CCP’s failure.
The recovery plan must include the following elements:
recovery of all CCPs’ assets and liabilities;
the recovery of the remaining CCP assets;
a recovery plan for the CCP and its clearing members.
A recovery plan should be drawn up by the CCP, which must include:
the amount of CCP assets that must be recovered from the CCP by the end of the recovery plan;
recovering the CCP's remaining activities;
all the CCPs' liabilities and the CCP members’ obligations to the CCP.
An EU country must notify the European Commission of its decision to use public funds as a last resort to resolve a CCP failure;
an EU country may decide to use the use of public funds only if it considers that the CCP is insolvent and that the failure would have an adverse impact on the economy of the EU country concerned.
Regulators must also:
ensure that CCPs set up a recovery fund to cover the costs of their recovery;
set up a resolution authority to handle the resolution process of a failing CCP;
make sure that the resolution authority is independent and independent from the national authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2018 has applied since 1 January 2019 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been working towards a common framework for resolving the crisis of the 2008 financial crisis.
For more information, see:
‘CCPs’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 40:
Reused urban waste water for agricultural irrigation
Reused municipal wastewater for agriculture
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — minimum requirements for water reuse
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out minimum requirements to be met by EU countries for the reuse of treated urban wastewater for agricultural purposes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to treated municipal wastewater which has been collected in collecting systems, that has been treated in urban wastewater treatment plants in accordance with Directive 91/271/EEC, and that undergoes further treatment, either in the urban waste-water treatment plant or in a reclamation facility, in order to meet the parameters set out in Annex I to the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that the water is reused whenever appropriate.
The water must be treated in a way that minimises the risk to human health, the environment and the safety of the water bodies used for the abstraction of water intended for human consumption and relevant safeguard zones.
In addition, EU countries must set up a water reuse risk management plan, which must include a description of how the water will be reused and how it will be used.

Summary 41:
EU Blue Card for highly qualified third-country nationals — mobility of highly qualified workers from non-EU countries
European Union (EU) blue card — mobility and business rights of highly-qualified third country nationals
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/36/EU — EU blue card for highly-skilled third-level nationals
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that highly qualified, highly skilled non-European Union citizens who are beneficiaries of international protection (i.e. those who have been granted the right to live and work freely in the EU) have the same rights and benefits as other EU citizens.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU nationals who are highly qualified and who have benefited from international protection.
It does not apply to EU citizens who apply to reside in an EU country as researchers in order to carry out research projects, as they fall within the scope of Directive 2016/801 of the European Parliament and of the Council, which introduced a specific procedure for admitting non-euro area nationals for the purpose of research.
EU countries must ensure that EU blue cards are valid for at least 5 years of professional experience at a level comparable to higher education qualifications and which is relevant to the profession or sector specified in the work contract or binding job offer.
Applicants must meet the following criteria:
have a high level of professional skills, attesting that they have at least 3 years of relevant professional experience in the relevant occupation or profession and that the work is inherently regarded as requiring such skills;
have at least 2 years of work experience in a relevant profession in the employer’s territory.
have completed a minimum of 2 years’ work in the country of residence in which they live or work.
The employer must accept applications for an EU Blue Card on the basis of evidence of higher professional skills attested by at least 4 years of experience in an occupation or sector in which the work to be carried out is regarded as demanding such competence.
Employers must provide for a lower salary threshold for specific professions where the EU country concerned considers that there is a particular shortage of available workers and where such professions belong to major group 1 or 2 of the ISCO classification. The salary threshold may not be lower than the applicable salary threshold but may be higher, in line with market conditions, labour law, collective agreements and practices in the Member State concerned.
An employer may refuse to issue a blue card if the holder has not respected the conditions for mobility within the allowed period for carrying out a business activity, has not submitted an application for long-term mobility or has not used the mobility rights within the required time frame, or has used them in an abusive manner. The employer may also withdraw or refuse to renew the card if a holder has failed to comply with the requirements for mobility under this directive, including in cases of the abusive use of mobility rights, for example by:
using a mobility right that is not respected;
using an abusive mobility right which is not recognised by the employer;
not respecting the conditions of mobility; or
using mobility rights that are not respected by the holder.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
   
For more information, see:
‘EU blue card’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2014/35/EU of 21 December 2014 on the EU blue-card for highly skilled third-line nationals (recast) (OJ L 183, 28.12.2014, pp. 1-32)
last update 14.01.2016



Summary 42:
European Union Asylum and Migration Fund (EUAF) for the period 2014-2020
European Union asylum and migration fund for the year 2014-2022
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — European Union Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Union asylum, migration and integration fund to support EU countries’ efforts to integrate and integrate third-country nationals.
KEY POINTS
The EUAF is the main funding instrument for the EU’s policy on migration and asylum.
It supports EU countries in setting up strategies and strengthening and developing policies for legal migration, and in enhancing their capacity to develop, implement, monitor and evaluate immigration and integration strategies, policies and measures for legally staying non-EU nationals, in line with EU legal instruments.
EU countries are responsible for the return and readmission and sustainable reintegration of returnees, including through the provision of cash or in-kind support.
The fund also supports the efforts of EU countries to enhance their capacities to develop and monitor their asylum policies in the light of their obligations under EU law.
In addition to the return of persons as provided for in this regulation, the EUAF supports other measures to counter irregular migration and the trafficking of migrants and to encourage compliance with legal migration rules, thereby safeguarding the integrity of EU immigration systems.
Activities funded under the fund are based on the principles of gender equality, non-discrimination and the best interests of the child. They must be carried out in accordance with the rights and principles enshrined in the EU acquis and the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the EU.
Funding
The European Union is responsible for:
supporting actions in the field of integration for the early stages of integration, as well as horizontal measures supporting Member States’ capacities in the area of integration;
promoting contact, constructive dialogue and acceptance between EU nationals and the receiving society;
encouraging the exchange of information between EU countries and civil society organisations;
providing financial support for actions that are tailored to the needs of non-European Union nationals;
facilitating the implementation of EU-funded actions carried out by national, regional, local and local authorities, civil society bodies and non-governmental organisations (NGOsOsOs);
support for EU countries with a longer-term impact, such as the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Investment Bank (ESB).
The Fund is funded by the EU budget and is co-financed by the European Commission and the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 14 March 2018.
BACKGROUND
The purpose of the fund is to support the EU and its Member States in their efforts to:
ensure the safe and dignified return of people in need of international protection;
support the integration of third country nationals in the Member States;
contribute to global resettlement initiatives.
For more information, see:
‘Asylum and migration’ on the European External Action Service's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 852/2018 of 13 March 2018 on the establishment of the European Fund for the Integration of Third-Country nationals for the Year 2014-2019 (OJ L 183, 14.3.2018, pp. 1-22)
last update 14.05.2019



Summary 43:
Workers' right to parental leave
Workers’ right to maternal and paternal leave
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/29/EU on parental leave for EU workers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to ensure that EU workers have the right to take parental leave.
It sets out the conditions for this right and the conditions under which it can be exercised.
KEY POINTS
 			
EU countries must ensure that workers who take parental leaves are entitled to a minimum of 5 working days per year.
The duration of parental leave is linked to the age of the child.
EU workers who exercise their right to leave or to request flexible working arrangements can request that parental leave be granted on a full-time or a part-time basis, in alternating periods, such as for a number of consecutive weeks of leave separated by periods of work, or in other flexible ways.
Employers must respect the rights of workers who have taken parental leave and must inform them of any dismissal or suspension of their employment contract or employment relationship.
National authorities must also ensure that employers respect the right of workers to take leave and that they have the necessary financial resources to pay for it.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
  —
Parental leave is one of the main pillars of gender equality in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Parental leaves’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 44:
Fraud affecting the EU's financial interests
Frauding the EU’s financial interests			
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/34/EU on the protection of the European Union's Financial Interests
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ criminal laws and administrative sanctions are applied in the event of fraud* affecting the financial interests of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
fraud with respect to revenues, expenditure and assets at the expense of the general budget of the Union (the ‘EU budget’), including financial operations such as borrowing and lending activities.
It does not apply to the following types of fraud:
passive corruption and misappropriation of funds or assets, contrary to the intended purpose and whereby the EU financial interests are damaged.
Defraud against the common VAT system, as defined in Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 883/2013.
EU countries must:
ensure that the Court of Auditors and the auditors responsible for auditing the budgets of EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies disclose to the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and to other competent authorities any fact which could be qualified as a criminal offence under this directive;
require that national audit bodies within the meaning of Article 59 of the regulation do the same, in accordance with Article 8 of the directive.
Impose sanctions
The EU countries must ensure that criminal sanctions are applicable to all serious cases of criminal offences against the Union's financial interest. They must also ensure that the sanctions are proportionate to the damage or advantage caused by the fraud.
The maximum amount of the sanctions is set at €10 million. The maximum period of imprisonment is at least 5 years from the time when the criminal offence was committed.
National authorities must ensure the processing of personal data by the competent national authorities, and the exchange of such data between EU countries on the one hand, and between competent EU bodies on the other.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Fraud’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 		  	
Council Directive 2014/35/EU of 21 December 2014 on the Protection of the Economic and Social Rights of the Member States and of the Community of European Union and on the application of the provisions of Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union (OJ L 183, 1.1.2015, pp. 1-8)

Summary 45:
Interchange fees for card-based payments and internet and mobile payments — harmonisation of EU rules on interchange fees for debit card and credit card transactions (EU-EU directive on card-by-card payments)
INTERFERENCE FRAMEWORK — harmonization of EU-EU rules on card interchange fees
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — harmonising the interchange fees applicable to debit card transactions in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the EU’s payment card interchange fee rules to ensure that the fees charged by payment card issuers and acquirers are set at an economically efficient level.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all payment card schemes in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the minimum interchange fees that issuers must charge to merchants for the use of their payment card systems.
It does not apply to:
debit card transactions;
card-based payment transactions that are carried out on a cross-border basis;
cross-border payment services provided by payment service providers.
The interchange fees must be set at the same level as those charged by the card issuer and the acquirer, irrespective of whether the payment card scheme is an issuer, an acquirer or a third party.
In addition, the regulation sets out a list of payment card companies that must be subject to the same business rules. These include:
the minimum amount of interchange fees to be charged by issuers for each payment card transaction;
the maximum amount of the interchange fee to be paid by cardholders for each debit card transaction.
EU countries must ensure that payment card fees are calculated on the basis of the volume and value of the transactions between issuers or acquirers;
payment card schemes must be regulated by national competent authorities to ensure compliance with the regulation;
each EU country must collect information regarding the volume of all debit card payments within a payment card or of the debit card activities pertaining to one or more payment card transactions pertaining to a payment service provider.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has been working towards a single European payment card market for more than a decade.
For more information, see:
‘Interchange fee harmonisation’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: a fee charged by an issuer or acquirer to a merchant when a card is used to make a payment. It is calculated on a percentage of the cost of the customer's use of a card.
ACT
 
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2019 of 12 July 2019 on the harmonisation for the first time of interchange charges applicable to the domestic debit card markets in the Union (OJ L 183, 14.7.2019, pp. 1-24)

Summary 46:
European framework to support the transparency and understanding of skills and qualifications acquired in formal, non-formal and informal settings, including through practical experiences, mobility and volunteering (Europass)
European framework for the transposition of knowledge and skills
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/852 — European framework to provide tools for the documentation of skills
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a European framework that aims to help individuals and employers to better communicate and present their skills and to compare qualifications.
KEY POINTS
The European framework aims to improve the visibility and relevance of skills in the context of education and training, as well as for work-related purposes.
Europass is a web-based tool set that provides tools for users to document and describe their skills acquired through working and learning, including by volunteering. It also aims to provide information on validation opportunities and guidance to help users to make decisions on employment and learning opportunities.
The decision sets up a European advisory group to advise on Europass’s development. It is composed of representatives from the European Commission, the European Agency for Fundamental Rights and the Council of the European Union (EU).
The advisory group is responsible for:
developing Europass tools;
advising on the development of Europass online platform;
assessing and describing personal information in a variety of formats, including curriculum vitae (CV) templates;
self-assessing of skills;
documenting and describing skills acquired by working or volunteering;
providing guidance on how to use Europass.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLACE IN?

BACKGROUND
The New Skills Agenda for Europe, adopted by the Commission on 10 June 2016, invites Member States, social partners, industry and other stakeholders to work together on ten actions to improve skills formation, to make skills more visible and comparable and to provide skills intelligence and especially information for better career choices.

Summary 48:
Statistics on the transport of goods and passengers by rail (Regulation (EU) 2017/852)
Statistics on rail transport (Regulations)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish common rules for the production of rail transport statistics at EU level.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 91/2003.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all modes of transport in the European Union (EU), except for light rail and tramways.
EU countries may exclude from the scope of the regulation:
railway undertakings which operate entirely or mainly within industrial and similar installations, including harbours;
railways which mainly provide local tourist services, such as preserved historical steam railways.
National transport is defined as rail transport between two places (a place of loading/embarkation and a place of unloading/disembarking) located in the reporting country.
Transport operations involving loading or unloading of goods/passengers at the border of the reporting EU country from/onto another mode of transport are not considered as transit.
The data collected by EU countries must be sent to the European Commission (Eurostat) for analysis and dissemination by the end of the following year.
Annexes I to V to the regulation specify the methods to be used in producing the statistics and the types of data to be collected.
Each EU country must provide Eurostat with information on the methods it uses to produce the statistics.
Eurostat must send the data to Eurostat within 5 years from the date of transmission of the data on rail passengers. The data must be transmitted by the EU country in which the data are collected. The deadline for sending the data is 5 years after the date when the data were received.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts on the basis of Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Rail transport statistics’ on Eurostat’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger: any person, excluding members of the train crew, who makes a trip by rail.
Train: a locomotive travelling on its own, is not considered to be a train;
train-km: the unit of measure representing the movement of a train over one kilometre.
Passenger-kilometre: the number of passengers travelling by rail in the course of combined transport operations, i.e. the total number of persons on board a train in a given day.
Light rail: light rail is a type of light rail system that is not separated from road traffic, whereas light rail may be separated from other systems;
Trams: tramways are generally not separated in the same way as road traffic;
light rail is separated in a similar way as light rail;
trams are separated in some cases from other forms of transport;
national transport: national transport is national transport between 2 places located in one EU country and the other located in another EU country;
transport of goods: goods are transported by rail from one place to another in a specified EU country, including by rail, by road, by rail or by air.
Railway passenger: a person, including members of passengers, who is a passenger on a train.

Summary 49:
Harmonisation of the visa requirements for non-EU nationals
Harmonomisation of visas for non EU nationals
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 539/2001 on the common policy on visas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the requirements for EU nationals who are subject to a requirement to be in possession of a visa for the crossing of EU countries’ external borders (also referred to herein as ‘the visa requirement’) and those whose nationals are exempt from that requirement.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to nationals of the EU countries of the Schengen area (non-EU countries) who do not have to carry a visa when crossing their external borders. It does not apply to EU citizens who are resident in non-Schengen countries.
EU countries may exempt certain categories of persons from the visa requirement or impose it on them in accordance with public international law or custom.
Exemptions
The list of countries on the exemption list is updated by the European Commission on a case-by-case basis.
The exemption is valid for 90 days in any 180-day period, notwithstanding the possibility of having country-specific amendments to those annexes in particular circumstances, for instance as a result of a Visa liberalisation process or as the ultimate consequence of a temporary suspension of the exemption from the exemption.
Visa-free nationals may be refused entry to the EU if:
the EU country of residence decides to make the nationals of one or more EU countries subject to an EU visa requirement; or
the list in Annex II decides that the EU country in which the exemption is granted is no longer appropriate; or does not meet the criteria for granting the exemption; or if the exemption has been granted by an EU country that is not an EU member.
Annex II also contains a list of third countries whose nationals do not require a visa to cross the EU’s external borders and those countries which do not impose a visa on their nationals when they do so.
National authorities must ensure that:
visas issued by EU countries are valid for a maximum of 90 days;
the third countries listed in Annex I do not apply a visa requirement for their nationals; and
the persons on the list do not hold a valid travel document.
If the third country in the list does not fulfil the conditions for granting a visa exemption, EU countries must inform the other EU countries and the Commission of the measures they have taken to ensure that the exemption continues to be granted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Visas: a visa issued by a European Union (EU) country for the purpose of allowing its nationals to cross its external borders without having to carry one.
* Non-EU country: a third country that does not require the holder of a travel document to enter its territory.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 50:
Eurojust — rules for the protection and free movement of operational personal data by Eurojust (European Union Agency for the Protection of the Financial Interest of the European Union) (Eurojust) (European External Action Service).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 – rules for Eurojust
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out Eurojust’s rules of procedure.
It aims to ensure that Eurojust is independent of national authorities in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Eurojust is a European Union agency responsible for preventing and combating serious financial crimes affecting the financial interests of the EU, including fraud, money laundering and terrorist financing.
Its main tasks are to:
ensure the protection of the personal data of EU citizens;
support and coordinate cross-border investigations;
facilitate the exchange of information between Eurojust and EU countries’ competent authorities.
The regulation lays down rules on:
the protection of operational data;
the free movement and the processing of data; and
the admissibility of personal data as evidence in criminal pre-trial and court proceedings.
EU citizens have the right to have their personal data processed only in accordance with the law of their EU country and in accordance, where appropriate, with the laws of the country in which they reside. They also have a right to be informed of any changes to their data that might affect their rights and to have access to the data they have been provided with.
In addition, Eurojust must ensure that:
data subjects have the legal right to access the operational data of Eurojust in the event of a breach of their data protection rights;
data protection measures are in place to prevent, detect and prevent the transfer of their operational data to a third country or to an international organisation for the purpose of preventing or combating such offences;
EU countries have to inform Eurojust of any breaches of data protection measures that they have taken and of the measures they are taking to prevent or prevent such breaches.
National authorities have to ensure Eurojust has the necessary operational powers with respect to their Member State and that they comply with the rules of this regulation in order to cooperate between themselves and with national authorities. Eurojust does not have the operational powers to carry out specific operations with regard to processing of operational operational data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Operational personal data: data that is used for the prevention, detection, investigation or prosecution of a criminal offence.
* Data protection measures: measures that are designed to protect the data of persons who have been the subject of a data protection request or have been a subject of an operational data request or a request for a processing request.

Summary 51:
Fighting money laundering by means of criminal law (Directive 2017/1371)
Fighting money trafficking by means OF CERTAIN ACTS
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/2371 — combating money laundering through criminal law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to make it easier for EU countries to combat money laundering* by criminal law.
It sets out rules for the freezing and confiscation of instrumentalities and proceeds of crime.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all types of money laundering. It does not apply to money laundering as regards property derived from criminal offences affecting the EU’s financial interests, which is subject to specific rules laid down in Directive 2014/42/EU (see summary).
EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that the conduct referred to in the directive is committed by the perpetrator of the criminal activity that generated the property (‘self-laundering’).
The definition of criminal activities which constitute predicate offences for money laundering should be sufficiently uniform in all EU countries.
The offences covered by the directive are:
money laundering by a legal person or legal entity within the meaning of Article 2(2) of the directive;
tax crimes relating to direct and indirect taxes;
extortion;
forgery;
piracy;
insider trading and market manipulation;
cybercrime, including any offence set out in Directive 2013/40/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council.
Penalties for the offences listed above are based on a number of factors, including:
the nature and seriousness of the offence;
the amount of the property and the nature and severity of the offences under investigation and the right to the protection of personal data.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that criminal offences are punishable by a maximum sentence of at least 5 years’ imprisonment. The maximum sentence may be increased if the offender has a prior or simultaneous conviction for that criminal activity, or if there is evidence that the offender knew that the property was the result of a criminal activity.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 52:
Recovery plan for bluefin tuna — EU countries’ annual fishing plans for 2021-2022 (European Commission Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Recovery Plan for bluein tuna — European Commission Regulation(EU)2019/853
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2017 — EU fisheries recovery plan
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s annual fishing plan for 2021–2022 to help the EU achieve a sustainable yield of tuna by 2022.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must submit their fishing plans to the European Commission by 31 December each year. These must include the total number of vessels and traps that may fish for, retain on board, tranship, transport, or land bluefin fish.
Each EU country must also submit a list of the vessels and equipment that may be used for fishing for, retaining on board and transhipment of live tuna.
The fishing plan must contain information on:
the number of fishing vessels registered in an EU country and of the fishing vessels flying the flag of a EU country that can fish for tuna;
the quotas allocated to each gear group referred to in Articles 11 and 12 of the regulation, including information on the vessel list, and
the individual quota allocated to vessels over 24 metres.
An EU country submitting its fishing plans must submit its fishing plan no later than 30 days before the start of its fishing season applicable to each such vessel.
A fishing plan may be modified at any time by the EU country with a quota of more than 10% of the total fishing capacity for the fishing season for that year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a party to the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT) which aims to restore the stocks of tuna in the Atlantic Ocean.

Summary 53:
Shipbuilding injurious pricing — EU action against sales of ships below their normal value which cause injury to the EU industry (IPI Code)
Shipbuilding injurious pricing – EU action to protect the European Union industry
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/755 on injuriously priced vessels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the protection of the EU’s shipbuilding industry against the sale of vessels below the normal value.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 182/2011, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2016.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to vessels from non-market-economy countries and, in particular, those to which the EU is a contracting party to the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) Shipbuilding Agreement (SAA).
It does not apply to:
ships built in non-EU countries;
ships imported from outside the EU; or
ships that are imported into the EU on the basis of a contract concluded under the SAA.
EU action
EU countries must take action against any vessel whose sale at less than normal value causes injury to their industry.
The EU may also take legal action against the seller of the vessel if:
the sale price is below its normal value;
the vessel is in the ordinary course of trade;
it is being sold to a buyer of the exporting country.
the buyer is a person or company that is legally or operationally in a position to exercise restraint or direction over the buyer.

Summary 54:
EU rules on cableway installations and cable-operated safety components (Cableway Directive)
EU rights and obligations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 765/2008 on the EU rules on the sale of subsystems and safety components on the European Union market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ national authorities have the same rules for the authorisation, inspection and monitoring of cableways and their safety components.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all cableways, whether vertical or inclined, permanently serving specific levels of buildings and constructions and not operating between cableway stations. It does not apply to cable-operating ferries.
It does not affect the right of EU countries to specify adequate procedures for authorisation of planned cableways installations, the inspection of planned installations prior to their entry into service and their monitoring during operation.
EU countries must ensure that:
substances covered by the regulation bear a CE marking indicating that they comply with the regulation’s requirements and are not liable to endanger the health or safety of persons or property when properly installed, maintained and operated in accordance with their intended purpose;
the CE marking is the visible consequence of a whole process comprising conformity assessment in a broad sense.
The CE marking, indicating the conformity of a subsystem or a safety component, must be visible in a single EU declaration of conformity. It must be accompanied by a certificate of conformity and be available for inspection by the competent national authorities and notified bodies.
manufacturers must ensure the CE marking and documentation drawn up by manufacturers are available to inspection by national authorities.
Importers must ensure their products are only available on the market if they meet the requirements of the regulation.
Manufacturers are responsible for ensuring that their products comply with this regulation and that they have supplied comply with its requirements. They must also ensure that they only make available on their own name or trademark any subsystem or safety component which is in conformity with the regulations.
National authorities must notify notified bodies of any changes to a cableway installation that could affect the safety of the installations or the health and safety of people or property.
Notification of defects
The notification of defects must be carried out by a competent national authority and must include the following information:
the type of defect;
a description of the defect and the reasons for its removal;
how the defect was removed or replaced;
where the defect or replacement has been identified, the manufacturer must inform the competent authority of the reason for the removal or replacement of the component or subsystem;
whether the defect has been replaced or replaced by a new component.
If a notified body considers that a product does not conform with the legislation, it must notify the EU country in which it is located and inform the European Commission of the reasons why.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directive 2008/65/EC has applied since 1 July 2008 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
The cableway directive is part of the ‘new approach’ principles, as set out in the Council Resolution of 7 May 1985 on a new approach to technical harmonisation and standards.
For more information, see:
‘Cableways’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cableway installations: installations designed to provide access to and use cableways for the transport of goods, such as cables, cables and cables of various types.
* Safety components: components used in cableways that are designed and constructed to provide the necessary safety and environmental protection for the operation of the cableway.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratom and the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 July 2008 on the Union rules on subsystems on the sales of subsystem and safety parts on the Community market (Codified version) (OJ L 183, 29.7.2008, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services in the digital single market for persons with disabilities (Directive 2018/22/EU)
Accessibility requirements in the online single market
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/23/EU on the accessibility of products, services and assistive technologies for people with disabilities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that all EU citizens, regardless of their abilities, can enjoy the benefits of accessibility* of goods and services.
It sets out a number of accessibility requirements for the online market, including:
products and services;
access to audiovisual media;
services for end-users with disabilities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
all products or services offered in the EU, including e-commerce services, that are intended to be used for the provision of those services with which the consumer interacts.
Products and services covered by the directive must comply with the following requirements:
the accessibility of the product or service must be equivalent to that enjoyed by other end users;
the product must be free of charge for persons who have long-term physical, mental, intellectual or sensory impairments which in interaction with various barriers may hinder their full and effective participation in society on an equal basis with others’.
The accessibility requirements must be met by:
manufacturers and distributors of the products and service covered by this directive;
operators of the services falling within the scope of this directive.
EU countries must ensure that the accessibility requirements are met in all EU countries by 31 December 2022.
Scope of the directive
The legislation applies to the following services:
air, bus, rail and waterborne passenger transport services, including real-time travel information through websites, mobile device-based services, interactive information screens and interactive self-service terminals, required by passengers with disabilities in order to travel.
Payment terminals, including both their hardware and software;
ticketing machines; ticketing machines issuing physical tickets granting access to services such as travel ticket dispensers; bank office queuing ticket machines; check-in machines.
E-commerce service accessibility obligations must apply to the online sale of any product and service and should therefore also apply to sale of a product or services covered in its own right under the directive. The directive does not apply to services offered within the territory of the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The new directive entered into force on 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
Accessible products are a basic precondition for participating in society, and must be accessible to all.
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Council Directive (EU, 2018/24/EU of 25 November 2018 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the access of persons to goods and to services and repealing Council Directive (EEC) No 552/2004 (OJ L 183, 1.12.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 57:
EU customs and trade: common rules and procedures for trade in goods between the EU and non-EU countries and territories
Uruguay and the European Economic Area (EEA)
The European Union (EU) has adopted a customs code to simplify customs legislation and modernise its approach to customs control.
ACT
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU of 26 February 2014 on the common rules of the EU customs and trading area and on the harmonisation of customs controls between the Member States and the EEA.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THE DECISION DO?
   
It lays down the rules and general principles for the EU’s customs code.
It sets out the general rules for the application of the customs legislation to trade in EU goods between EU countries or territories outside the EU.
KEY POINTS
The EU customs code is designed to:
simplify customs formalities;
ensure uniform application of customs legislation;
promote the use of modern tools and technology;
improve the efficiency of customs control procedures;
facilitate the exchange of information between customs authorities and the business community;
provide for the proper levels of control of goods brought into or taken out of the Customs territory of the Union.
EU countries must ensure that the information provided by economic operators is shared, taking account of the relevant data-protection provisions, between customs officials and with other agencies involved in that control. It must also be shared with the European Commission.
Customs controls must be based on a common risk management framework and an electronic system for its implementation.
common risk criteria and standards for the control of imports, exports and exports.
Implementing measures must be in place to minimise the risk to the EU, its citizens and its trading partners.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 March 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  EU countries have to ensure that their customs legislation is consistent with the EU rules on trade and trade.
For more information, see:
EU Customs and trade (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Customs control: the formalities and procedures that are applied to the import, export and transit of goods by the customs authorities of the Member State concerned.
* Trade in goods: the process by which goods are imported, exported or transited through the customs territory of an EU country.

Summary 58:
Ensuring the enforcement of intellectual property rights in the EU by means of small consignments
Ensuing the enforceability of intellectual properties in the context of customs supervision and control
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — small consignment destruction procedure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ customs authorities are able to enforce intellectual property (IP) rights by destroying goods that are suspected of infringing EU laws.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to goods that:
have been manufactured by a person duly authorised by a right-holder to manufacture a certain quantity of goods, in excess of the quantities agreed between that person and the rightholder;
are manufactured as genuine goods and are therefore not liable to customs supervision or control;
have not been placed on the market for the first time in the European Economic Area (EEA) without the authorisation of the right-holders.
It does not apply to:
goods that have been manufactured in a non-EU country and placed on a European Union market without his or her authorisation;
materials that are manufactured in an EU country but placed on an EU market without their authorisation.
Implementation of the regulation
Customs authorities must carry out adequate customs controls to prevent the diversion of infringing goods onto the market of the EU.
The procedure is compulsory with regard to all infringements of IP rights and should be applied, where the declarant or the holder of the goods agrees to destruction.
EU countries may refuse to apply the procedure if they consider that the goods do not comply with EU law or that they would be liable under the legislation of the country where the goods were found.
In the event of a refusal to destroy the goods, the EU country in which the goods are to be destroyed must inform the customs authorities of the reason for the refusal and inform them of the reasons for the decision. The EU country which is responsible for enforcing the rights of the infringer must also inform the EU countries in which it is to be enforced.
A standard form must be provided to customs authorities when they receive a request for destruction of a consignment. The form must contain information such as:
the name and contact details of the person who authorised the manufacture of the consignment;
the date and place of entry into force of the decision and the date of destruction;
details of the date and time when the goods will be destroyed;
where the goods have been moved, under customs supervision, between different places within the EU’s customs territory, they must be moved under the supervision of the customs authority of that EU country.
An advisory procedure must be established for the adoption of the standard forms.
If a customs authority considers that the consignment does not conform with the regulation, it must take appropriate action.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Small consignment: a small amount of goods that can be moved by a single individual or group of persons within the customs territory of the European Union (EU).
* Importers: persons or entities who have a legitimate interest in the export or import of goods.
* Large consignment of goods: a large number of goods which can be transported within the territory of a single EU country for a limited period of time.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  –   –
Commission Regulation (EEC) No 1752/2017 of 12 January 2017 on small consigned destruction procedure (OJ L 183, 1.2.2017, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) 17 52/2017 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 59:
Customs 2020 programme — EU-wide customs cooperation
Customs2020 programme — Union-wide Customs cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/849 on the Customs 2020 programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets up a customs programme for the EU to support the EU’s customs union and the customs authorities working together and acting as one.
KEY POINTS
The programme aims to provide a framework for actions that aim to:
contribute to protecting the financial and economic interests of the EU and its Member States;
ensure the security of the Union and its citizens;
promote the harmonisation of customs procedures and procedures;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice in the field of customs and border control;
improve the efficiency and effectiveness of customs control systems and procedures.
The regulation sets out the rules for the operation of the programme and the financial framework for the programme, as well as the conditions for the participation of EU countries in the programme.
Implementation of the Customs programme is governed by a multiannual work programme, which runs from 2021 to 2023. The work programme is funded by the EU budget and is managed by the European Commission. It is funded through grants, procurement, prizes, indirect implementation, and checks on the responsibility of financial actors.
EU countries are responsible for implementing the programme on their own and for the following reasons:
to support the customs union, the EU countries’ customs authorities and the European Anti-related activities;
to contribute to the protection of the financial interests of EU citizens’ financial interests; and
to protect the EU financial interests.
To achieve these objectives, EU countries must cooperate with the Commission and the EU anti-fraud office (OLAF).
The EU countries are required to cooperate with OLAF, the Court of Auditors, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EPEU) in order to prevent, detect and combat fraud, corruption and any other illegal activity affecting the EU's financial interests, and to ensure the security and security of EU nationals.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION AND ENTRY INTO FORCE?
The implementation of the regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
EU customs cooperation is a dynamic policy area facing new challenges, such as globalisation, new patterns in fraud and smuggling, and digitalisation. These challenges increase the demand for support to customs authorities, and call for innovative solutions.
For more information, see:
EU Customs 2020 (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Customs programme: a new programme designed to support EU countries and the Commission in their customs cooperation with each other.
* EU country: an EU country that participates in a customs cooperation framework with a non-EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation of 1 January 2019 on the customs programme (‘Customs programme’) (OJ L 183, 1.2.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 60:
Equal opportunities and access to the labour market: the European Pillar of Social Rights and the fight against social exclusion, poverty and discrimination (ESF+)
Equity and Access to the Labour Market: The European Pillar and the Fight against Social exclusion, Poverty and Discrimination (EFS+)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2019/852 — ESF+
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE ESF?
The decision sets out the ESF’s objectives and aims.
The ESF is the European Union (EU's) main social policy instrument. It aims to support EU countries in their efforts to achieve the objectives of the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development (SDGs).
It aims to:
promote equal opportunities and equal access to quality employment;
improve access to and quality of education and training to aid reintegration into education systems, promote social inclusion, facilitate access to healthcare for vulnerable persons and contribute to eradicating poverty;
contribute to competitiveness, including through graduate tracking, and to societal and economic innovation by supporting sustainable initiatives that are capable of being scaled up in those fields and adapted to different target groups such as persons with disabilities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The European Pillar is the cornerstone of the EU's social policy. It is the main instrument for the implementation of the SDGs. It provides the EU with the financial resources to implement its social policy objectives.
It is funded by the EU budget and is managed by the European Stability Mechanism (ESM).
Elements of the Pillar
The Pillar is structured in three categories:
equal opportunities and the right to work;
fair working conditions;
social protection and inclusion.
In order to achieve these objectives, the Pillar supports EU countries by:
providing targeted support to learners in need and reduce educational inequalities, including the digital divide;
preventing and reducing early school leaving;
fostering links with non-formal and informal learning;
facilitating lifelong learning and employability;
encouraging career transitions;
supporting people with disabilities;
promoting social inclusion;
helping vulnerable people to return to work or to stay in their country of origin;
improving the effectiveness of the labour markets;
ensuring the protection of the environment;
protecting human rights and fundamental freedoms;
enhancing the quality of life of the most vulnerable people;
raising awareness of the impact of social exclusion and poverty on children and young people.
Programmes
EU countries are responsible for implementing the Pillar. They must:
ensure that the Pillar is implemented in line with the guidelines of the European Parliament and of the Council;
take the necessary measures to implement the Pillar;
develop and implement national strategies to achieve its objectives;
coordinate the Pillar with the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) and with other relevant bodies.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Council adopted the decision on 20 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle poverty and social exclusion in recent years. These include:
the European Pillar for Social Rights (EPSR), which aims to reduce social exclusion by providing equal opportunities for all people in the EU;
the ESF, which is designed to help people who are at risk of falling into poverty.
For more information, see:
‘Equal Opportunities and Equal Opportunities’ on the European Council's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) 2019-852 of 19 June 2019 on the Employment and Social Fund (EESF) (OJ L 157, 20.6.2019, pp. 1-8)
last update 20.02.2021



Summary 61:
European Solidarity Corps (ESC)
European solidarity corps (ESCs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — European Solidarity Corp
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to provide young people with more and better opportunities for solidarity activities covering a broad range of areas, to support national, regional and local actors in their efforts to cope with different challenges and crises.
KEY POINTS
The ESC aims to encourage young people to participate in solidarity activities in order to contribute to the common good by:
supporting the efforts of national and regional organisations to respond to crises and disasters;
raising awareness of the importance of humanitarian aid;
providing support for the development of local networks and communities.
The ESC is funded by the European Structural and Investment Funds and the EU programmes relating to migration, security, justice and citizenship, health and culture.
It is open to young people from all EU countries, regardless of their nationality, age, gender, ethnic origin, language, religion, sexual orientation or political affiliation.
EU countries must ensure that participating organisations comply with the principles and requirements of the ESC and that they respect participants’ rights and responsibilities during all stages of the solidarity experience.
Organisations must:
provide a high level of support to participants before, during and after the solidarity activity;
ensure that participants are treated fairly and respect the rights and obligations of EU citizens;
assist in organising the solidarity activities;
encourage the participation of non-EU citizens in the organisation of solidarity activities.
support the activities of young people by providing them with information on their rights, responsibilities and opportunities to participate.
Participating organisations are responsible for:
organising solidarity activities for young people;
preparing participants before and during their solidarity activities, including training participants and guiding them to local organisations;
coordinating the activities and providing support for participants after their solidarity experience;
promoting the values and principles of solidarity in the context of the EU’s common foreign and security policy.
Eligibility for participation
Participants must be 18 years of age or older and must have a valid residence permit or residence visa. They must be accompanied by a suitable for their age and gender by the time they leave the ECC.
A quality label is provided for each ECC activity to ensure that the organisation is complying with the rules and obligations set out in the regulation. The ECC is open for participation by young people of all ages, irrespective of their nationalities, gender or sexual orientation.
Registration and registration procedures
Participation procedures are based on the European Schengen Agreement (Schengen rules) and the European Anti-Fraud Office (EAF).
The European Commission has set up a single entry point for ECC activities throughout the EU and beyond. The portal is accessible to all interested individuals and organisations.
In addition, the ELC provides information on:
the ECC and the EACs;
participation criteria;
the conditions for entry and residence permits and residence visas;
a list of ECCs and EAC countries;
information on EAC activities and the conditions for renewal of residence permits;
contact details for EAC members and their families.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
ECC is one of a number of initiatives designed to support young people in the fight against social exclusion and promote solidarity with victims of crises and natural disasters in the world.
For more information, see:
‘ECC’ on the EEC's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018 / 852 of 7 December 2018 on the introduction of the European Solidity Corps (OJ L 183, 9.12.2018, pp. 1-37)

Summary 62:
European Globalisation Adjustment Fund for Displaced Workers (EGF) (2018-2027)
European Generalisation and Labour Market Regulation (EGMRA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/2027 on the European Globalisation adjustment fund for displaced workers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the EGF, a flexible and special instrument designed to help displaced workers who lose their jobs in large-scale restructuring events and to help them to find other jobs as quickly as possible.
KEY POINTS
The EGF is funded by the EU’s multiannual financial framework (MFF) and is designed to support displaced workers in the event of major restructuring events.
EU countries are responsible for providing financial support to EU countries that have a significant impact on the local or regional economy.
Beneficiaries are selected by EU countries on the basis of the following criteria:
the number of displaced workers;
the reasons for the redundancies where relevant and anticipate future labour market perspectives and required skills.
Applicants must be able to prove that they have been unemployed for at least 6 months;
they have a valid employment contract with an employer in the EU country in which they live or work.
An application for financial support is triggered when:
a major restructuring event takes place in an EU country with a population of more than 500,000;
a significant restructuring event has a significant economic impact in the area in which the displaced worker lives or works.
The EU country that receives the financial support must:
ensure that the beneficiaries have access to all relevant information and services;
provide a list of eligible beneficiaries in each EU country;
implement a coordinated package of personalised services aiming to facilitate the reintegration of the targeted beneficiaries;
promote the objectives of the Digital Agenda and the Digital Single Market Strategy.
Each year, EU countries must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) on the implementation of the regulation. The EEC is responsible for monitoring the application process and for evaluating the results.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Displaced workers’ (European Commission).
