Summary 63:
Access to the EU’s transparency register for interest representation
Accession of the European Union (EU) to the Transparency Register for Interest Representation
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the accession of interest representatives to the transparency register
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve the transparency of EU policy-making and policy implementation by allowing interested persons to express their views on decisions that may affect them.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
Activities carried out by interest representatives with the objective of influencing the formulation or implementation of policy or legislation, or the decision-making processes of the signatory institutions or other EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies (together referred to as ‘EU institutions’) are covered by the agreement.
Applicants for registration in the register must meet the following conditions.
They must be:
natural persons acting in a strictly personal capacity and not in association with others;
spontaneous meetings, meetings of a purely private or social character and meetings taking place in the context of an administrative procedure established by the TEU or TFEU or legal acts of the Union.
Requests for information must be made in writing and must be accompanied by supporting material demonstrating that the information relating to their registration continues to be accurate;
cooperate sincerely and constructively in accordance with the procedures set out in Annex III.
Registration is voluntary and open to all interested persons.
Applications for registration must be submitted by:
a person acting on their own initiative;
a non-EU national or subnational level engaging in interest representation activities;
an EU institution, body, office or agency representing the interests of the public or the private sector.
Annex IV sets out a code of conduct for interest representatives, which must be observed by all applicants and registrants.
Participation in the EU transparency register is conditional upon:
compliance with the conditions laid down in Annex I of the agreement;
participation in certain activities that are conditional upon registration;
respect for the confidentiality of the information that is provided by the registrants;
ensuring that the register remains up-to-date with regard to the activities covered by this agreement; and
respecting the principles of openness, confidentiality and integrity of the register.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The EU accession agreement entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Parliament and the Council are the EU institutions responsible for the implementation of EU policies and programmes.
For more information, see:
EU transparency register (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interest representation: activities undertaken by persons who have a legitimate interest in the formulation, implementation or interpretation of EU legislation, programmes or policies.
ACT
Council Decision (CFSP) 2018/852 of 20 December 2018 on the conclusion of the Agreement between the Council and the representatives of the Member States on the Accession of Interest Representations (OJ L 183, 21.12.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 64:
Financial framework 2021-2027 agreement between the EU institutions and the European Commission (CFSP) and the EU Fisheries Commission
Framework Agreement between the European Union Institutions and the Union Fisheries Commission (FSP)
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on the financial management of the multiannual financial framework 2021–2027
Decision (EU) 2021/2027 on the conclusion of the Agreement on the Financial Management of the Multiannual Financial Framework 2021–2020
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve budgetary discipline, to improve the functioning of the annual budgetary procedure and cooperation between the Institutions on budgetary matters as well as to ensure sound financial management.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The financial framework is the EU’s main financial instrument for the period 2021-2020. It covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027. The agreement is binding on the institutions for as long as it is in force.
Under the agreement, the institutions are responsible for:
operating the financial framework;
maintaining budgetary discipline;
ensuring that sufficient margins are left available for the various headings of the MFF, except for the sub-heading ‘Economic, social and territorial cohesion’;
establishing a roadmap towards the introduction of new own resources that are sufficient to cover the repayment of the EURI recovery instrument established under Council Regulation (EURI) 2020/2094 (the European Union Recovery Instrument);
introducing the EU fisheries agreement (EURI);
the EU Fisheries Fund (EU Fisheries Fund);
implementing the European Solidarity and Emergency Aid Reserve (EUFSARIF);
providing the European Parliament and the Council with the necessary information on the causes of the situation and on measures which might be adopted under established procedures.
Each year, the Commission must present to the EU Parliament and to the Council detailed information about the implementation of fisheries agreements in force and a financial forecast for the remainder of the year. It must also provide a forward-looking document, which must be transmitted by the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions.
In the event that the Commission considers that the conditions for mobilising the EURI recovery instrument are met, it must submit a proposal to the Parliament and of the Council for a transfer from that reserve to the corresponding budget lines in accordance with the Financial Regulation. If appropriations relating to fisheries agreements, including the reserve, prove insufficient, it shall provide the EU countries with the appropriate information on causes of such a situation.
Every year, each institution must provide the Commission with the following information:
the total amount of CFSP operating expenditure for the financial year;
the amount of appropriations for each of the headings;
an estimate of the costs of the various CFSP actions;
a forecast for payment appropriations for at least the period at least until 2027;
measures to be taken in the event of insufficient progress towards achieving applicable targets.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Decisions (EU and EU countries) have to incorporate them into national law by 1 January 2022.
BACKGROUND
The EU is one of the largest donors in the world, with a combined gross domestic product (GDP) of more than €1.2 trillion.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ (European Commission).

Economic and social support for COVID-19 victims
Economic and Social support for CoVID - 19 victims
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — exceptional and temporary empowerment of the EU’s own resources
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an EU-wide programme of economic and social assistance for the victims of the crisis caused by the crisis in the former Yugoslavian Democratic Republic of Macedonia (known as the ‘CoVID’).
It aims to support the economic recovery of EU countries that have been particularly hard hit by the COVID crisis.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries which have been directly or indirectly affected by COVID.
The measures covered by the regulation are:
support for the recovery of the economy, employment and social cohesion;
economic and social development in rural areas in addressing the impact of COVID–19;
financial support for the implementation of specific EU programmes.
Funding
The instrument is financed by EU countries’ own resources (i.e. the revenue they receive from their own resources) and the EU budget.
Implementation
The instruments are financed by the EU's own resources.
EU countries must provide the EU with the necessary financial resources to implement the measures set out in the regulation. The EU countries must ensure that the measures are implemented in accordance with the relevant EU programmes and that they have approved by the European Commission or by their EU counterparts by 31 December 2023.

Summary 66:
Safer economic governance in the euro area: the Stability and Growth Pact (SGP) and the fines for countries that fail to comply
Safer Economic governance in Europe: the stability and growth pact (the SGP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — strengthening the surveillance of national economic policies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system of sanctions for EU countries which fail to implement the stabilisation and corrective parts of the European Economic and Fiscal Union (Euratom).
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ deficit and debt data are accurate and transparent.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to EU countries whose currency is the euro.
It applies to all EU countries with an annual gross domestic product (GDP) of at least 2.5 % of the EU’s GDP.
EU countries that do not comply with the SGP must lodge with the European Commission an interest-bearing deposit amount of 0.2 % of their GDP in the preceding year.
The amount of this deposit must not exceed €1.5 billion.
If the EU country fails to lodge the deposit within 10 days of adoption of the Council recommendation, the Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for the failure to do so. The Council may, within 20 days, recommend that the Commission cancel the deposit or take further action to reduce the amount of the deposit.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO ENTER INTO FORCE?
   
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation applies from 1 January 2019.
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable growth and jobs’ on the Euratom website.

Summary 67:
Censuses of population and housing — common rules for the decennial provision of comprehensive data on population and Housing
Censures of population AND housing — Common rules for their collection and dissemination
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 — common EU rules for population and households
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out common rules to ensure that EU countries provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the population covering determined demographic, social and economic characteristics of persons, families and households, as well as on housing at a national, regional and local level.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
EU countries must submit to Eurostat data on:
the population of the EU;
the number of people living in their place of usual residence for a continuous period of at least 12 months before the reference date; or
those who arrived in the EU for at least one year with the intention of staying there for more than one year.
The data must be collected on the basis of:
a combination of register-based censuses and sample surveys;
traditional surveys with rotating samples (rolling censuses);
appropriate surveys with rolling samples.
Each EU country must submit a programme of the statistical data and of the metadata to be transmitted to fulfil the requirements of the regulation.
Eurostat, in cooperation with the national statistical authorities, must provide methodological recommendations designed to ensure the quality of the data and metadata produced, acknowledging, in particular, the Conference of European Statisticians’ Recommendations for the 2010 Censuses.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The European Union (EU) has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality and comparability of its statistics.
For more information, see:
‘Census’ on Eurostat’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Census: an annual survey of the population of an EU country and the number of persons living in its territory. It covers all aspects of population, social, economic and household characteristics, including the age, sex and ethnic origin of people, families, households and the place of residence of persons in the country.

Summary 68:
Rules of Procedure for the European Parliament
RULESP rules of procedure
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2019/302/EU — Rules of Procedure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE RULES OF PROCEDURE?
The European Parliament (EPS) is the European Union’s (EU) legislative body. It is responsible for the conduct of the legislative business of the EU and for the implementation of EU policies and programmes.
The EPS sets out the rules for the functioning of the EPP.
KEY POINTS
Membership
EPS members have the right to:
exercise their mandate freely and independently,
not be bound by any instructions and
not receive a binding mandate.
They are entitled to certain privileges and immunities, including:
the right to inspect the premises of the Parliament and to participate actively in the work of its committees and delegations;
the protection of their personal and professional interests;
privileges, including immunity from prosecution and immunity from liability for the performance of their parliamentary duties;
protection of their privacy, including the protection of personal data and personal data of their spouses and children;
access to the information of the European Commission, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Committee on the Functioning of the Council and the Council of Europe.
Members may not be prevented from performing their duties by virtue of their privileges or immunities if:
they have not been able to perform their duties in a way that would damage their personal interests or the interests of the public interest;
they are confident that they have already exercised their immunities;
their immunity has been waived;
it would be difficult for them to carry out their duties or to perform them in a manner that would cause damage to their private interests or to the public interests of their spouse or children.
Member immunity
Member immunities are granted to Members of Parliament who have not exercised their privileges and immunity in the exercise of their duties and who are not able to disclose their personal or professional interests. Member immunity may be waived if the Member is confident that their immunity has already been exercised and that they are not capable of performing their legislative duties or that would make it difficult to perform those duties, or that it would cause them to disclose personal interests, or if they have not performed their Parliamentary duties in accordance with their personal immunities.
A Member may, on the basis of a reasoned decision, be denied the right of inspection a Parliament if, after inspection would cause such damage to the interests or for them, they would not be able to do their duties for reasons of public interest or for their personal defence.
In exercising its powers on privileges and Immunities, Parliament must act to uphold its integrity as a democratic legislative assembly and to ensure the independence of its Members in their performance of its duties.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
They apply from 22 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The rules of Procedure are part of a package of rules on the workings of the assembly adopted by the High Level Group on Gender Equality and Diversity on 11 April 2018.
For more information, see:
‘The EPP’ on the EEP's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS

Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 652/2014 of 22 May 2014 on the interpretation of certain provisions of the Statute for Members of the Union and repealing the Act of 20 September 1976 concerning the election of the members of European Parliament by direct universal suffrage and national legislation deriving from the Treaties.

SUMMARY OF:

The European Union’s (EU's) new data protection law (the European Union's (EU)) sets up an independent data protection supervisory body to ensure the protection of the personal data of EU citizens.

advising EU countries on data protection matters;
coordinating with the European Commission and national data protection authorities in the field of data protection;
reviewing complaints and judicial remedies;
adopting opinions or formal comments on complaints;
making recommendations to EU countries and EU countries;
assisting the Commission in its work;
approving and implementing decisions on complaints, investigations, investigations and judicial proceedings.

Summary 70:
The EU’s own resources — recycling and reuse of plastic packaging waste (recycled and reused)
The EU's own resources – recycling and reused plastic packaging wastes (RECOVERY AND RESILIENCE)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2014/335/EU on the introduction of a new own resource based on value added tax (VAT)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets up a new system of own resources based on the gross national income (GNI) of EU countries.
The new own resources will be calculated on the basis of the total amount of plastic waste generated by EU countries each year.
KEY POINTS
The own resources are calculated using a weighted average of the GNI of each EU country. They will be divided by the population of the EU country concerned.
EU countries will be able to contribute up to €1.5 billion to the own resources for the period 2014-2027.
This amount will be used for the following purposes:
the recovery and resilience of the European Union (EU) in the event of a crisis;
the financing of the recovery and resiliency of the Member States in the aftermath of the crisis; and
the payment of interest to the European Financial Stability Facility (EFSF).
The decision sets out the amounts that EU countries will have to provide for the repayment of their own resources by 31 December 2023. These amounts will not exceed 1.40 % of the sum of all the EU countries’ GNIs.
In addition, EU countries must provide the EFSF with the amounts of cash resources that they have provisionally provided in the EU budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The Decision has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been working on a new EU own resource since 2013.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 71:
Tyre labelling — fuel efficiency, wet grip and external rolling noise (EU rules on tyres)
Tyre labels — fuel consumption and other parameters
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1242 — EU rules on the labelling of tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the information on tyres on the European Commission’s website.
It lays down rules on fuel efficiency and other performance parameters for tyres.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all new and re-treaded tyres, as well as to tyres fitted on new vehicles. It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 2222/2009, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2021.
EU countries must ensure that tyre labels are clearly displayed in all technical promotional material and visual advertisements for specific tyre types, including where such material is made available on the internet. They must also include the following information:
the fuel efficiency of the tyre in actual use, within the constraints arising from the need for reliable, accurate and reproducible laboratory testing, in order to enable end-users to compare different tyres and to limit testing costs for manufacturers.
the performance of the tyres during use, including their wet grip, tyre abrasion and mileage.
The EU countries must also ensure that the tyre label contains a pictogram showing the minimum ice grip index values set out in the International Organisation for Standardisation (ISO) standard for tyres, and the minimum roadworthiness index values for new tyres, which must be included on the label of a tyre which meets the requirements of that standard.
A pictogram must also be included in the technical documentation uploaded to the product database by suppliers on a voluntary basis.
Annexes to the regulation provide information on:
fuel efficiency,
wet grip and
external rolling noise.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2022.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the harmonisation of EU laws on the sale of motor vehicles and their trailers.
For more information, see:
‘Tyre Labelling’ on the Commission's website
Fuel efficiency (European Commission).
KEY TERMSCategories of tyres covered by the regulation
Type of tyre: tyres designed and manufactured for use on the road and tyres intended for use in the passenger compartment of a vehicle.
Fuel consumption: the amount of fuel consumed by the vehicle and the number of kilometres driven.
External rolling noise: the noise emitted by the tyres when the tyre is being driven on a road.

Summary 72:
EU financial support for customs control at the external borders of the EU (External Customs Instrument)
European Union (EU) financial support on customs control is designed to ensure that the EU’s external borders are effectively controlled.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 552/2017 of 23 March 2017 on the European Union financial support to customs control and on the establishment of a European Union Customs Control Instrument.
SUMMARY
The European Union is a customs union (customs union) which aims to protect the financial and economic interests of the Union and its Member States. It is therefore important to provide EU countries with the necessary financial resources to enable them to ensure equivalence in the performance of border control and customs control procedures at the EU's external borders.
To achieve this objective, the EU provides financial support through grants, procurement, prizes and indirect implementation.
The aim of the instrument is to:
increase the effectiveness of customs controls at EU external borders;
improve cooperation between customs authorities and other law enforcement authorities;
ensure that customs controls are effective, efficient and cost-effective;
encourage the use of modern and reliable customs control equipment;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice.
Funding
The EU provides funding for the following purposes:
to EU countries to improve their performance of customs control;
to improve cooperation between EU countries in the field of customs and border control.
EU countries must:
provide the European Commission with a list of EU countries that are eligible to receive EU financial support;
use the funds for the purpose of enhancing customs controls with equivalent results;
implement the EU customs control instrument on their own initiative;
apply the instrument for the purposes of the implementation of EU programmes or instruments.
Implementing the instrument
The Commission is responsible for:
establishing and implementing the instrument;
monitoring the results achieved by the instrument and actions under it;
providing the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions with information on the instruments and their implementation;
assisting the Commission in implementing the EU Customs control Instrument on its own initiative.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 April 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 April 2018.
BACKGROUND
Customs control is one of the main pillars of the customs union and is essential to ensure the smooth functioning of the internal market and the protection of the interests of EU citizens.
For more information, see:
EU customs control (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Customs control: the control of goods entering and exiting the EU by means of customs procedures.
* Border control: a customs procedure in which the customs authorities of an EU country check whether goods are being moved through the territory of a non-EU country.

Summary 73:
Citizens’ rights in the internal market for electricity (EU directive on citizen energy)
Citizen’s rights in a liberalised internal market of electricity
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/36/EU on citizen’t rights in electricity markets
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to ensure that citizens have equal access to the internal electricity market in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU citizens, regardless of where they live, work or live.
It does not apply to EU countries that have not yet liberalised their internal markets for electricity.
EU countries must ensure that consumers have the right to:
have their electricity metered by a single metering point;
have access to a network of metering points across the EU;
be able to manage their electricity consumption on their own initiative;
benefit from lower electricity prices or other incentive payments;
participate in community energy initiatives.
Meeting points must be accessible to all consumers, irrespective of their location.
Customers must be able to make their own billing arrangements and have access to information on their consumption, including:
their consumption and consumption costs;
their energy consumption and energy consumption charges;
the amount of electricity they consume and how much they pay for it;
how they manage their consumption and their energy consumption costs.
Member States must also ensure that:
metering points are open to all energy suppliers, including new energy service providers (e.g. small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs)) and energy storage providers (i.e. energy storage companies) with a minimum of 1 megawatt of installed capacity;
market operators have the power to set their own fixed electricity prices and to set variable electricity prices.
The market operator must also have the authority to: provide information on the metering system and on consumers’ consumption;provide consumers with information on consumption costs, including the amount of energy they pay and how they can control their consumption;establish a contact point for consumers to make complaints about their electricity bills.
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 amends the directive to allow the Commission to adopt delegated acts in the light of technological developments.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
 			
For more information, see:
‘Citizen energy’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Metering: the process by which electricity consumers are billed for their consumption.
* Distribution system operator: the company responsible for the operation of the electricity distribution system.
ACT
  The directive on the citizen energy market (recast) is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and liberalise the EU electricity market.

Summary 75:
The EU’s 2020 climate action and international climate leadership (European Green Deal)
The European Union's 2020 climate change and international leadership
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — the EU's 2020 Climate Action Plan
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a framework for the implementation of the 2020 EU climate action plan.
It aims to ensure that the EU is a global leader in the fight against climate change.
KEY POINTS
The EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle climate change, and to delivering on the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’) by 2020.
The regulation sets out how the EU will meet the long-term temperature goal of 1.5 °C above pre-industrial levels by the end of the century, which is set out in Article 2(1) of the Paris agreement.
EU countries are required to set national targets to reduce their greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by at least 40 % below 1990 levels by 2030.
By 2050, the EU must have no net GHG emissions of greenhouse gases (GHGs) from its activities.
To achieve this goal, EU countries must:
reduce GHGs by 40 % by 2030;
set up a low-carbon economy by 2050;
ensure that GHGs are eliminated from the EU economy by 2035;
put in place measures to reduce GHGs from their activities by 2040;
50 % by 2045 % by 2050.
Implement the EU ETS (the European Union Emissions Trading System (ETS), which aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions in a cost-effective way.
In addition, the regulation sets up a European Commission Climate Advisory Board to monitor and assess the impact of climate change on the environment and the health of citizens.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Commission has, in its communication of 11 December 2019 entitled ‘The European Green Deal’, set out a new growth strategy that aims to transform the EU into a fair and prosperous society, with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy, where there are no net emissions of GHGs in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use.
For more information, see:
‘The Green Deal 2020’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 76:
Preventing and responding to health crises in the EU: EU Pre-access Assistance Programme
Prevent and dealing with health crises
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — European Union Pre-Access Assistance programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Union (EU’s) pre-access assistance programme (pre-access programme), which aims to prevent, prepare for, respond to and manage health crises within the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme is designed to support the prevention and response of health crises and to reduce the impact of non-communicable diseases (NCDs) on individuals and society.
It aims to:
increase awareness of the importance of preventive and curative measures;
promote the use of information to prevent and respond to NCDs;
ensure the availability of medicines and medical devices;
support the production, procurement and management of crisis-relevant products, while ensuring complementarity with other EU instruments.
Programmes
Pre-access is funded by the European Commission and the European Investment Bank (EIB).
The European Commission is responsible for the overall budget of the programme.
The budget is balanced and focuses on the following areas:
preventing, preparing for, responding to and managing health crises;
contributing to the development of the European Medicines Agency (EMEA);
promoting health literacy;
improving access to medical devices and medicines;
providing information to the general public and healthcare professionals;
facilitating the exchange of information on NCD-related products and services;
encouraging cooperation between EU countries.
Implementing measures
The EU countries are responsible for their health policies and for the organisation, management and delivery of health services and medical care.
EU countries have the responsibility for the implementation of their national health policies, but the EU is also responsible for protecting public health in a spirit of European solidarity.
In line with the objectives of the EEA, the programme is supported by the EIA and the EIB. The EIA supports the EMA and EIB in the areas of information, communication and information technology (ICT) and information and communication technologies (ICT).
Implementation
The EIA is implemented under the framework of the Horizon 2020 programme, which was launched by the Commission in 2019.
Actions
The Programme is funded through the EU's general budget, which is divided into the following categories:
Prevention and response to health emergencies:
the EIA aims to strengthen the capacity of EU countries to respond to, and prevent, health emergencies;
prevention and control of the spread of diseases;
health literacy: to encourage people to take an active role in managing their health by improving their health literacy, access to sexual and reproductive healthcare, quality of life, workers’ health, productivity, competitiveness and inclusiveness, while reducing pressures on national healthcare systems and national budgets.
Promotion of healthy lifestyles: the programme aims to promote healthy lifestyles by encouraging people to adopt a healthy lifestyle and to improve their knowledge and skills in order to reduce their risk of developing and transmitting diseases.
Health literacy: the aim is to improve the knowledge of the causes and symptoms of diseases and to encourage the development and use of effective preventive and control measures. It is also to encourage healthy lifestyles and to promote the use and dissemination of information about the prevention, diagnosis, monitoring and treatment of diseases. The programme also aims to improve health literacy by encouraging the sharing of best practice in the field of health.
Information and communication activities:
to provide information to individuals, healthcare professionals and the public;
to promote health literacy in the context of public health policy and public health information campaigns; and
to combat misinformation and disinformation.
Disease prevention and control: this includes:
information on the causes of and prevention of diseases, including the prevention of communicable diseases, and the control of their spread;
education and training on the management of health systems and health systems;
information and awareness-raising activities;
exchange of best practices in the area of health care;
research and development on the development, use and distribution of health information;
the development of information and communications technologies;
training of health professionals and health care professionals on the application of EU policies and programmes;
development of information systems and information systems on the health and safety of persons;
assessment of the situation in the event of a health crisis;
monitoring and monitoring of the supply chain of medicines, medical devices, medical products and medical equipment;
implementation of the information and information exchange system;
coordination of information campaigns and information campaigns on the availability and quality of medical products;
organisations and bodies responsible for coordinating the response to a crisis, such as the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and the World Health Organization (WHO);
the coordination of the COVID-19 outbreak.

Summary 79:
Aromatised wine products — protection of geographical indications
Aroma-driven wines — protecting geographical indications of certain aromatised wines
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — rules on the production, description, presentation, labelling and protection of certain aromatic wine products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out rules on how aromatisation of wine products is to be marketed and sold in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all wines produced in the EU for export. It does not apply to wines produced on the territory of a non-EU country.
It does not affect the production methods used for aromatising wine products.
The rules apply to all products with a geographical indication that are produced in their own territory, in so far as such rules are compatible with EU law.
Production methods and methods of analysis
The products covered by the regulation must meet the following criteria:
they must be produced using traditional production methods;
they should be produced in a way that minimises the risk of contamination of the wine’s aromatics;
the product must be labelled with a description of the specific characteristics of the product and the geographical indication.
They must be accompanied by a sales denomination (e.g. ‘Aromatised’, ‘Aromatic’ or ‘OJ’).
The European Commission has the power to:
approve or reject applications for protection of a geographical indications on grounds of inadmissibility;
establish and maintain a register of the geographical indications protected under this regulation;
review applications on the basis of scientific, technical and statistical evidence;
make recommendations to EU countries on how to ensure that the products comply with the rules.
Labelling
EU countries must ensure that their products are labelled with:
the name of the production method and the sales denomination;
a description of each product;
an indication which identifies the wine product as originating in a region, a specific place, or a country, where a given quality, reputation or other characteristics of that product is essentially attributable to its geographical origin.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Aroma’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 80:
Fishing opportunities for vulnerable fish stocks in the EU
Fishing opportunity for vulnerable species in the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/973 on fishing opportunities for certain species in EU waters of the eastern Mediterranean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the fishing opportunities to be made available to EU countries for certain fish stocks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fisheries in the waters of EU Member States’ exclusive economic zones (EEZs);
fishing opportunities in the exclusive economic zone of the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) and the EU’s other EEZs (EEA and EFTA countries);
allocated fishing opportunities by Member States in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 1380/2013 on the conservation of marine biological resources (see summary).
It does not apply to fishing opportunities that fall under the landing obligation (i.e. the obligation to keep the fish caught for the purpose of discarding them).
By-catch
The fishing opportunities are set at levels that help the biomass of vulnerable stocks to recover to sustainable levels. By-catch are fish that have been caught by the fishing sector and are considered to be by-catch because they are part of the ecosystem of the species concerned.
The EU countries must ensure that:
by-catch quotas are set according to the best available scientific and economic information;
the fishing opportunities do not exceed the maximum sustainable yield (MSY) of the fish species concerned;
there is a limit on the number of by-catches that can be made in a given year;
bycatch quotas do not fall below the maximum MSY of the stocks concerned; and
the EU countries do not discard the fish that they catch.
Bycatch quotas
The European Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the fishing of certain species by the EU countries;
assessing the impact of the fishing activities on the stocks;
developing and implementing conservation measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 August 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Fishing Opportunities’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Bycatch: fish caught by fishing vessels that are not intended for human consumption, such as sharks, rays, skates and skates.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —

* Monetary assistance: the EU provides financial support to countries that are not members of the IMF, the EIB or the EFSF, to help them to meet their financial needs.

Summary 82:
Recovery plan for swordfish in the Mediterranean Sea (‘Mediterranean swordfish’) — multiannual recovery plan from 2017 to 2031 (Regulation (EU) 2019/2107)
Recovery of swordfish
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, 2017/2106 — implementing general rules for the implementation by the European Union of the Multiannual Recovery Plan for Swordfish (Xiphias gladius)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s fisheries are managed in line with the recommendations of the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
the fishing effort of fishing vessels flying their flag is commensurate with the fishing opportunities for Mediterranean swordfish available to each EU country;
the number of vessels flying the EU flag is proportionate to the number of by-catches permitted by EU countries’ national quotas;
fishing effort is based on transparent and objective criteria, including those of an environmental, social and economic nature, and shall also endeavour to distribute national quotas fairly among the various fleet segments, giving consideration to traditional and artisanal fishing, and to provide incentives to EU fishing vessels deploying selective fishing gear or using fishing techniques with reduced environmental impact.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework for the period 2014-2020 (MFF) - rules for the use of the EU’s multiannual budget for this period (2014-2020)
The multiannational financial framework (mFF) is the main mechanism for the European Union (EU) to provide financial support to EU countries in the context of their medium-term development priorities.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2014 of 1 January 2014 on the multiannually financial framework
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for using the MFF, the main financial instrument for the EU, for the years 2014 to 2020.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The MFF is designed to provide the EU with the financial resources it needs to prepare and implement its long-term investment priorities. It aims to:
provide the necessary financial resources for the implementation of the objectives of EU policies and programmes;
support EU countries’ efforts to develop their economies;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice on EU policies, programmes and activities;
ensure that EU countries have the resources they need to implement their investment priorities;
contribute to the development of a sustainable and inclusive EU economy.
Categories of MFF funding
The main categories of funding are:
budgetary allocations for the current and future financial years (2014 to 2020) are set out in the annex to the regulation. These are divided into the following categories:
total appropriations for payments:
€7.5 billion for 2014-2019;
€9 billion for 2020-2021 billion for 2021-2022 billion for 2022-2024 billion for 2024-2025 billion for 2025-30 billion for 2030-40 billion for 2027-30.
Specific rules are also necessary for dealing with large-scale infrastructure projects whose lifetime extends well beyond the period set for the mFF.
Special instruments
The European Union Solidarity Fund is intended to allow financial assistance in the event of major disasters occurring on the territory of an EU country or of a candidate country, as defined in the relevant basic act. It has a maximum annual amount of €500 million (2011 prices).
The Emergency Aid Reserve (EIR) is set up to provide emergency financial support in the case of major natural or man-made disasters.
The Flexibility Instrument is set at €471 million for each of the years.
There is a limit on the annual amount available for the Contingency Margin, the European Globalisation Adjustment Fund (EGAF) and the European Research and Innovation Fund (ERASMUS).
Specific flexibility to tackle youth unemployment and strengthen research
The EIR has a limit of €150 million per year for each year.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 31 December 2014 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has an annual budget of €20.8 billion for the 2014-20 period.
For more information, see:
‘The EU budget’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 84:
The European Maritime Fund for the Farmed Animals (EMFAF) (European Fisheries Fund) (Euratom)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — the European Maritime Facility for the Fished Animals
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EMFAF’s priorities, its budget and the specific rules for providing EU funding to undertakings in the fishery and aquaculture sector.
KEY POINTS
The European Union fisheries fund (EMF) aims to support the common fisheries policy (CFP) and the EU‘s maritime policy.
The EMF is funded by the EU budget.
EMF funding is divided between the EU countries’ fisheries budgets and the European Fisheries Fund (EFSP), which is managed by the European Commission.
EU countries must provide the EMF with at least 50 % of the total EU fisheries budget for the period 2019-2023.
Eligibility for EMF funding
EMFs are eligible for EU funding if they meet the following criteria:
they fulfil the objectives of the CFP and the maritime policy;
they contribute to achieving the environmental, economic, social and employment objectives set out in Article 2 of the EU fisheries policy, in particular:
restoring and maintaining populations of harvested species above levels which can produce maximum sustainable yield (MSY);
avoiding and reducing, as far as possible, unwanted catches;
minimising the negative impact of fishing activities on the marine ecosystem.
Funding
The regulation sets out specific eligibility rules for certain other investments in the fishing fleet, so as to prevent those investments from contributing to overcapacity or overfishing. These include investments in selective fishing gear, in the improvement of port infrastructures and in the marketing of unwanted catches.
Amendments to the regulation have been incorporated into the original text.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘The EU fisheries fund’ is part of the Euratom programme.
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries Fund’ on the Euratom website.

Fishing closure periods for purse seine vessels for tropical tunas and sharks in the Pacific Ocean (Inter-American Tropical Tuna Commission) (IATTC) area (Regulation (EU) 2017/2403)
Fishingclosure periods for purses and trawls for tropical tuna and sharks
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 — fishing closure periods and conservation and management measures for the long-term conservation and sustainable use of the fishery resources in the IATTC area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to vessels operating in the area covered by the IITC area. It covers the period from 29 July to 8 October or from 9 November to 19 January.
It does not apply to vessels fishing in the following areas:
the Pacific Ocean bounded by the following lines: the 50°S parallel from its intersection with meridian 150°W until the intersection with 130°W;
the 4°W parallel from the intersection of meridian 140°W and the 4°S perpendicular from its intersections with 150°E until the convergence of the meridians.
Operators of EU fishing vessels may operate within one nautical mile of a data buoy, provided that those vessels do not interact with the data buoy or deploy fishing gear, as set out in paragraphs 1 and 2 and 3 of the regulation.
EU fishing vessels must inform the European Commission by 15 June each year of which of the two closure periods are applicable to their vessels.
The IATC area covers the following waters:
The Pacific Ocean;
The Atlantic Ocean; and
the Caribbean Sea.

Summary 86:
Better law-making: EU institutions’ common approach
Better law making: EU authorities’ collective approach
SUMMARY OF:
Common Understanding between the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission on Better Law-Making
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve cooperation between the three European Union (EU) institutions in order to improve the quality of EU legislation.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The three institutions recognise the role and responsibility of national parliaments as laid down in the Treaties, in Protocol No 1 on the role of National Parliaments in the European Union annexed to the Treaty on European Union, the Treaty establishing the European Atomic Energy Community and the Protocol No 2 on the application of the principles of subsidiarity and proportionality.
In this context, the three institutions agree to reinforce the EU’s annual and multiannual programming in line with Article 17(1) of the Treaty, which entrusts the Commission with the task of initiating EU-wide programming. The Commission will engage in a dialogue with the EU institutions both before and after the adoption of its annual work programme (known as the ‘Commission Work Programme’). The Commission Work Programme will include major legislative and non-legislative proposals for the following year, including repeals, recasts, simplifications and withdrawals.
Public consultations will be conducted in an open and transparent way, ensuring that the modalities and time limits of those public consultations allow for the widest possible participation.
EU countries’ experts will be consulted before the Commission adopts a proposal.
Implementation of delegated acts will be based on the best available scientific and technical information.
Delegislation will be drawn up by the Commission on the basis of a proposal submitted by the EU countries.
Each institution will submit a proposal to the Commission for a vote within 6 months of adoption of the proposal. If the Commission decides not to submit the proposal in response to such a request, it will inform the institution concerned of the detailed reasons, and will provide, where appropriate, an analysis of possible alternatives and respond to any issues raised by the colegislators in relation to analyses concerning 'European added value' and concerning the "cost of non-Europe".
The European Parliament and the Council will, when they consider this to be appropriate and necessary for the legislative process, carry out impact assessments on the Commission's proposal. They will also inform the Commission of the results of these impact assessments before adopting or revising their guidelines concerning their tools for better law making (public and stakeholder consultations, impact assessments and ex-post evaluations).
The Commission will also explain in its explanatory memoranda how the measures proposed are justified in the light of the principle of subsidarity, proportionality and how they are compatible with fundamental rights.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The common agreement entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Better Law-making’ on the EU's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
  —   —
Common understanding between the representatives of the three EU institutions (European Parliament, European Council and European Commission) on better lawmaking (Common Understanding) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2019, pp. 1-12)
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 of 27 December 2018 on the conclusion of the Common Understanding between Member States on better Law- making (Oj L 183 of 1.2.2018, p. 1)

Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiative — rules for organising and collecting statements of support from EU citizens
Citizens' initiative — rights for citizens
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — rules on the organisation of citizens’ initiatives
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures and conditions to be followed by EU citizens who wish to participate in a citizens' initiative (i.e. a proposal for a legal act of the European Union for the purpose of implementing the Treaties similar to the right conferred on the European Parliament under Article 225 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) and on the Council under Article 241 TFEU.
KEY POINTS
Organisers of citizens' initiatives must:
provide the European Commission with information and informal advice on citizens' activities;
ensure that the initiative is representative of a Union interest;
collect and send the necessary information to the Commission within 3 months of receiving the request.
The Commission must recognise the certificates issued by the competent authorities of other EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the information provided by the organisers is accurate, complete and up-to-date. They must also ensure that information on the sources of support and funding for the initiative are available on the organisers’ website.
National authorities must verify that the organisers are complying with the regulation’s rules before issuing a certificate to the organisers.
Organisations must also:
use an online data collection system to collect the necessary support from signatories;
keep a register of the information they have collected;
use a publicly accessible website to provide the public with information about the initiative and its activities.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
Citizen initiatives are a key tool for the implementation of EU policies and programmes. They allow citizens to express their views on EU policies, programmes and activities. They also allow the Commission to act on their behalf.
For more information, see:
Calls for a European Union (European Commission).

Summary 88:
Statistics on the placing on the market and use of pesticides in the EU (Regulation (EC) No 223/2009)
Statistical data on pesticides in EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — EU statistics on pesticides on the European Union market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a common framework for the systematic production of EU-wide statistics on the placement on the EU market and the use of those pesticides which are plant protection products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all pesticides listed in Annex III contained in pesticides placed on the markets in each EU country.
EU countries must collect and transmit to the European Commission (Eurostat) data on the pesticide placement on their market and on their use.
Each 5-year period, EU countries must compile statistics on pesticide use for each selected crop within a reference period as defined in Section 4.
The data must be sent to Eurostat within 12 months of the end of the reference year. EU countries may choose the reference period at any time of the five-year.
Data collection
Each EU country must collect, transmit and process agricultural holding data, including confidential data, in accordance with the schedules and with the periodicity specified in Annexes I and II.
Eurostat must submit a report on the implementation of the regulation to the EU Parliament and the Council every 5 years.
Quality report
Each year, Eurostat must send a quality report to the Commission on the quality of the data transmitted and on the methodology used to collect the data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
    —
It has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  —

Summary 89:
Plant protection products — EU rules for active substances and the placing on the market of plant protection products (Regulation (EU) 2017/852)
Plant Protection Products — Regulation (EU), the EU’s plant protection legislation
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2003/96/EC on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to active substances
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the rules for the authorisation of active substances* for the manufacture and use of plants and to ensure that these substances do not adversely affect human or animal health or the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances, including micro-organisms having general action against organisms or on plants, that are used as active substances for plant protection. These substances are referred to as ‘plant protection products’.
Active substances may contain safeners or synergists for which similar rules should be provided.
Authorisations are granted by EU countries on the basis of harmonised criteria. They must be based on:
the safety of the active substance and its effect on plant health and the environment;
the protection of human and animal health; and
the environmental impact of the plant protection product.
EU countries must ensure that active substances or products placed on the European Union market do not affect human, animal or plant health, the environment or the health of plants or animals.
The authorisation procedure is based on a number of criteria, including the following a comparative assessment of the substances’ effects on human health and plant health.
the authorisation must be granted by the EU country in which has the highest level of protection for plant health or environmental protection.
An advisory procedure is set up to assist EU countries in the application of the regulation.
If an EU country considers that an active substance does not pose a risk to human health or plant protection, it must inform the European Commission and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) of its decision. The Commission must then inform the EFSA of the reasons for the decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
ACT
Commission Regulation (EEC) No 852/2003 of 12 December 2003 on the Harmonisation of laws relating to the authorisations of active substance for the production, use and sale of plants, and amending Directive 91/414/EEC (see summary)

Summary 90:
Internal market information system (IMI) for administrative cooperation between EU countries and between EU States and the European Commission
Internal market Information system (IMEI)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — rules for the use of an Internal Market Information System for administrative coordination between EU Member States and between the European Union and the EU Commission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for using an internal market Information System (‘IMI’) to improve the functioning of the EU’s internal market by providing an effective, user-friendly tool for the implementation of EU acts.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1), the United States of America (1) and Canada (1).
It applies to EU countries that have adopted an EU law on administrative cooperation or mutual assistance in the internal market.
EU countries must ensure that their IMI coordinators have the necessary resources to carry out their obligations in accordance with the regulation.
Each EU country appoints a coordinator to carry the main tasks of the IMI.
The coordinator is responsible for:
coordinating the use and operation of IMI;
ensuring that IMI is compatible with the security and confidentiality requirements for the exchange of information in IMI and can be offered at a reasonable cost.
Implementing the regulation
The IMI coordinator must be independent of the national coordinator and may be appointed by the EU country in which IMI operates.

Summary 91:
Conservation, management, exploitation, monitoring, marketing and enforcement measures for fishery and aquaculture products in the Mediterranean and the Black Sea
Conservation and management of fisheries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 — rules for the application by the European Union of the conservation, management and exploitation of fisheries in the EU’s waters in accordance with the General Fisheries Commission for the Mediterranean (GFCM) Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE AGREEMENT?
The GFCM Agreement provides an appropriate framework for multilateral cooperation to promote the development, conservation, rational management and best utilisation of living marine resources in Mediterranean and Black Sea at levels which are considered sustainable and at low risk of collapse.
The EU is a contracting party to the agreement and its rules are binding on the EU and EU countries.
This regulation lays down the rules for applying by the EU of the Conservation, Management, Exploitation, Monitoring, Marketing and Enforcement measures established by the general fisheries commission for the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels operating in the waters of the Mediterranean, Black Sea and connecting waters.
Vessels authorised to fish in the area referred to in Article 4 of the regulation must be issued with a fishing authorisation by their EU country.
Authorisations are valid for a period of 5 years and must be renewed at least every 5 years.
Fishing vessels are subject to a number of conditions, including:
the use of minimum mesh size for nets used for trawling activities exploiting demersal stocks in the eastern Mediterranean;
a ban on the use of electronic devices in the fishing area;
the closure of the fishing season for dolphinfish and the reporting of lost days due to bad weather in the closed season.
EU countries must ensure that vessels flying their flag are accompanied by an EU fleet register number and that they carry out port inspections.
Dolphinfishes
The fishing season runs from 1 January to 31 August and is open to all species of fish, except for certain species of crustaceans, which are closed to fishing during certain periods of the season (e.g. during the breeding season).
Fisheries restricted areas
The following fisheries are excluded from the regulation:
fishing in the Gulf of Lions;
fisheries in the Atlantic Ocean;
aquaculture activities in the western Black Sea;
seaweed fishing in the North Atlantic Ocean.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2010. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Fishery management’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 2012/2009 of 20 January 2009 laying down the general provisions of the General Agreement for the Conservation of the Marine Life of the Member States and of the Union in the field of fishery management in the Community and amending Regulations (EC, EFTA, EC, EU and EFTA) (OJ L 183, 21.2.2009, pp. 1-8)

Summary 92:
Unitary patent protection in the EU — EU countries’ laws on patents (EU-EU unitary patent protection)
Unitary product patent protection
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1260/2012 — European patent with unitary effect
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that patents granted by the European Patent Office (EPO) in EU countries have the same set of claims in all EU countries.
It sets out the rules for granting and renewing patents in the post-grant phase.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
patents granted in the EPO’s European Patent Bulletin (EPB) are registered in the national patent registers of the participating EU countries;
patent proprietors pay a single annual renewal fee for a European patent.
The EPB publishes the list of EU countries that have granted and renewed EU patents.
A European patent granted in a participating EU country is treated as a national patent in all the EU countries provided that it has been granted in that EU country and has been registered in that country.
If a patent is granted in an EU country that has not granted a patent on its territory, the EU country in which the patent was granted is responsible for determining whether the patent has taken effect on their territory.
Where a patent does not take effect on its own territory, it is deemed not to have been granted as a patent and may be licensed to a third party from the EPB.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Uniform patent protection’ is a key element of the EU strategy to create a single market for goods and services across the EU.
For more information, see:
EU patent protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Unitary patent: a patent granted by a patent holder in one EU country to a patent proprietor in another.
* Product patent: an invention that has the potential to be used in more than one country and which has the ability to be patented in more EU countries than one.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 661/2012 of 21 December 2012 on the European patent system (UPS) (OJ L 183, 28.12.2012, pp. 1-8)
last update 28.01.2016

European Observatory of Counterfeits and Pirates (Eocop) - European level for the enforcement of Intellectual Property Rights in the Internal Market
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/2010 on the European Observatory onCounterfeiting & Piracy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE EOCOP?
The regulation sets up a European Observatory (OOCOP), which is responsible for collecting, analysing and disseminating relevant objective, comparable and reliable data on intellectual property (IP) rights and infringements of those rights.
The OOCOP’s main tasks are to:
facilitate and support the activities of national authorities, the private sector and the EU institutions relating to the enforcement and reporting of IP rights;
raise public awareness of the impact of infringements;
make recommendations to the Commission on issues falling within the scope of the regulation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The European Observatory is composed of representatives from the EU countries’ national trade mark offices and the European Commission.
It is based in Brussels and has the following tasks:
collect, analyse and disseminate relevant objective and comparable data regarding the value of IP;
identify and promote best practices and strategies to enforce IP rights and to raise public awareness;
provide technical assistance to EU countries and to officials from non-EU countries who are involved in the protection of IP right.

Summary 94:
International trade agreements — protection of the EU’s economic interests in the event of breaches by non-EU countries of international trade rules (Trade in goods and services — EU-non-EU agreements)
International trade obligations — protection for the EU's economic interests
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 — protection against breaches by third countries of the Union's rights under international trade agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to ensure that the European Union (EU) is able to exercise its rights to protect its economic interests when a third country breaches its international trade obligations in order to harm the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that have signed an international trade agreement with an EU country or a separate customs territory (i.e. a country which is not a member of the European Economic Area (EEA) or the Schengen area).
It does not apply to:
third countries that are not members of the EEA;
non-members of the WTO or the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) or other multilateral trade agreements.
It applies to any EU country that does not respect its obligations under the WTO Agreement on Trade in Goods and Services (GATS) or any other international agreement.
EU countries may take measures to protect their economic interests by:
suspending concessions or other obligations in the trade relations with the third country if the treatment accorded to goods from the EU is altered in a way that affects their interests;
imposing a mandatory price penalty on suppliers of goods or services originating in the EU;
rebalancing concessions and other obligations if no compensatory adjustments have been made;
restoring the level of nullification or impairment of the benefits accruing to the EU under the GATT.
The measures may include:
the imposition of a minimum price on tenders of suppliers of products or services established in and operating from the third EU country concerned and/or on that part of the tender consisting of goods originating in that country;
suspension of concessions, other obligations or other compensatory measures;
the introduction of a quota system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE?
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts determining the appropriate commercial policy measures.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘Safeguards’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2006 of 25 November 2006 on the protection of economic interests of the Member States in international trade matters (OJ L 183, 27.11.2006, pp. 1-12)

Summary 95:
Agreement between the EU and Central America on the stabilisation of the banana market and the protection of the EU banana sector
Agreement with the Central American Republic of Costa Rica
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/852 on the conclusion of the Agreement between the European Union and the Central America Republic on the establishment of a stabilisation mechanism for bananas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
 
The agreement aims to prevent serious harm to the EU’s banana growing sector, which is of great importance to the agricultural producers of many of the outermost regions of the Union.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Community (now the EU).
KEY POINTS
The EU and Costa Rica signed the agreement in June 2012.
Under the agreement, the Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the observance by Central America of the social and environmental standards laid down in Title VIII of the agreement.
Implementing acts for the temporary suspension of the preferential customs duty for bananas and for the extension of the monitoring period for certain sectors to which monitoring has been extended.
Monitoring of the implementation of the measures
The European Commission must ensure that the measures are implemented in line with the relevant EU legislation and that they are proportionate and proportionate to the risks to the banana sector.
An investigation into the existence of a threat of serious injury is carried out by the Commission to determine whether the conditions for imposing a safeguard measure are met.
If the Commission considers that there is a serious injury, it must initiate a proceeding to impose the safeguard measure.
A request for a proceeding must contain evidence that the necessary to impose a safeguard measures is met. The Commission must inform the parties concerned of the reason for initiating a proceeding and of the grounds for imposing the safeguard measures. The request must contain the following information:
the nature and amount of the increase in imports of the product concerned in absolute and relative terms, the share of the domestic market taken by increased imports and changes in the level of sales, production, productivity, capacity utilisation, profits and losses, and employment.
the level of imports of bananas from the country concerned and the number of workers employed by the company concerned.
In the event that the Commission does not reach a conclusion of a proceeding within the time limits set by the EU, it may make a decision to suspend the import of bananas or to impose provisional safeguard measures until the investigation is completed.
Where the Commission determines that the conditions are met, it can adopt definitive safeguard measures (known as ‘preventive measures’).
The Commission must notify the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of its decision to adopt the measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE AGENCY APPLY?
The Agreement entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
  * KEY TERMS
* Stabilisation mechanism: a mechanism designed to ensure the stability of the market for bananas in the EU.
* Safeguard measures: measures designed to protect the EU's banana sector against serious harm caused by the importation or export of bananas.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Decision 2010/853/EC of 29 June 2010 on the signature of the Agreements between the Union and its Member States, of the one part, and the Republics of Guatemala, Honduras, El Salvador, Nicaragua, Panama, Nicaragua and Panama on the creation of a mechanism for the prevention and protection of serious injuries to the Union banana sector (OJ L 347, 29.6.2010, pp. 1-8)
Agreements between Member States and their Member States are concluded by the European Commission and the governments of the countries of the Western Caribbean and the Caribbean, the Caribbean Sea, the Pacific Ocean, the Atlantic Ocean and the Gulf of Mexico.
Commission Implementing Act (EC) No 852/2018 of 11 December 2018 on the signing of the Framework Agreement for the establishment, in accordance with the provisions of Article 2(1) of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU) on the harmonisation of laws, regulations and administrative provisions of the Member States with regard to the establishment and enforcement of the laws of the Community, the European Free Trade Association (CFTA) and the Community Agreement on the Community Framework for the Protection of the Rights and Freedoms of the Nationals of the Banana (TFU) (OJC L 345, 30.12.2018, p. 1)

Summary 96:
Preservation of bank accounts and debtors’ assets in the EU (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Preservation OF bank accounts AND debtors' assets in EU (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the application for a Preservation Order in civil matters having cross-border implications
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the efficiency of enforcement of judgments in the European Union (EU), in particular in cases involving bank accounts.
It sets out the procedure for a creditor wishing to secure the enforcement of a later judgment on the substance of the matter prior to initiating proceedings on the matter and at any stage during such proceedings.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU citizens who have a bank account in another EU country.
Creditors may apply for a preservation order if:
the creditor has not yet obtained a judgment, court settlement or authentic instrument requiring the debtor to pay the creditor’s claim;
the debtor is a consumer domiciled in an EU country other than that of the EU country in which the creditor has an account or accounts maintained in that EU country, even if the creditor applies at the same time for a protection order which concerns an account and accounts maintained outside the EU.
The creditor must provide the court with all the information needed to identify the debtor's bank account before the creditor can obtain an order. The creditor must also provide proof of the initiation of the proceedings and provide proof that he lodged his application for an order within a specified period of time.
If the court considers that there is an urgent need for such account information, it must issue an order to ensure compensation to the debtor and it should be open to the court, in the absence of specific evidence as to the amount of the potential damage, to consider the amount in which an order is to be issued as a guideline for determining the security.
An order may be issued by the court of a country where the creditor is a resident and the bank account concerned is maintained in the country of domicile of the creditor. The court of that country must issue the order if it is satisfied, on the basis of the evidence submitted by the creditor, that the debtor has not complied with the conditions of the order and that the order is necessary to ensure that the payment of the judgment is made.
A court of the country which receives the order may refuse to enforce the order, unless it has the power to do so under certain conditions.
In the event of a breach of the conditions laid down in the regulation, the court in question may order the creditor to pay a fine of up to €10,000.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Implementation of the regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the Regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Preservation Order’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019-852 of 12 December 2019 on the accession of the Member States to the Treaty on European Union and on the enforcement procedures for judgments and orders concerning bank accounts (OJ L 183, 14.12.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 97:
European Border Surveillance System (Eurosur)
European border surveillance system (EuroSur)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 on border surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union Agency for the Coordination of European Border Surveillance (EUBS) and sets out rules for coordinating border surveillance operations at sea.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EUBS is responsible for the coordination of border surveillance at sea by EU countries and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBSA).
It is the EU’s external border control agency.
It has the responsibility for:
coordinating border surveillance in the event of an attempt to cross the external borders of the EU;
operating in the context of the Schengen acquis (the agreement between the EU, the EU countries, the European Economic Area (EEA) and the countries of the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) on the free movement of persons, goods and services across their external borders;
cooperating with the competent authorities of non-EU countries in the field of border control.
EU countries and EBSA must ensure that the operation is conducted in line with EU law and that it respects human dignity, fundamental rights and the rights of refugees and asylum seekers.
Cooperation between EU countries
Coordination of EBSAs is based on the principles of:
mutual respect for human dignity and fundamental rights;
non-discriminatory and proportionate measures;
the protection of human dignity;
human rights and fundamental freedoms of migrants and refugees;
protection of personal data, including the right to privacy and personal data protection;
safety of persons rescued at sea and their return to their country of origin or destination;
respect for the fundamental rights of the persons rescued and the protection of the child;
responsibility for the safe return of the rescued persons to their countries of origin and destination.
Operational plan
The operational plan must be established in accordance with the provisions of this regulation governing interception, rescue at sea (i.e. the operational area of a sea operation includes the search and rescue region of a third country, the establishment of communication channels with that third country and the setting up of contact points with those authorities.
The plan must include:
a description of the objectives of the operation;
an operational plan for the implementation of the operational plan;
details of the measures to be taken in the course of a surveillance operation and the procedures for the disembarkation of rescued persons at sea;
a list of persons to be rescued and those to be returned to their place of safety;
rules for the return of those rescued and disembarked at sea, including a list of the countries to which the persons are to be transferred.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
