Summary 98:
EU funding to support the development of the area of freedom, security and justice in the field of home affairs (2014-2020)
EU funding for the development and implementation of the EU’s common foreign and security policy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2014/852 — common principles on EU funding to the development, implementation and evaluation of EU funding in the areas of freedom and security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common EU funding rules for the EU's home affairs policy.
It aims to support EU countries’ efforts to develop and implement their national programmes in this field.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Union (EU) countries that are members of the European Economic Area (EEA).
EU countries are responsible for implementing their national home affairs policies and programmes.
The European Commission is responsible for coordinating EU funding for these policies, programmes and actions.
Funding is provided by the EU budget for the period 2014-2020.
Eligible actions
The EU funding is based on strategic multiannual programming, drawn up by each EU country in dialogue with the Commission.
Each EU country must establish, in a manner consistent with the principle of proportionality and the need to minimise administrative burden, a partnership with the authorities and bodies concerned to develop, implement and evaluate its national programmes throughout the period of funding.
Actions are eligible for EU funding if they meet the following criteria:
the objectives of the relevant EU policies and actions;
the EU priorities;
they are based on the principles of solidarity and respect for international and human rights law, including as regards actions implemented in non-EU countries.
they meet the criteria set out in Article 18(4) of the Treaty on European Union, including in relation to the management of the external borders and the protection of the Schengen acquis.
They must be coordinated with other EU policies, such as those of the Council of Europe, the European Parliament and the Council.
National authorities must:
ensure that all EU actions are coordinated and coordinated with each other;
coordinate their actions with the relevant national authorities;
identify and coordinate the actions of EU countries in line with their national policies;
provide information to the public and the media on the actions they are carrying out;
establish a website or website portal with the necessary information.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
EU home affairs — EU funding (European External Action Service)
KEY TERMS
* EU funding: the EU funding provided by EU countries to the European Commission to the EU countries, the EAS and the EEA countries to which it is applicable.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU/Euratom) No 852/2014 of 20 December 2014 on the common principles of European Union funding (OJ L 183, 21.12.2014, pp. 1-8)

Summary 99:
The Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund (AFMIF)
The asylum, migration and integration fund (AFMF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 establishing the Asylum, Migrations & Integration Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s first asylum and migration fund.
It aims to contribute to the development of the common EU policy on asylum and immigration and to the strengthening of the area of freedom, security and justice in the light of the application of the principles of solidarity and responsibility-sharing between the EU countries and cooperation with non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The AFMIF supports EU countries’ efforts to:
enhance their capacity to develop, monitor and evaluate their asylum policies in line with existing EU law;
facilitate legal migration to the EU in accordance with the economic and social needs of EU countries;
prepare the integration process already in the country of origin of the third-country nationals coming to the European Union;
ensure the protection of the rights and dignity of returnees.
EU countries are responsible for:
ensuring that their immigration and asylum policies are compatible with EU law and that they respect human rights and fundamental freedoms;
developing, implementing, monitoring and evaluating in general all immigration and integration strategies, policies and measures, including EU legal instruments.
Financing
The fund is financed by EU countries under their national programmes.
The amount of the funding is allocated to each EU country on the basis of the latest available statistical data.
Actions financed under the fund must be specific and complementary to actions financed by the European Social Fund (ESF), the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The EU countries may also use the funds for other purposes, such as:
supporting the development and implementation of the Common European Asylum System (CERAS);
facilitating the return of third country nationals to their countries of origin;
providing financial support for the implementation of a Union Resettlement Programme and the transfer of beneficiaries of international protection from one EU country to another.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Asylum and migration’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 852/2017 of 1 January 2017 establishing the asylum, Migration & Integration fund (OJ L 183, 1.2.2017, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 852 have been incorporated in the basic text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 101:
Non-sustainable fishing by non-EU countries in the EU — measures against them
Non-Sustainability of fisheries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — measures to be taken against non-sustainably fishing by third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the type of measures that may be taken with regard to non-Sustainable fishing* by third country with an interest in a fishery involving a stock of common interest to the EU and to the European Economic Area (EEA).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to countries that:
fishery activities that jeopardise the sustainability of the common fisheries of the EU or to the EEA;
fishing activities that are contrary to existing fishing patterns or the rights, duties and interests of other countries and the EU in its management;
are not compatible with international law, in particular with the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation (WTO).
It does not apply to fishing by vessels operating under the control of an EU or EEA regional fisheries management organisation (RFMO) or, where RFMOs have no competence for the stock in question, by means of ad hoc arrangements among the countries having a vested interest in the fishery.
The measures may include:
restrictions on the importation of fish products caught by vessels conducting fisheries on a common fishery;
restriction of the access to ports for those vessels;
preventing EU fishing vessels or EU fishing equipment from being used for fishing on the common fishing grounds of the country under its control;
prohibitions on the use of EU vessels or equipment.
suspension of fishing vessels from operating on the stock under the country’s control.
EU countries must notify the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for the identification of that country as a country allowing sustainable fishing and describe the possible measures that could be taken in relation to it pursuant to this regulation.
Implementing acts
The Commission must adopt implementing acts determining whether the conditions laid down in the regulation have been complied with and, where necessary, providing that the measures adopted by the country concerned cease to apply. These implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 8(2) of the regulation and must provide for an appropriate system for their enforcement by competent authorities.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the EU's website.

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements between the EU and non-EU countries (until 9 January 2013)
Bilateral investments in non-European Union (EU) countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001 — EU-non-EU bilateral investment agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions under which EU countries are empowered to amend or conclude bilateral investments agreements with non-euro area countries.
It aims to ensure that these agreements remain in force while respecting the EU’s exclusive competence.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are required to notify the European Commission of any bilateral investment agreement they conclude with a non-Euro area country. The notification must include a copy of those agreements.
The EU country concerned must notify the Commission of the conclusion and entry into force of the agreement, and of any subsequent changes to the status of that agreement.
If the Commission finds that the agreement does not fulfil the requirements of Article 9(1) and (2) of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU), it must authorise the EU country to sign and conclude such an agreement. If the Commission does not grant an authorisation, it must inform the country concerned thereof and state the reasons therefor.
An advisory committee is set up to assist the Commission in the implementation of the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has exclusive competence with respect to the common commercial policy.
For more information, see:
Investment agreements (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bilateral investment agreement: an investment agreement between an EU country and a non euro area country on the basis of which the EU acquires a right to invest in that country.
* Non-EU country: a country which is not a member of the European Economic Area (EEA).
* EU country: any EU country which does not participate in the EEA.

Summary 103:
Tariff preferences for sustainable development and good governance in the EU (SDFG)
Tariffs preferences for Sustainable development and Good Governance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 on the general arrangement for the common commercial policy in the field of development cooperation for developing countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions under which the European Union (EU) grants tariff preferences to developing countries which share a common developing need and are in a similar stage of economic development.
The general arrangement is part of the EU’s Common commercial policy (CPD), which aims to support the development of the world economy and the sustainable development of developing countries.
KEY POINTS
Beneficiaries
Eligible countries are those countries which fulfil the eligibility criteria for the special incentive arrangement (SAP), which provides the EU with preferential market access for certain goods and services.
SAP countries must meet the following criteria:
have a per capita income of at least 60% of the average level of gross domestic product (GDP) of the countries covered by the SAP;
have an annual GDP of more than 60 %;
meet the minimum requirements for the protection of human rights, labour rights, environmental protection, good governance and their effective implementation, by examining the conclusions and recommendations of the relevant monitoring bodies established under those conventions.
A list of eligible countries is established in Annex I of the regulation.
Beneficiary countries may withdraw from the SFP if they do not meet the criteria for graduation over 3 consecutive years. The Commission must notify an eligible country concerned of any relevant changes in its status under the scheme.
Implementation of the scheme
The SFP is implemented by the European Commission, which adopts implementing acts. These include:
Implementing acts on tariff preferences for non-sensitive products and for sensitive products;
implementing measures to ensure a satisfactory utilisation rate while at the same time taking account of the situation of the corresponding EU industries.
Amendments to the regulation
The regulation has been amended several times, most recently by Regulation (EC) 2018/852 on the implementation of the common customs code (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 20 November 2008.

Summary 104:
Export authorisation and import and transit measures for firearms, their parts and essential components and ammunition (United Nations Firearms Protocol)
Export and importation of firearms and their parts
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2009 — export authorisation for firearms and certain parts and accessories for firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the export and import of certain firearms and related parts for the purpose of implementing the United Nations Protocol against the Illicit Manufacturing of and Trafficking in Firearms, their Parts and Components and Ammunition (the ‘UN Firearms Protocol’).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
firearms manufactured after 1899 and after the date of entry into force of the UN Firearms Protocol (3 July 2005);
firearm parts manufactured before 1899 and manufactured after the year 1899;
firing arms manufactured after 1894 and manufactured before the year 1900;
deactivated firearms;
antique firearms and firearms manufactured before 1894.
Authorisation
EU countries must grant authorisation to the exporter of firearms or their parts or accessories for export to the relevant EU country. The exporter is the person who has the power for determining the sending of the item out of the customs territory of the EU. If no export contract has been concluded or if the holder of the contract does not act on its own behalf, the exporters must mean the person with the power to send the item.
The exporter must ensure that:
the item has been authorised in the EU country in which it is being exported;
the exporter has the necessary to carry out the export;
it is accompanied by a certificate of authorisation issued by the competent authority of that EU country;
there is no obligation to inform the customs authority of the country of origin of the export or import of the firearm or its parts.
Exporters must notify the customs authorities of the items they intend to export and the reasons for their export.
Imports and transit of firearms are subject to authorisation. Exporters may refuse to export a firearm or a component if they consider it to be a threat to public security, public order or public health, or if it is contrary to the public policy of an EU country, or to the interests of the public or the security of a person or organisation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    дарганска барка  грациальна года  хародна 
It has applied since 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  б  балкабраги

‘Firearms’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 105:
EUROSUR — EU countries’ cooperation with the European Union Agency for the Protection of the Internal Border and Coast Guard (EUROSSUR)
EurosUR — European Union agency for the protection of the internal border and coast guard (EurOSUR)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 on the EU’s external borders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union (EU) external border surveillance system for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must establish national coordination centres to improve the exchange of information and the cooperation for border surveillance between them and the European External Action Service, the European Agency for Safety and Security at the External Border and Co-ordination of the Member States’ external borders.
Each EU country must designate, operate and maintain a national coordination centre which will coordinate, and exchange information among, all authorities with a responsibility for external border border surveillance at national level, as well as with the other EU countries and the Agency.
National coordination centres must provide the Agency, via the communication network, with information from their national situational pictures which is required for the establishment and maintenance of the European situational picture and of the common pre-frontier intelligence picture.
The coordination centres shall store sensitive and classified information in a secure manner and near-real-time with, in accordance with the standards, and store it in their national data centres.
in accordance with their national procedures.
Coordination centres must be able to:
coordinate operational measures with each other and with the Agency;
share operational information with other countries, without prejudice to the competences of the Agency and of EU countries.
They must also:
cooperate in the field of border control and border surveillance with other EU and non-EU countries;
provide the Agency with the situational awareness and reaction capability it needs to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration;
ensure that EU countries cooperate in the fight against transnational organised crime.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLYORKER DOCUMENT AND THE DECISION APPLACE?
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
EURosUR is the EU's external border control system. It is designed to provide EU countries with the infrastructure and tools needed to improve their situational awareness, reaction capability at the external borders of the EU (‘external borders’). It is also designed to contribute to the prevention and combatting of illegal immigration.
For more information, see:
External border control (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External border surveillance: the surveillance of external borders by EU countries of the one part of the Schengen area, the other part of which is the Mediterranean region.
* Transnational organized crime: transnational criminal activity that takes place outside the territory of an EU country.

Summary 107:
Protection of designations of origin and geographical indications for agricultural products and foodstuffs (Regulation (EC) No 882/2004)
Protection OF designations OF ORIGIN AND GARESE
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — protecting designations* of origin, geographical indications and traditional specialities guaranteed
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to protect designations (designations* or geographical indications) of origin* and specialities (traditional specialities) guaranteed* in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Designations are designed to recognise the quality of products and their model of production as a factor that adds value to consumers’ health and well-being.
The regulation applies to all products produced in the EU by farmers and producers who have kept traditions alive while taking into account the developments of new production methods and material.
Protected designations must be registered on the European Commission’s register of protected designations and protected geographical indications.
Traditional specialities are protected under the regulation and are automatically included in the register.
National authorities must ensure that names of genuine traditional products are registered under the scheme, in particular those concerning the definition of ‘traditional’, which should cover products that have been produced for a significant period of time.
Registered names may be refused or, if registered, invalidated on the grounds that they conflict with a prior designation of origin or a geographical indication.
Registers must be established to ensure that the protected names are used fairly and in order to prevent practices liable to mislead consumers and to prevent the misuse, imitation and evocation of the registered names on goods as well as on services.
EU countries must ensure the protection of protected names is extended to the use of the protected name on goods and services and that the registered name is protected against counterfeiting and counterfeiting.
A register of registered names is set up for protected names. The register must contain information on:
the names of protected products;
the registered name of protected specialities;
procedures for registering protected names;
a list of the national authorities responsible for enforcing the regulation;
rules for the registration and enforcement procedures;
measures to be taken by national authorities in the event of counterfeiting or counterfeiting of protected name or protected geographical indication names.
An EU country may grant transitional protection at national level without affecting intra-EU or international trade.
Directive 2000/13/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 March 2000 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the labelling, presentation and advertising of food and agricultural products, and in particular the provisions aimed at preventing labelling that may confuse or mislead consumers, lays down the general rules on labelling of agricultural products.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2018/853 has applied since 1 July 2018 and had to become law in the Member State concerned by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Protecting designations’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Protected designation: an EU symbol or geographical indication that indicates that a product is of a particular origin or geographical origin.
* Speciality: a product that is guaranteed to be of a certain quality by virtue of its geographical or cultural origin or by its geographical characteristics, or by a combination of the characteristics of the product and the geographical origin of the place where it is produced.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017 / 852 of 20 July 2017 on protecting designs of origin (OJ L 183, 1.8.2017, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 184/2017 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 108:
Single-hull oil tankers — accelerated phasing-in of the double hull requirements of MARPOL (European Maritime Safety Agency) and the ban on the transport to or from EU ports of heavy grade oil in single-Hull oil tanker
Single-hwulled oil tanker (SOHO)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — the application of Regulation 20 of the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) on the safety requirements for the construction of double hulls and the prohibition of the transport of heavy-grade oil in SOHO
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all oil tanker operating in EU waters comply with the requirements of the EU’s common maritime safety policy (MARPOL).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to SOHOs of 600 tonnes deadweight and above.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 852/97 which had been in force since 1 January 2000 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2002.
The new regulation applies from 1 January 2019.
EU countries must ensure that:
all oil tanker above 15 years of age, flying the flag of an EU country, is equipped with a double hull or equivalent design;
the vessel is inspected at least once a year by the European Commission and the European Maritime Safety Authority (EMSA);
the ship is inspected by the EMSA at least twice a year.
If an oil tanker does not comply with these requirements, the EU country in which it is based must inform the IMO of its decision to deny entry into its jurisdiction to the oil tanker.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 February 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 February 2020.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce the risk of accidental oil pollution in European waters.
For more information, see:
‘Single-HULLed Oil Tankers’ on the EEA's website.

Summary 109:
European System of Financial Supervision (ESFS)
European system of financial supervision (EFSF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 on the European System of financial supervision
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a single European system of supervisory supervision for all financial institutions in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The ESFS is an integrated network of national and EU supervisory authorities, leaving day-to-day supervision to the national level.
It aims to improve the functioning of the internal market by ensuring a high, effective and consistent level of regulation and supervision taking account of the varying interests of all EU countries and the different nature of financial institutions.
The regulation sets out the role of the European Financial Supervisory Authority (EFSA).
The EFSA’s main tasks are to:
assist competent national authorities in the consistent interpretation and application of EU rules and contributing to financial stability necessary for financial integration;
ensure that the ESFS’ rules are compatible with the objectives of the EU acquis concerning the establishment of a stable and single EU financial market for financial services;
provide advice to the European Banking Authority (EBA), the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Investment Bank (EIB).
It also has the power to investigate alleged incorrect or insufficient application of Union law obligations by national authorities, concluded by a recommendation.
In addition, the EFSA can:
require national authorities to take specific action to remedy an emergency situation;
impose sanctions on financial institutions if they fail to comply with EU rules;
take corrective action if a national authority does not comply with the formal opinion addressed to it and in which EU law is directly applicable to the financial institutions by virtue of existing or future EU regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    —
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND

For more information, see:
European financial system (European Commission).

Summary 110:
Mobility of labour within the EU: mobility of workers, employment and vocational training (EU mobility policy)
Mobilities of labour, employment, vocational training and mobility policy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 — mobility of labour between EU countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU citizens who wish to work in another EU country can do so without discrimination on grounds of nationality.
It lays down the conditions for the mobility of EU citizens working in other EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to nationals of all EU countries who are employed in the territory of an EU country.
Eligibility for employment
Any national of a EU country and any employer pursuing an activity in the EU may exchange their applications for and offers of employment, and may conclude and perform contracts of employment in accordance with the provisions in force laid down by law, regulation or administrative action, without any discrimination resulting therefrom.
The employment services of the EU countries must grant workers who are nationals of those EU countries the same priority as the relevant measures grant to nationals vis-à-vis workers from non-EU countries. The European Coordination Office (ECO) is responsible for:
coordinating practical measures necessary for vacancy clearance at EU level and for analysing the resulting vacancies;
analyzing the results of the resulting applications;
providing technical advice to the Advisory Committee and the Technical Committee on the implementation of the regulation;
assisting the Commission in implementing the regulation.
Nationalsatisfying vacancies
The European Commission must publish a list of the services covered by the regulation, drawn up by common accord, of services referred to in paragraph 1.
EU countries must notify each other of vacancies within a reasonable period, not exceeding 1 month.
Each EU country must send to the ECO any information concerning living and working conditions and the state of the labour market that is likely to be of guidance to workers from the other countries. This information must be sent to the specialist services of each EU country, and to the technical committee of the European Union (EU).
The ECO also sends to the Commission the list of services that are covered by this regulation and the Commission publishes such list for information in the Official Journal.
An Advisory Committee is set up to advise the European Commission and the Member States on the application of this regulation. It is composed of representatives of the relevant EU countries and is chaired by the Commission and comprises representatives from each country. It has the power to:
approach the Commission, the European Parliament and the Council;
make recommendations to the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs and the European Social Committee;
recommend the establishment of a European Commission Employment Advisory Committee.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2006.
BACKGROUND
The mobility of work is one of the main means by which workers are guaranteed the possibility of improving their living conditions and promoting their social advancement, while helping to satisfy the requirements of the economies of EU countries, particularly where the latter aims at putting workers in a position to take up concrete offers of jobs from other regions of the Union.
For more information, see:
‘Mobility within the Union’ on the Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 550/2006 of 29 June 2006 on mobility of Labour within the Community (codified version) (OJ L 183, 1.7.2006, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Communication from the Commission to the Council on the conclusion of the Agreement on the Mobility of Labour between Member States (COM(2006) 552 final, 30.6.2006)

Summary 111:
Hydrochlorofluorocarbons — phase-out of the use of hydrochlorofluride and methyl bromide in the European Union (EU) (Regulation (EC) No 552/2008)
Hydrofluorocarbon (HFC)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
The regulation repeals and repeals Regulation (EEC) 98/8/EEC, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2008.
It aims to ensure that the EU’s obligations under the Montreal Protocol on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer are met.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The legislation applies to all products and equipment containing or relying on HFCs, as well as to the import and export of those products. It does not apply to:
products and equipment that are used for the maintenance or servicing of refrigeration and air conditioning equipment;
products or equipment used for essential laboratory and analytical uses;
parts and equipment used in the manufacture, manufacture and transport of chemicals, such as paints, solvents, paints and coatings;
electrical and electronic equipment that is used for electrical and electronic work.
Exemptions
The European Commission may grant exemptions to certain substances for certain essential laboratory or analytical uses. The exemption for critical uses of certain substances is suspended until the end of 2020.
The exemption for certain chemicals is suspended for a period of 5 years.
Export of HFC-containing products to non-EU countries is prohibited.
Imports of products or equipment that use HFCBs are prohibited until 2023.
EU countries must ensure that:
the export of products containing or using HCFCs is prohibited after the entry into force of a ban on the export of HCFC-related products or of controlled substances for their maintenance and servicing within the EU;
the import of products, equipment or of chemicals containing HCFC is prohibited for the period of 2023-2027.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Directive 2008/552/EC has applied since 1 January 2009.
Decision 2008/6/EC repeals the regulation and replaces it with Decision 2008/468/EC.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘HFCs’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* HFC: a fluorocarbon with a high greenhouse gas (GHG) potential. It is a naturally occurring substance that is a constituent of the ozone layer and has been banned since the 1970s.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 of 20 January 2008 recasting and repealing Council Regulation 98/9/EC of 16 December 2008 (OJ L 183, 20.1.2008, pp. 1-18)

Summary 112:
European Environment Agency (EEA) (European Commission)
European Environment Association (EESA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001 on the European Environment Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets up a European Union (EU) agency to collect, analyse and disseminate information on the state of the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EEA’s main tasks are to:
collect and analyse data on the environment;
provide the public with the necessary technical and scientific support;
coordinate the activities of the national environment information networks (NENs) in order to ensure the proper application of EU environmental legislation;
assist the European Commission in implementing EU environmental policy.
Activities
EEA activities are based on a number of principles, including:
to collect and analyse information on:
the present and foreseeable state of environmental conditions;
the quality of environmental resources;
environmental pressures on human health and the safety and security of human beings;
ensuring the protection of human health, the environment and the health of animals, plants and plants;
to promote sustainable development.
EASA activities are governed by a management board and an executive director. Each member of the management board has a vote. The Executive Director is assisted by a scientific committee, which delivers opinions on any scientific matter concerning the EEA and on any other scientific matter which the Management Board or the Executive Director may submit to it.

BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the EU environmental policies.

Summary 113:
Eco-management and audit scheme (EMAS) for organisations with an environmental impact in the EU
Eeco-Management and Audit scheme (EMO) for companies with an Environmental Impact
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the European Union’s environmental management and audit system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) EMO scheme for companies whose activities have an impact on the environment.
It aims to improve the environmental performance of companies across the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
EMAS is open to all organisations, in and outside the EU, whose activities affect the environment and whose environmental performance is assessed by an independent environmental auditor.
The regulation applies to all EU companies with a direct environmental aspect, including:
direct environmental aspects associated with activities, products and services of the organisation itself over which it has direct management control;
direct and indirect environmental aspects which are not directly related to the organisation's activities;
environmental policy and environmental management system;
the environmental aspects and impacts of the organisations’ activities and the objectives and targets for achieving these objectives and the deadlines for achieving them;
organisations’ environmental performance and compliance with applicable legal obligations relating to the environment as set out in Annex IV.
Reporting
Organisations must report on their environmental performance on a regular basis to the public and other interested parties. They must:
provide information on the environmental status of their organisation and its environmental performance;
report on the performance of their environmental management systems, including an objective and periodic evaluation of their performance of such systems, the provision of information on environmental performance, an open dialogue with the public, and the active involvement of employees in organisations and appropriate training.
They must also:
make publicly available their environmental statement and their updated environmental statement;
ensure that the information and data in an organisation's environmental statement are reliable, credible and correct and meet the requirements of this regulation;
make their environmental statements and updated environmental statements available for public inspection and verification.
EU countries must ensure that organisations comply with all applicable legal requirements relating to environmental protection and that they have a system in place to detect, prevent and investigate breaches of these requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
From 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
European Union EMO (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Environmental audit: a systematic, documented, periodic and objective evaluation of the environment performance of an organisation, management system and processes designed to protect the environment, using appropriate benchmarks and scales.
* Direct environmental aspect: an environmental aspect which is directly or indirectly associated with the activities or products or services of a company over which the company has direct control.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Summary 114:
European Union rules on the production, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks and on the protection of geographical indications of certain spirit drinks
European Union (EU) rules on both the production and presentation of spirits and their geographical indications
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 on the definition, presentation, labelling and marketing of spirits
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the production of spirits in the EU and on their labelling.
It aims to safeguard the reputation which Community spirit drinks have achieved in the European Union and the world market by continuing to take into account the traditional practices used in the making of these drinks as well as increased demand for consumer protection and information.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks produced in or exported from the EU for export. It does not apply to spirits produced on the territory of an EU country.
The rules apply to the following categories:
vodka;
spirits made from raw materials of agricultural origin other than cereals and/or potatoes;
spirits produced by means of traditional production methods other than the use of agricultural raw materials other than those listed in Annex I to the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (Annex II to the regulation);
sour spirits made from spirits other than vodka;
spice-based spirit drinks made by means other than that listed in Annex III to the Regulation;
cider-based spirits;
liquor-infused spirit drinks;
malt-influenced spirit drinks.
ciders and other spirits.
the regulation also applies to spirit drinks which meet the following requirements:
the spirit drinks must bear the geographical indication ‘Vodka’ or ‘Malt’ in the form of a geographical indication in the format set out in Annex II to this regulation;
the spirits must bear a description of the spirit drinks they are made from and must bear an indication of the geographical origin of the raw materials used where the vodka is made from other than grains and potatoes.
Labelling
Each of the categories listed in the regulation bears the following information:
an indication of geographical origin in the shape of a bar code;
a label with the words ‘Spice-Influenced’, ‘Liquor’ and ‘Spirits’ printed on the label.
Annexes II and III contain the terms ‘natural or nature-identical flavouring substances and preparations’ which may be used to describe the flavour of a spirit drink.
Directive 2008/13/EC sets out rules for the labelling, presentation or presentation of the spirits listed in this regulation. These rules are based on the European Commission’s definition of ‘spice’.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Spiced spirit: a spirit which is made of spirits, spirits, liqueurs or other spirit-based products.
* Vodka: the spirit of vodka, spirits or other spirits which are made of vodka.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) 2015/552 of 21 December 2015 on the regulation on the definitions, presentation of and marketing spirit drinks (OJ L 183, 28.12.2015, pp. 1-18)
last update 14.01.2016



Summary 115:
Common rules for safeguarding civil aviation — common basic standards on aviation security (EU rules on security of civil aviation)
Common rules on safeguarding aviation security
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — common rules for the implementation of common rules on civil aviation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common basic security standards for civil aviation in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
all airports or parts of airports located in the territory of an EU country that are not exclusively used for military purposes;
all operators, including air carriers, providing services at airports referred to in point (a);
all entities applying aviation security standards that operate from premises located inside or outside airport premises and provide goods and/or services to or through airports;
airside and adjacent terrain and buildings or portions thereof, access to which is restricted.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EC) No 2320/2002.
EU countries must draw up a national civil aviation security programme.
Each EU country has the competence to decide whether to deploy in-flight security officers on aircraft registered in that EU country and on flights of air carriers licensed by it as well as to ensure that such officers are specially selected and trained, taking into account the required security and safety aspects on board an aircraft.
In addition, each EU country must designate a single authority responsible for the security of passengers and air carriers flying within the EU.
Airport security procedures must be in line with the EU’s common security standards.
The European Commission may, on the basis of a request from the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA), which is responsible for aviation security in the EU, set up an aviation security committee to review the security requirements of the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
 			  	 	
The EU has adopted a number of aviation security rules since the entry into force of the Lisbon Treaty in 1999.
For more information, see:
‘Civil aviation security’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 116:
Targeted restrictive measures — human rights violations and abuses — EU countries’ obligations to protect human rights and international humanitarian law (CFSP 2020/1999)
Monitoring and freezing of funds and economic resources — targeted restrictive measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — EU-wide restrictive measures against persons, entities and bodies responsible for, providing support to or otherwise involved in serious human rights abuses or abuses
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
The EU’s restrictive measures aim to prevent, deter and punish individuals, entities or bodies who commit or are suspected of committing, or are involved in, human rights or humanitarian violations or abuses in the territory of the EU.
The regulation and the decision are part of a package of measures known as the ‘Treaty on European Union (TEU) measures’.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The measures apply to:
individuals, entities, bodies and bodies listed in the annex to the regulation, as well as those associated with them;
natural or legal persons or bodies listed by the annex;
those associated with those listed by points (a) and (b) of the regulation;
the EU countries and the European Commission.
EU countries must inform each other of the measures taken pursuant to this regulation and of other relevant information at their disposal in connection with this regulation. They must also inform the other EU countries of any authorisations granted under this regulation within 2 weeks of its entry into force.
Activities covered by the regulation and decision
The European Commission has the right to request information from the EU countries concerned on the individuals and bodies covered by this regulation, including:
the names and addresses of the individuals or bodies,
the nature and nature of the funds or economic resources frozen and
the reasons for the freezing and the refusal to make them available to those listed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE PROTOCOL APPLY?
Directive 2013/34/EU — the European Union adopted a series of measures to combat human rights abuse and violations of international human rights law (known as ‘treaty’) in the context of the European Pillar of Human Rights (see summary).
The directive applies from 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The TFEU is the cornerstone of EU policy to combat all forms of abuse of human rights in the EU and the world.
For more information, see:
‘The EU and its territories’ on the European External Action Service's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 117:
European Union military operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFOR MEDI 2020/472 - EU naval mission in Libya (EUVFOR Med IRINI)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020-472 on a European Union Military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE OPERATION?
The European Union (EU) is leading a military operation to combat the trafficking of arms and related materiel in the eastern Mediterranean and to disrupt the business model of human smuggling and trafficking networks in the area of the Mediterranean.
KEY POINTS
The EU’s mission is to:
contribute to the implementation of the UN arms embargo on Libya by using aerial, satellite and maritime assets;
support the Libyan Coast Guard and the Libyan Navy in their capacity building and training of the Libyan coast guard and navy;
combat the smuggling of human trafficking and human trafficking networks through information gathering and patrolling carried out by aerial assets above the high seas, in the agreed Area of Operation and in the Area of Interest.
EUNAFOR MEDINI is responsible for:
collecting extensive and comprehensive information on the trafficking in arms and associated materiel from all directions and sharing it with relevant partners and agencies on a case-by-case and need-to-know basis through the mechanisms in the relevant planning documents in order to contribute to a full situational awareness of the operation and to the prevention and disruption of the trafficking networks;
contributing to the fight against the smuggling and human smuggling networks by:
supporting the detection and monitoring of human smugglers and trafficking in human beings;
providing technical assistance to the Libyan authorities, including through the provision of technical assistance;
ensuring the protection of the lives of Libyan nationals and their families;
disrupting the business models of trafficking in persons, goods and services;
carrying out inspections to detect and prevent the illicit export of arms from Libya.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 31 March 2020 and had to become law in the EU countries by 31 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The decision and the operation are part of the EU-led mission to support the efforts of the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY) to restore democracy and human rights in the region.
For more information, see:
European Union missions in the Eastern Mediterranean (European External Action Service).

Summary 118:
Facilitating the exchange of information on authentic and false documents and on known methods of falsification at the external borders (FADO) (European Union Agency for the Protection of the Rights of Passengers, Refugees and Citizenship)
Facilitating cooperation between the European Union (EU) and non-EU countries in the fight against document fraud
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, 2019/1896 — FADO system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the Facility for the Exchange of Information on Authentic Documents and on Known Methods of Falsification* at the External Borders (FadO) system.
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities in order to detect and combat document fraud.
KEY POINTS
The European Union’s (EU), EU countries, the European Commission and the European External Action Service (EEAS) are responsible for the operation of the FadO system. The European Union Agency (EAS) is responsible for taking over and operating the system. It is assisted by the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).
EU countries are obliged to provide the EAS with information on the authentic documents which they issue, including on the security features of such documents, and on the false versions of these documents in their possession.
The EAS is assisted in the detection of false documents.
EAS has the responsibility for the implementation of the system and for the management of the information provided by EU countries.
In addition, EAS has a role in the field of fraud prevention and detection.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  
The EU has set up a system to facilitate cooperation between national authorities to detect document fraud at external borders.
For more information, see:
‘Faced with document fraud’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official document: an official document issued by an EU country.
Authentic document: a document which is authentic and which is falsified.
Falsification: the falsification of a document by means of deception or other means.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU/2019/1898 of 21 December 2019 on the establishment of the Facility of Information and Documentation at the Border (Faced With Document Fraud) (OJ L 183, 28.12.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 119:
Access to regulated professions and the pursuit of their activities
Assessment of the proportionality of the requirements restricting access to a regulated profession or the pursuit thereof
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/29/EU — assessment of the proportions of requirements restricting the access to, or pursuit of, regulated professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that European Union (EU) citizens have the right to access regulated professions.
It lays down the principles of non-discrimination and proportionality.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU citizens who wish to work in, or are interested in working in, a regulated or non-regulated profession. It does not apply to those who are already employed or self-employed.
EU countries must ensure that the requirements are justified by public interest objectives, such as those within the meaning of the TFEU, namely public policy, public security and public health, or by overriding reasons in the public interest, recognised as such in the case-law of the Court of Justice of the European Union.
National authorities must carry out proportionality assessments in an objective and independent manner, including where a profession is regulated indirectly by giving a particular professional body the power to regulate.
The assessment must be based on developments found to have occurred in the area of the regulated profession since the measure was adopted. It must also take into account the temporary or occasional nature of the service.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  —
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2013 — access to the regulated professions (see summary) (European Commission)
KEY TERMS
* Regulated profession: a profession which is regulated by an EU country to ensure the protection of human health and the safety of the environment.
* Non-professional organisation: a professional organisation or body which is responsible for organising, supervising or supervising professional education and training.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Directive 2014/30/EU of 21 December 2014 on the assessment of proportionality and the application of the principle of freedom to choose an occupation and the freedom to conduct a business

Summary 120:
Limiting fishing in EU waters of the North Sea — multiannual management plans for demersal stocks and their fisheries in the north sea
Limiting flying fish in the northern sea — multi-year management plans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — multannual management plan for the management of fish stocks in the North sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the implementation of the EU’s common fisheries policy (CFP), which aims to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time possible, at least to levels that can produce sustainable levels of marine life (MSY).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all stocks of species to which the landing obligation applies under Article 15 of the CFP.
It applies to the following:
fisheries covered by the EU fisheries policy, i.e. those that are open to fishing in the EU waters in which the EU has fishing rights (e.g. in the Baltic Sea, the Black Sea and the Atlantic Ocean);
fishing rights for the fishing of demersals (those fish that are not covered by EU fishing rights) in the European Union waters of which the European Commission has jurisdiction;
the fishing rights of the fishing vessels of the European Economic Area (EEA).
It does not apply to fishing rights for non-EU fishing vessels.
Fishing rights
The European Commission must ensure that fishing rights are based on the best interests of the fish stocks covered by this regulation.
 and that fishing opportunities are proportionate and proportionate.
the European Commission may adopt delegated acts to adjust the geographical distribution of the stocks set out in the plan if the scientific advice provided by the International Commission for the Conservation of Marine Living Resources (ICES) indicates a change in the distribution of stocks.
Limitations
The EU has to ensure that fish stocks are managed in such a way as to:
reproduce MSY (maximum sustainable yield of fish) at levels that are sustainable for the long-term survival of the species;
ensure that the fishing opportunities provided for in the management plan are not disproportionate to the size of the stock;
restore the MSY of the relevant stocks;
protect the marine environment and the health of the marine ecosystem;
promote a fair standard of living for those who depend on fishing activities, bearing in mind coastal fisheries and socioeconomic aspects;
implement the ecosystem-based approach to fisheries management.
EU countries must also ensure that:
Fisheries are managed according to the best available scientific and technical advice;
Fishers are not allowed to take more than 10% of the total catch of fish in a given year;
fishers are prohibited from taking more than 20% of total fish in any given year.
In addition, EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure the sustainable management of fishing rights.
The management plan must include a list of stocks and fishing opportunities for each of the fisheries covered by it. The plan must be updated every 5 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 6 August 2019 and had to become law in EU countries by 6 August 2023.
BACKGROUND
The CFP is the framework for the EU's fisheries policy.
For more information, see:
Common Fisheries Policy (European Commission)
European Commission (European Fisheries Agency).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 752/2019 of 7 July 2019 laying down the multiannually managed fish stocks and the fishing rights in the Northern Sea of the Union (OJ L 183, 6.8.2019, pp. 1-18)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 752 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 121:
Fishing for straddling fish stocks in the South-East Pacific Free-Range Fisheries Organisation (SPRFMO)
Fishing to straddle fish stocks
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — fishing for stradling fish species in the south-east Pacific free-range fisheries Organisation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the fishing of bottom fishing species by EU vessels operating in the southern-east pacific free-ranging fisheries organisation (SPFMO).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
EU fishing vessels operating within the SPRFMO Convention Area;
third country fishing vessels transhipping fishery products harvested in the area;
non-EU vessels that are the subject of an inspection in, or being the object of an EU port and carrying fishing products harvested within the area.
EU vessels using demersal longlines must use line weights and tori (bird scaring) lines.
All EU fishing vessels must:
not discharge offal during shooting and hauling;
not use gillnets;
use gill nets, which is more than 2,5 kilometres in length the purpose of which is to enmesh, entrap or entangle fish by drifting on the surface or in the water;
avoid using bottom gears in the normal course of fishing operations, including reefs, seamounts, hydrothermal vents, cold water corals or cold water sponge beds.
National authorities must report to the Commission the following data regarding jack mackerel fisheries:
by the 15th day of each month, the list of fishing vessels flying their flag engaged in transhipment in the preceding month;
the number of fish caught in the previous year.
The Commission must also:
report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the impact assessment carried out by the Commission;
report annually to the Council and the European Commission on the results of the Commission’s impact assessment;
publish an annual report on bottom fishing activities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The SPRFO is a non-governmental organisation established by the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea, which contains principles and rules with regard to the conservation and management of the living resources of the sea. It is responsible for the long-term conservation and sustainable use of fishery resources through the application of the precautionary approach to fisheries management and an ecosystem-based approach.
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 852/2017 of 21 July 2017 laying down rules relating to fishing for bottom fishing stocks and repealing Council Decision 98/392/EC

Summary 122:
Controls on cross-border movements of cash in the EU (Regulation (EU) 2017/1939) (see summary) (See summary)
The European Union has adopted a series of rules to combat money laundering and terrorist financing. These rules aim to prevent, detect and investigate these activities.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1882/2017 of 26 February 2017 on the control of cash movements in the European Union (EU).
SUMMARY
The aim of the regulation is to ensure that cash movements for illicit purposes do not undermine the functioning of the EU’s internal market.
The regulation lays down a system of rules that, in addition to contributing to the prevention of money laundering, and especially predicate offences such as tax crimes as defined in national law, and terrorist funding as such, facilitate the prevention, detection, and investigation of the criminal activities defined in Directive 2015/849.
This regulation applies to natural persons entering or leaving the EU.
It does not apply to:
cash couriers;
cash handlers;
travel agents;
passengers and freight crossing the external borders;
any unaccompanied consignment or receptacle crossing that border which may contain cash, or a means of transport carrying them.
EU countries must ensure that:
the competent authorities of EU countries collect the declarations and perform controls under this regulation;
the relevant national authorities carry out all the necessary controls on persons, their luggage, the means of transporting them and any unaccompanied consignments or receptacles crossing the EU borders.
Where the competent authorities register a failure to declare or disclose cash or where there are indications of criminal activity, they should promptly share that information with competent authorities in other EU countries through appropriate channels.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that persons who fail to disclose cash are accompanied by a statement of reasons and must adequately describe the specific factors that have given rise to the action.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts must be in line with the principles of the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and the Financial Action Task Force (FATF).
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 27 February 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 27 February 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has introduced a number of measures to fight money laundering. Among them are:
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2015/884 on the prevention and detection of money-laundering, terrorist financing and the financing of terrorist activities (see overview);
Regulation(EU) 2016/794 on controls on the movement of cash within the EU and on the exchange of information between EU countries on cash movements;
Commission Directive 91/308/EEC on the introduction of measures and obligations on financial institutions, legal persons and certain professions as regards transparency and record-keeping, as well as ‘know-your-customer’ provisions.
For more information, see:
‘The fight against money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Council Regulations (EC, EFTA and Euratom).
27.3.2017
-
OJ L 263 of 27.3-2017.

Summary 123:
Integrated national energy and climate plans for the EU and its Member States (Regulation (EC) No 517/2014)
Integrated National energy and carbon plans for EU and their Member States
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — integrated national energy & climate plans
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s objectives and targets for energy security, energy efficiency, decarbonisation, renewable energy and energy interconnections.
It aims to ensure that the EU is a resilient energy union with an ambitious climate policy at its core.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU Member States. It covers five dimensions:
energy security;
the internal energy market;
energy efficiency;
decarbonisation;
research, innovation and competitiveness.
The objectives of the energy union are to give EU consumers, including households and businesses, secure, sustainable, competitive and affordable energy, and to foster research and innovation by means of attracting investment, which requires a fundamental transformation of Europe's energy system.
EU countries are required to:
ensure that energy security is achieved through a combination of EU initiatives and coherent national policies set out in integrated national policies;
encourage investment in energy efficiency and renewable energy;
increase the share of renewable energy consumed in the EU by at least 30% by 2020;
improve the efficiency of the EU energy supply chain;
reduce greenhouse gas (GHGWholesale energy consumption by 20% by 2030;
20% by 2050;
by 2023 by 20%;
by 2030 by 2028 by 2035 by 2040 by 20%), by 2030 by 20%, by 2037 by 2042 by 2045 by 2046 by 2050 by 2060 by 2065 by 2070;
achieve a 40% domestic reduction in GHG emissions by 2030 compared to 1990 by 2030.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
the EU has a balanced balance between anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions by sources and removals by sinks as early as possible and, as appropriate, achieve negative emissions thereafter;
there is a gradual shift towards a sustainable low-carbon economy.
Each year, the Commission publishes a report on the progress made by the Member States towards achieving the objectives and the implications of those policies on the remaining global and EU carbon budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  The energy union is one of the pillars of the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘The Energy Union’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 124:
EU rules on the manufacture, placing on the market and use of perfluorocarbons (POPs) and their metabolites (PFCs) (Regulation (EU) 2021/852) (see summary).
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 528/2012 on the EU’s obligations under the Protocol and the Convention on the Elimination of Pollutants by Chemicals (POCs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU has a common legal framework within which to take measures designed, in particular, to eliminate the manufacturing, placing onto the market or use of intentionally manufactured POPs.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all substances listed in Annex I of the regulation. It does not apply to substances that are already on the European Union (EU), the European Economic Area (EEA), the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) or the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP).
EU countries must notify the European Commission and the European Agency for the Prevention of Chemical Pollution (EAP) of the presence of POPs in the environment. They must also notify the EAP and the Agency for Research on the Effects of Chemicals on the Human, Animal and Biological Diversity (RAPD) of any POPs found in their territory.
The notification must include the following information:
the name of the facility or site where the substance was manufactured or used;
the location of the manufacturing or use site and the total amount of the POPs released into the environment;
a description of the method of manufacture or use;
details of the site and of the substance used.
National authorities must ensure that POPs are identified and reduced as soon as possible, with the ultimate aim of eliminating POPs where feasible.
Implementation
Each EU country must draw up and implement an implementation plan for the regulation by 16 July 2021. It must include:
a list of the substances covered by the regulation;
measures to be taken by EU countries to ensure their compliance with the regulation and to monitor their implementation;
information to be provided to the Commission and to the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of this regulation; and
a system of monitoring and reporting to the public.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation has applied since 16 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The POPs Directive (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* POPs: a group of organic solvents that have the ability to emit small amounts of volatile organic compounds (VOCs).
* PFCs: an organic substance with the potential to release small quantities of VOCs into the air and water.
* COVID-19: a global network of laboratories and research institutes that aims to reduce VOC emissions and to improve the environmental protection of human health and the environment worldwide.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Regulating (EC, Euratom) No 529/2012 of the European Central Bank and the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States of the Union, meeting within the Council of 16 July 2012 on the common framework for the enforcement of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) (OJ L 183, 16.7.2012, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, EEC, L 183 of 16.6.2012 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products — producer responsibility and waste management (EU directive on single-use plastics and plastic products) (European Union directive on plastic products and plastic waste management)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/36/EU on the production and use of plastic and on waste management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce the amount of plastic waste generated in the European Union (EU) by reducing the quantity of plastic products that can be thrown away or disposed of.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all plastic products intended for short-lived applications, which are not designed for re-use or cost-effective recycling. It does not apply to food containers or fast-food containers or meal, sandwich, wrap and salad boxes with cold or hot food, or food containers of fresh or processed food that does not need further preparation, such as fruits, vegetables or desserts.
It applies to plastic containers and lids made of plastic.
Labelling requirements
Plastic containers must bear the following information:
the number of trips or rotations by which they can be refilled or re-used for the same purpose for which they are conceived;
the total number of times the product can be reused or recycled.
The packaging must be clearly visible on the packaging.
Plastics manufactured with modified natural polymers, or plastics manufactured from bio-based, fossil or synthetic starting substances are not naturally occurring and must therefore be addressed by this directive.
Producers must ensure that their plastic products meet the following requirements:
they must:
have a design that minimises the plasticity of the product;
have the ability to be reused and recycled;
make the packaging reusable and reusable;
keep the packaging in a state of good condition for at least 10 years.
They must also ensure that the packaging is reusable and recyclable.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle plastic waste. These include:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on plastic packaging (see summary);
EU legislation on plastic waste (Directive 2008/98/EC) on the producer responsibility for plastic waste and on the waste management of plastics and other plastic products (Directives 2000/59/EC, 2000/60/EC and 2008/56/EC);
Regulations (EC, EFTA and EFTA) on plastic-related waste management and on microplastics and micro-plastics;
EU rules on the disposal of plastic items in landfills and on their disposal in the sewer system (directive 2013/13/EU).
For more information, see the European Commission’s website on plastic and microplastic waste management.

Summary 126:
EU trade mark protection — protection of goods and services from counterfeiting and other infringements of EU trade marks (Regulation (EU) No 608/2013 on trade marks
European Union trade mark law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the protection of trade marks in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It sets out rules for trade marks to be granted by the European Commission and the European Trade Marks Office (ETMO).
It also sets out the conditions for the establishment of the EU Office for Trade Marks, which is responsible for administering the EU trade mark system.
EU trade marks are protected by a set of rules, including:
the right of the proprietor of a trade mark to use the trade mark in a manner which is consistent with the special character of EU law;
the exclusive rights conferred on the proprietors of EU marks;
rules for the designation and classification of goods or services for which trade marks apply for protection;
a procedure for the application for protection by the applicant to determine the extent of the protection applied for.
The EU Office is independent of the national trade mark office and is independent from the EU countries. It is funded by the EU budget and has the power to grant or reject applications for protection.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission published a report on the implementation of the regulation in December 2013.

Trade mark protection (European Agency for the Protection of Trade Marks and Trademarks).

Summary 127:
Access to published works for persons who are blind, visually impaired, or otherwise Print Disabled
Access to Published Works for persons with disabilities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 — Accessible format copies of books, including e-books, journals, newspapers, magazines and other kinds of writing, notation, including sheet music, and other printed material, including in audio form, whether digital or analogue, which have been made in any EU country in accordance with the national provisions adopted pursuant to Directive (EU, 2017/1364 (see summary)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that people with disabilities have access to printed works in a way that would be normally acceptable for them.
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright and related rights for the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain works and other protected subject matter, and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
accessible format copies made in an EU country for the benefit of beneficiary persons or authorised entities that provide the same services to beneficiary persons as one of its primary activities, institutional obligations or as part of its public-interest missions.
Authorised entities may distribute, communicate or make available these accessible format copies to beneficiaries or to an authorised person or authorised entity that provides these services for the same purposes as an authorised entity.
EU countries must ensure that authorised entities provide the following information in an accessible way, on request, to beneficiary personal data:
the name and contact details of the beneficiary person and authorised entity;
the date and place of entry into the EU of the accessible format copy of the book or the authorised entity’s contact details;
a description of the content of the printed work and the authorisation of the accession of the authorised entities.
Exceptions or limitations
An authorised entity established in a EU country carrying out the acts referred to in Articles 3 and 4 of the regulation may provide information in a non-discriminatory way to the beneficiaries of the accessibility of printed works, including the name of the authorising entity, the contact details and the contact information of the person receiving the access to the printed works.
Accessibility of accessible formats of books
An EU country may only export, communicate, make available to a beneficiary person, authorised entity or public institution or non-profit organisation that provides the same service for beneficiary persons in the same way as it does for the beneficiary persons of the EU.
The EU country exporting, communicating, making available or making available an accessible format version of a printed work must:
provide the same conditions as those for the export and import of accessible format versions of the same printed material made in the EU under the directive on the protection of intellectual property rights (Directive 2001/29/EC) and the directive repealing Directive 95/38/EC (Directives 2001/37/EC and 2001/39/EC);
ensure that the EU country that exports, communicates, makes available or makes available the same access conditions as the country in which it is exporting or importing the same accessibility format copies under the EU directive on access to cultural materials.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 11 October 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 11 October 2023.
BACKGROUND
The Marrakesh Treaty on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities is the cornerstone of the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Accessible formats for books’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Authorised entity: an organisation or person responsible for the provision of services or goods to a person who is blind, visually impaired or otherwise print disabled.

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices — EU rules for their manufacture, testing and use
In vitro diagnostics — EU controls for their manufacturing, testing & use
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — rules for the placing on the market and putting into service in the EU the in vitro diagnostic and related medical devices and their accessories
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to ensure the smooth functioning of the EU’s internal market as regards in vitro medical devices, taking as a base a high level of protection of health for patients and users, and taking into account the small and medium-sized enterprises that are active in this sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all medical devices emitting ionizing radiation (i.e. ionising radiation) and related products for research and clinical use. It does not apply to medical devices for research use only.
Manufacturers must ensure that their devices comply with the general safety and performance requirements and other legal requirements, such as those relating to quality and risk management, laid down in this regulation. They must also ensure that they have the necessary documentation to demonstrate that they comply with these requirements.
EU countries must establish a European database on medical devices (Eudamed) to collate and process information regarding devices on the EU market and the relevant economic operators, certain aspects of conformity assessment, notified bodies, certificates, performance studies, vigilance and market surveillance.
Importers, importers, distributors and distributors are responsible for ensuring that the devices are placed on the European Union market and that they put into service are in accordance with their intended purpose.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of Eudamed.
In addition, EU countries must ensure the safety and quality of the devices on sale and the monitoring of their use by EU countries’ competent authorities.
Disputes between manufacturers and their authorised representatives are handled by the competent authorities of EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
