Summary 130:
Ensuring the safety of appliances and fittings in the EU — new approach to technical harmonisation and standards (new approach principles) (European Union directive) (EU directive 2009/142/EC)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — new approaches to technical standards for appliances and fitting in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the technical requirements for the sale and putting into service of all types of energy-related appliances in the Member States.
KEY POINTS
Scope

electromagnetic compatibility (EMC) appliances, whether new or second-hand, imported from a non-EU country, and appliances or fittings made by a manufacturer established in the Union or imported from an EU country.

it bears the CE marking and bears a reference number (reference number) indicating that it meets the requirements of this directive.

they must notify the competent national authorities and notify the European Commission of any doubts about the appliance’s conformity before putting it on the EU market.

Summary 131:
Cross-border transport of euro cash by road: minimum protection rules for cash-in-transit (CIT) companies
Cross - border transport of euros by road
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009 — minimum protection requirements for cross-border CIT companies operating in the euro area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum protection standards for the professional cross-Border transport of cash* by road by CIT operators in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all CIT-licensed companies operating across the EU.
It does not apply to:
cash handlers, such as cash handlers employed by banks, the large retail sector and other professional cash handlers;
custodians of cash;
transport security staff;
drivers of CIT vehicles;
cash-handling staff.
Requirements for a CIT licence
CIT companies must apply for a licence from the competent authority of the EU country in which they are established.
The licence must be valid for at least 7 days following the international carriage of cash from one EU country to another.
A CIT vehicle must carry out at least 3 cabotage operations in the host EU country during that 7-day period. The number of operations that may be carried out within the host country is limited to three cabotages within 7 days.
Cit vehicles must be equipped with at least one armed security staff and at all times with a valid CIT driver’s’ licence and carry at least 1 weapon.
Cross-carrying CIT security staff must have at least 2 language skills and be able to communicate with the relevant national authorities in the relevant EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the CIT company has at least two language skills (A1 and B1) and at least a language proficiency level of at least A1 or B1 in the EU official languages, as set out in Annex VII of the regulation.
Minimum rates of pay
The minimum rate of pay must be guaranteed for the duration of the whole working day and these should not impose an unnecessary administrative burden on the operators.
An EU country may decide to reduce the minimum rates for CIT drivers’ pay if it considers that they are not able to carry out the activities covered by the regulation or that they do not meet the minimum standards.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* CIT: a company that transports euro cash between EU countries by road.
* Cross-border cash transport: the transport by road of cash to and from a central bank or CIT cash centre in another EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EU) No 1070/2009 of 21 December 2009 laying down minimum protection provisions for the crossing-border crossing of the euro by road (OJ L 183, 1.1.2011, pp. 1-18)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, No 1070) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 132:
Nutrition labelling — EU rules for food information for consumers (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Nutrition Labelling — European Union rules for foods for consumers
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 852/2018 on the provision of food information to consumers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down common rules for the EU’s food information law.
It aims to ensure that consumers are appropriately informed as regards the food they consume and to prevent any practices that may mislead the consumer.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all foods sold in the EU and to food business operators.
EU countries must ensure that all foods on sale in their territory bear a nutrition declaration on the packaging.
The declaration must contain the following information:
the amount of protein, carbohydrate, fat, carbohydrate and protein content of the food;
the composition of the foods;
a description of the ingredients and their composition;
an indication of the origin of the product.
Food business operators must provide the nutrition declaration in a language easily understood by consumers.
Labelling must be accompanied by a picture of the nutrition content of each food and a description of its composition. The declaration must also contain a statement of the amount of the protein and carbohydrate content, the carbohydrate content and the protein content.
Directive (EU countries may exempt from the mandatory nutrition declaration certain categories of foods that are unprocessed or for which nutrition information is not a determining factor for consumers’ purchasing decisions, or if the packaging is too small to accommodate the mandatory labelling requirements, unless the obligation to provide such information is provided for under other EU rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEVELATION APPLY?
From 1 July 2018.

WHAT DOES THIS REGULTRY TO DO?
The new regulation lays down rules on the nutrition information law for food for consumers. It aims to simplify and streamline EU rules on food information so as to ensure easier compliance and greater clarity for stakeholders and to modernise it in order to take account of new developments in the field of nutrition information.

Summary 133:
Issuing of euro coins and commemorative coins
Issuance of eurocoins and commemororative coins
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2011 — the issuing of euro coin and collector coins
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the issuing and redemption of coins in the euro area by EU countries.
It aims to ensure that euro coins are issued and put into circulation at face value in all EU countries whose currency is the euro.
KEY POINTS
EU countries may issue two types of coins:
circulation coins* and
collector coins* which are intended for collection that are not issued with a view to their entry into circulation.
Collector coins are legal tender only in the EU country of issuance and should not have a shaped edge with fine scallops or a ‘Spanish flower’ shape.
The EU countries may also issue 2-euro coins to celebrate specific subjects, subject to limits set per year and per issuing EU country for the number of issues of such coins.
Commission Regulation (EU) 2015/849 amends the regulation to allow EU countries to issue euro collector coins with a common design collectively issued by all the EU countries that have issued euro coins. The issuances of collector coins must be accounted for on an aggregated basis in the volume of coin issuance to be approved by the European Central Bank, rather than for each individual issue.
In order to avoid that euro circulation coins are destroyed by one EU country while there may be a need for such coins in another, EU country, EU countries must consult each other prior to the destruction of these coins and ensure that they are destroyed in accordance with the rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLACE INTO FORCE?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  —
For more information, see:
‘The euro’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Circulation coins: coins denominated in euro and cent and complying with the denominations and technical specifications laid down by the Council of the European Union (EU).
* Collector coins: euro coins that are intended to commemorate a specific subject as specified in Article 1h of Regulation (EC).
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 134:
European Social Fund (ESF) — missions and funding
European social fund (ESFs) — mission and funding	The European Social Fund aims to improve employment opportunities, strengthen social inclusion, fight poverty, promote education, skills and life-long learning and develop active, comprehensive and sustainable inclusion policies in order to contribute to economic, social and territorial cohesion in accordance with Article 162 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and Article 174 TFEU.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1081/2006 of 20 December 2006 establishing the missions of the EU’s European Social fund (ESAF) and the scope of its support, specific provisions and the types of expenditure eligible for assistance.
SUMMARY
The ESF is the EU's social fund and is responsible for providing financial support to EU countries to achieve the objectives set out in the EU strategy for smart growth (the ‘Europe 2020 strategy’).
The European Commission has delegated the tasks of the ESF to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) and to the Committee of the Regions.
The objectives of the ESAF are to:
improve employment opportunities;
strengthen social inclusion;
fight poverty;
promote education, training and lifelong learning;
provide active and comprehensive inclusion policies at national, regional and local levels;
contribute to the sustainable development of the labour market;
ensure the protection of human health and well-being.
support for the Youth Employment Initiative (YEI)
EEOI
The EEOI supports young people not in employment, education or training (NEET), who are unemployed or inactive, by providing them with a good-quality offer of either employment, continued education, an apprenticeship or a traineeship within a period of 4 months of becoming unemployed or of leaving formal education. The EEOIs supported by the ESI is open to all EU countries and regions, including the European Free Trade Association (EFTA), the European Investment Bank for Europe (EIBE), the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund (IMF). The EOEI supports NEET by:
providing them with access to employment, training, apprenticeships and traineeships;
encouraging the participation of NEET in the social and economic policies of EU countries;
supporting the implementation of the Youth Guarantee, which provides that young persons should receive an employment, continuing education, apprenticeship and a traineeship within a 4-month period if they become unemployed or have left formal education, or if they are inactive for more than 6 months after becoming unemployed.
In addition, the EEOi supports the development of a common set of output and result indicators to measure the impact of ESF funding on the EU and its Member States’ policies and programmes. These indicators are based on:
the number of jobs created and the number of unemployed and inactive NEET;
the average number of hours worked by NEET over the previous 12 months; and
the percentage of unemployed NEET who have been unemployed for at least 6 months.
EU countries and the regions are encouraged to leverage ESF financial instruments to support, for example, students, job creation, the mobility of workers, social inclusion and social entrepreneurship.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The European social fund’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Resolution (EU) 2018/852 of 21 December 2018 on the mission and scope of the Union's European social Fund (OJ L 183, 28.12.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 135:
European statistics on population and vital events (Estat) — common rules for the development, production and dissemination of European statistics on demographic and vital event data
European statistical systems on population
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — common legal framework for developing, producing and disseminating European statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a common framework for EU countries to provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the population of the EU and on vital events.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide Eurostat with the following data on their usually resident population at the reference time:
live births by sex, month of occurrence and live birth-order;
deaths by age, sex, year of birth, region of residence, country of birth and country of citizenship.
The data must be collected by EU countries’ national and European statistical authorities and by Eurostat using the European Statistics Code of Practice (ESDP).
The EU countries must report to Eurostat on the sources, definitions and estimation methods used for the collection of the data and on the use of the European Statistical System (ESSP) standards.
Each EU country must ensure that the data it sends to the Commission are:
scientifically-based and well-documented statistical estimation methods;
representative of the population at national level;
based on the data provided by the EU countries and Eurostat;
provided by the relevant national and regional statistical authorities;
processed and processed by the national statistical authorities in accordance with the European statistical system standards;
inform Eurostat of the sources and methods used to collect the data;
provide the Commission with the relevant data and metadata;
keep the Commission up-to-date with the data they have received.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
Annual demographic statistics are fundamental for the study and definition of a wide range of policies, with particular regard to social and economic issues.
For more information, see:
‘European statistics’ on Eurostat’s website
KEY TERMS
* EU country: an EU country is defined as one which has a population of at least 1 million people.
* Vital events: deaths, births, deaths, marriages, divorces and deaths of persons who are considered to be usual residents of a specific geographical area:
those who have lived in their place of usual residence for at least 12 months before the reference period; or
people who arrived in the EU for the first time with the intention of staying there for more than one year.

Summary 136:
Textile labelling and labelling of textile products — fibre composition of textile fibres and non-textile parts of animal origin (EU rules on the use of textile fibre names and related labelling, marking and analysis of fibre composition) (European Union rules on textile fibre names and the labelling or marking of fibres of non-animal origin)
Textile fibres — fibres (i.e. fibres originating from animals other than humans)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — textile fibre labelling
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for textile products with a new textile fibre name or marking to be used in the EU. It aims to improve the functioning of the EU’s internal market and to provide accurate information to consumers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all textile products consisting of two or more components. It does not apply to customised textile products made up by self-employed tailors.
Labelling
Textiles must bear the following information on the label:
the name of the fibre;
the fibre composition;
a description of the product;
an indication of the weight of the fibres;
how the fibre is treated and how it is separated from the other fibres.
The labelling must include a description of how the fibre has been treated, separated and separated from other fibrous materials and how the fibrous material has been separated.
Manufacturers must indicate that the new textile names are used only for the purpose of identifying the new fibres, and that they are not used for other purposes. They must also indicate that they have not been used for the manufacture of other textile products.
EU countries must ensure that manufacturers, or other persons acting on their behalf, include in the technical file to be submitted with their application available scientific information concerning possible allergic reactions or other adverse effects of the new fibre on human health, including results of tests conducted to that effect in compliance with relevant EU legislation.
National authorities must apply a single agreed allowance in doubtful cases. This allowance must not exceed 0.5 % of the total fibre content of the textile product and must not be exceeded by more than 0.05 %.
In the case of binary textile fibre mixtures for which there is no uniform method of analysis at EU level, the laboratory responsible for the test should be allowed to determine the composition of such mixtures, indicating in the analysis report the method used and its degree of accuracy.
If the laboratory determines that the fibre composition is not consistent, it must inform the manufacturer and the European Commission of the reason for this.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Textile fibre names’ on the EU Commission's website.

  — Textile fibre labelling (European Commission)

Summary 137:
The European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF) (European Union Fisheries Fund)
The European maritime and fisheries fund (EFMFF) is a European Union (EU) fisheries fund. Its main objective is to support sustainable fishing activities in the EU’s oceans, seas, coastal regions and maritime sectors.
ACT
Regulation (EC) No 1380/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 February 2013 establishing the European Maritime & Fisheries Fund.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The EMFF is the main EU fisheries fund for the EU. It is funded by the European Union fisheries budget (EMF) and the European Fisheries Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD).
KEY POINTS
Under the EMFF, EU countries are responsible for:
fostering environmentally sustainable, resource efficient, innovative, competitive and knowledge-based fisheries and aquaculture;
fostering the implementation of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP);
increasing employment and territorial cohesion, fostering marketing and processing;
supporting the Implementation of the International Maritime Organisation (IMO).
EMFF expenditure is divided between the EAFRD and the EFMFF, with the latter being the main source of EMFF funding.
EU countries are also responsible for managing the EU fisheries budget through a single EMFF operational programme.
Each year, EMFF spending is divided into the following categories:
Fisheries, including fishing vessels;
aquaculture, including fish and shellfish;
seafoods, including oysters, mussels, clams and clams;
other fisheries, such as fisheries and fisheries research and development;
environmental protection, including the protection of marine protected areas;
sustainability of fishing activities;
protection of the environment;
the protection of human health and the environment.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
It has applied since 26 February 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 26 February 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU fisheries policy is part of the Europe 2020 strategy.
For more information, see:
‘Fishing in Europe’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1308/2013
26.2.2013
-
OJ L 183 of 26.3.2013, pp. 1-8
RELATED DOCUMENTS
   
Regulations (EU), Euratom and the Commission have adopted a number of implementing acts to implement the European fisheries policy. These include:
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU of 27 February 2014 on the common framework for EU fisheries policies and programmes (see summary);
Council Implementing Regulation (Euratom) 2018/853 of 27 March 2018 on the EU Fisheries Fund and on the Common fisheries policy programme (See summary).

Summary 138:
Transparency of retail investment products offered to retail investors (PRIIPs)
Transparency and disclosure of PRIIPs (Retirement Investment Products)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — transparency and disclosure requirements for retail investments in pension funds
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the transparency of retail investments offered to investors by the financial services industry.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all retail investments* offered by financial services companies to investors in pensions.
It does not apply to individual insurance accumulation or saving products that do not offer investment opportunities and deposits solely exposed to interest rates.
The key information document (KIT) must be provided by the PRIIP manufacturer to the retail investor in good time before any transaction is concluded. The KIT must contain:
the product’s investment policies and its objectives;
the investment targets;
a description of how the investment targets are achieved;
financial jargon and terminology which is not immediately clear to retail investor.
Manufacturers must ensure that the KIT is produced in a standardised format which allows retail investors to compare different PRIs, since consumer behaviour and capabilities are such that the format, presentation and content of information must be carefully calibrated to maximise understanding and use of information.
EU countries must designate the competent authorities responsible for supervising the products covered by the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Retirement investment products’ is one of the key pillars of the European Pillar of Social Rights (PSR), which aims to protect the interests of pensioners and their families.

Summary 139:
Harmonising the resolution of banks in the EU
Harmonomically harmonising the Resolution of Banks in the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — resolution of failing banks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the rules relating to the resolution* of banks across the EU and provides for cooperation among resolution authorities when dealing with the failure of cross-border banks.
It sets up the Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM).
KEY POINTS
The resolution of a failing bank is a key element of the EU’s banking union (Budapest strategy), which aims to restore trust and credibility in the banking sector. It aims to ensure that the EU has a level playing field for all banks in order to foster economic growth and ensure the proper functioning of the internal market for banking services.
The regulation sets up a single resolution board, which is chaired by the European Banking Authority (EBA) and composed of representatives of the national authorities of the participating EU countries. It has the power to adopt a resolution resolution plan and to adopt the terms and conditions of the resolution process.
EU countries are required to set up a Single Resolution Fund (SRF), which is managed by the EBA and is funded by the EU countries’ national authorities. The SRM is responsible for providing the EU with the financial resources needed to fund the SRM.
A resolution plan is adopted by the national resolution authorities of each participating EU country and the European Investment Bank (EIBBanks).
The SRMoves to implement the resolution plan include:
the establishment of a resolution board and a resolution committee;
the setting up of a national resolution board;
setting up of an EU-wide resolution committee to ensure consistency in the application of resolution rules;
drawing up a resolution plan for each participating country;
establishing a resolution mechanism for each EU country.
In addition, the resolution board must:
ensure that national resolution rules, practices and approaches to burden-sharing remain national and that financial resources for funding resolution are raised and spent at national level;
apply the same principles and procedures for resolution and financial backstops in all EU countries, irrespective of whether they are national or EU-based;
act in the best interest of the public and the creditors of the failing bank;
take all necessary measures to ensure the stability of the banking system and the protection of the financial interests of all stakeholders, including the public, in the event of a bank failure.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Directive 2014/59/EU has applied since 1 January 2019 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The banking union has been in place since the end of 2008 and has been working towards its goal of creating a single EU banking union since 2010.
For more information, see:
Resolution of banks (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Council Regulation (EEC) No 852/2018 of 21 December 2018 on the resolution and resolution committee of the European System of Banks (SSM) (OJ L 347, 24.12.2018, pp. 1-24)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
 
  Е   баранска  х  тарски

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions at European Union airports — balancing the impact of aircraft noise
Noisive aircraft — balancing noise
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — noise-related operational restrictions at EU airports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the sound level of air traffic by reducing the noise impact from aircraft at the EU’s airports.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all airports in the European Union (EU), including those located in non-EU countries.
It does not apply to airports located in the EU countries of the European Economic Area (EEA).
It applies to airports in which the noise level of civil subsonic aircraft exceeds the maximum permissible level.
EU countries must designate one or more competent authorities responsible for the process to be followed when adopting operating restrictions. The competent authorities must be independent of any organisation which could be affected by noise.
Noise mitigation measures must be identified at the level of the airport where a noise problem has been identified.
The measures must not lead to delay in the implementation of operational measures which could immediately alleviate the noise effect without substantially affecting the operational capacity of an airport.
Airport authorities must also ensure that local residents, or their representatives, and relevant authorities are consulted to ensure that they are consulted in a transparent way on the intended actions.
Annex II to the regulation sets out the responsibilities of the air traffic management network coordinator, who is responsible for monitoring the noise situation at the airports of the EU.
Member States must ensure that:
airport operators are informed of any noise mitigation measures to be taken at their airports;
airports are notified of any operational measures that may be taken to mitigate the noise at their premises;
noise levels at the airport are assessed, in accordance with Annex III of the regulation;
measures are adopted and sufficient notification is provided for;
the measures are implemented;
dispute resolution is available for.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce the noise of aircraft at its airports, including:
Directive 2006/93/EC on the removal of the noisiest aircraft from airport operations (see summary);
Directives 2002/30/EC and 2004/96/EC (see consolidated version);
EU Regulation (ECAC Doc 29) on noise management;
EU Treaty on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States on the right to fly and on the establishment of a European Union Aviation Safety Agency;
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 677/2011 on noise mitigation at airports and repealing Regulation (EEC) No 852/2004;
European Commission Regulation (CFSP) No 552/2013 on the use of operating restrictions in the field of noise management at airports; and
Commission Decision (EU/2014/722/EU) on the application of the Balanced Approach to the noise management of airports at airports (See summary).

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings — common rules for cross-border proceedings in insolvencies in the European Union (EU) (European Commission).
Cross-Border insolvencations — Common rules for crossing borders
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2006 — common procedures for cross border insolvent proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the application of certain of the EU’s common rules on insolvent undertakings.
It sets out common procedures to be followed by EU countries when insolvent companies or legal persons move to another EU country.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all insolvent entities, legal persons and legal entities in the EU. It does not apply to insolvent individuals or legal entities.
EU countries must ensure that:
the insolvent company or legal person or company is located in an EU country other than that of its registered office;
the debtor is carrying out an economic activity with human means and assets in that EU country, provided that the debtor is not exercising an independent business or professional activity;
there is a presumption that the centre of the debtor's main interests is in the country where the company's central administration is located, and where a comprehensive assessment of all the relevant factors establishes, in a manner that can be ascertainable by third parties, that the company is actually located in that other EU country and that its actual centre of management and supervision and of the management of its interests is located there.
the court of a EU country has jurisdiction to open proceedings in the case of the insolvences.
The court of the country in which the insolvent debtor is located has the right to:
preside over the main insolvent assets;
restore the main debtor's assets to the EU country where they are located;
grant a temporary stay on enforcement actions brought by individual creditors where such actions could adversely affect negotiations and hamper the prospects of a restructuring of a debtor's business.
Creditors’ rights
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulation. The report concluded that the regulation is functioning well in general but that it would be desirable to improve its application.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY ENTER INTO FORCE?
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Expiry: when a company ceases to be able to pay its debts as they fall due.
* Creditors: the general body of creditors of a company which is insolvent and which is responsible for the payment of the debts of the company and for the management and control of its assets.

Summary 142:
Carriage of passengers and their luggage by sea — liability of the carrier and/or performing carrier
Carrying passengers on board a passenger vessel is governed by the same level and nature of liability as international and national transport.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 — liability rules for the carriage of passengers by sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the liability rules of the passenger carrier and of the performing carrier for damage caused to passengers in the event of a maritime accident.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies from the date of the entry into force of the Athens Convention for the Community, and in any case from no later than 31 December 2012.
Passengers are covered by Article 3(3) of the convention.
Carriers and performing carriers must ensure that passengers are provided with appropriate and comprehensible information regarding their rights under this regulation.
The liability of passengers is governed only by the regulation. In the absence of any such applicable national legislation, the carrier or performing carrier is responsible only for the loss of mobility equipment or other specific equipment used by a passenger with reduced mobility.
Compensation for damage to mobility equipment
The passenger is entitled to compensation for:
the loss of, or damage to, mobility equipment in his or her cabin or on board the vessel;
the damage to the mobility equipment of a passenger who is accompanying a vehicle or live animals which is covered by a contract for carriage of goods not governed by this regulation; or
the compensation of the person who received it or who is not the person entitled to damages.
Liability of the carrying carrier
The carrier and performing carrier are responsible for the liability of their passengers and for their cabin luggage.
In the case of loss of cabin luggage, the passenger and the carrier are liable for the damage caused by the carrier.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The Convention on the Carriage of Passengers and their Luggage by Sea, 1974, was adopted on 1 November 2002 under the auspices of the International Maritime Organisation (IMO).
For more information, see:
‘Carriage by sea’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 of 31 October 2008 on the liability requirements for the passenger carriage by sea (OJ L 183, 31.10.2008, pp. 1-12)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, ER) No 555/2008 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 143:
European Union (EU) rules on the establishment of rail freight corridors for competitive freight
European Union rules on rail freight
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2006 on the European Union’s rules on establishing rail freight networks for competition
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the efficiency of freight transport relative to other modes of transport.
It sets up a network of freight corridors in the EU to facilitate the movement of freight between EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries with a rail border with another EU country, unless this obligation has already been met under Directive 2001/14/EC on the allocation of railway infrastructure capacity and the levying of charges for the use of rail infrastructure.
Each EU country must designate at least one freight corridor for competition purposes.
The EU countries are responsible for:
designating the initial freight corridors;
establishing a management board to manage the development of the freight corridor;
drawing up a long-term investment plan for the corridor and drawing up an indicative investment plan;
setting up an advisory board to advise the management board on the management of the corridor.
EU countries must ensure that:
the freight corridor is open to all types of freight, irrespective of the type of transport;
the management board is composed of representatives of railway undertakings interested in using the corridor; and
the board draws up a management plan to draw up the investment plan.
the executive board takes its decisions on the basis of mutual consent of the representatives of the authorities of the EU countries concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLACE IN FORCE
  
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2006.
BACKGROUND
 
 Â    —
For more information, see:
Railways (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Rail freight: the transport of goods by rail between the EU and non-EU countries, such as by road, by rail, by air or by sea.
* Freight corridor: the railway infrastructure of an EU country which is used exclusively for the transportation of freight by rail.

Summary 144:
Road transport operators — EU rules on admission to the occupation of road transport operator
Road transport professionals — European rules on entry into the occupational field
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 — rules on the admission of undertakings to the occupational occupation of the road transport operators
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that EU countries apply the same rules to road transport undertakings across the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all undertakings engaged in road passenger transport services exclusively for non-commercial purposes or which have a main occupation other than that of road passenger operator.
It does not apply to undertakings which operate solely by means of motor vehicles with a maximum authorised speed not exceeding 40 km/h.
EU countries may, however, lower this limit for all or some categories of road transportation operations.
Authorisation to pursue the occupation
An EU country must authorise a road transport undertaking if it considers that:
the undertaking satisfies the conditions laid down in the regulation;
it has an effective and stable establishment;
the undertakings have a minimum financial standing to ensure their proper launching and administration.
The EU country in which the undertaking is established must ensure that the undertaking meets all the conditions for the granting of an occupational licence.
Procedure
The competent authority of the EU country which authorises an undertaking to pursue its occupation must:
establish a model certificate to prove that the undertakings meet the conditions set out in this regulation and that they have the necessary financial standing;
issue the model certificate in accordance with the regulation’s requirements;
ensure that the organisation of the undertaking complies with the requirements laid down by the competent authority;
conduct an investigation procedure to establish whether the undertaking has complied with the conditions of the regulation.
If an EU country considers that an undertaking has not complied with these conditions, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for its decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
  — road transport
For more information, see:
Road Transport (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Road transport operator: any natural person, any legal person, whether profit-making or not, any association or group of persons without legal personality, or any official body, whether having its own legal personality or being dependent upon an authority having such personality, engaged in the transport of passengers or any natural or legal person engaged in transport of freight with a commercial purpose.
* Driver: any person who drives a motor vehicle for the purpose of transporting passengers, goods or passengers in a vehicle other than a passenger vehicle.

Summary 145:
Cooperation between EU countries' social security institutions
Cooperation among EU countries’ social security organisations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the coordination of the social security systems of the Member States
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the cooperation between the European Union’s (EU) social security (SSP) institutions.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
provide all data necessary for establishing and determining the rights and obligations of persons to whom the regulation applies.
Provide the relevant institutions with all relevant information, including:
the identity of the person concerned;
the details of the national bodies likely to be involved in implementing the regulation;
details of the persons concerned who could be entitled to benefit from the regulation.
The relevant institutions must forward the information and issue the documents to the people concerned without delay and in all cases within any time limits specified under the legislation of the EU country in question.
If there is doubt about the validity of a document or the accuracy of the facts on which the particulars contained therein are based, the institution issuing the document must ask the issuing institution for the necessary clarification and, where appropriate, the withdrawal of that document.
In the event of a dispute between the institutions or authorities of two or more EU countries about which institution should provide the benefits in cash, the person who could claim benefits if there was no dispute should be entitled, if that dispute was brought by the institution that first applied the regulation, to the benefits of the institution which first granted the benefits.
Where there is a difference of views between the EU countries on the place of residence of a person to whom this regulation applies, the Commission may refer the matter to the institutions of the other EU countries. The Commission may also refer the case to the European Court of Auditors.
When an EU country does not provide the relevant information to the institution of the same EU country, the relevant institution of that EU country must provide the information to that institution and the Commission must submit a report to the Council and the European Parliament on the application of the regulation and the rules for the recovery of claims relating to certain levies, duties, taxes and other measures.
This regulation replaces Regulation (EEC, No 574/72 of 21 March 1972 fixing the procedure for implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1408/71 on employment and social security schemes to employed persons and their families moving within the Community.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality, comparability and accessibility of European Union (EU) statistics on health and work at work.
KEY POINTS
Scope

EU countries must collect and disseminate data concerning public health on work at EU level on a voluntary basis.

National statistics authorities must ensure that:
the data collected are collected in accordance with the principles set out in the European Statistics Code of Practice (ESP);
the collection and transmission of the data are confidential;
the statistical methodologies and data collections are in line with the requirements of the European statistical code of practice (ESDP);
data are produced and transmitted in a way that is consistent with the ECHI and with the developments and achievements resulting from Community action in the area of health;
data collected and transmitted are collected on the basis of national surveys or surveys or survey modules or national administrative or reporting sources.

Summary 149:
Access to the EU’s natural gas transmission networks (EUTN)
Access to natural gas’ transmission networks
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — conditions for access to the European Union (EU, Euratom) transmission networks for natural gas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that natural gas is traded on a level playing field across the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
transmission networks* in all EU countries;
transport networks in the European Economic Area (EEA) and in the Baltic Sea and the Black Sea regions;
third-party access services* to the network of transmission system operators.
It does not apply to existing transmission networks.
Tarrifs
Transmission tariffs must be calculated according to the actual costs incurred, insofar as such costs correspond to those of an efficient and structurally comparable network operator, and are transparent, as well as the need to provide appropriate return on investments and incentives to construct new infrastructure, including special regulatory treatment for new investments as provided for in Directive 2009/73/EC (see summary).
National regulatory authorities must also have access to data that provides information on operational decisions made by supply undertakings.
National authorities may establish an entity or body to carry out one or more functions typically attributed to the transmission system operator, which must be subject to the requirements of this regulation.
The European Transmission System Operator for Gas (ENTSO for Gas) is responsible for administering the EUTN and for ensuring that it operates in accordance with the rules of the regulation. It is assisted by the European Gas Regulatory Forum (EGRF) and the European Energy Regulators’ Office (EERO).
The ETSO is responsible, among other things, for:
monitoring the operation of the network;
coordinating the activities of the national regulatory authorities;
assisting the ENTSO in the implementation of the regulations;
ensuring that the regulation is implemented in line with the principles of subsidiarity, as set out in Article 5 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU;
providing the ETRO with the information it needs to implement the regulation;
exercising its role as a forum for the exchange of information and best practice in the field of natural gas.
EnTSO responsibilities
The Commission must ensure that:
the regulation is complied with;
the ETRN and the third party access services are sufficiently compatible;
there is a common minimum standard of access in practice throughout the EU, to allow the benefits accruing from a well-functioning internal market in natural gas to be exploited.
In addition, the Commission must establish or adopt guidelines for good practice to ensure the minimum degree of harmonisation required to achieve the aims of this Regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: a natural gas distribution or transmission network operator.
* Third party access service: a service provided by a third party, such as a gas storage or distribution facility, to the end user of the transmission or distribution system.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —   —
Commission Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 1 January 2018 on the conditions for Access to the Network of Transmission Systems for Natural Gas (OJ L 183, 1.2.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 150:
Agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries on civil and commercial matters
Agreements involving the EU or the European Union (EU) on civil matters
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 — procedures for EU countries to conclude agreements with third countries on matters falling within the exclusive competence of the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a procedure to authorise an EU country to amend an existing agreement or to negotiate and conclude a new agreement with a third country, subject to the conditions laid down in this regulation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that are members of the European Economic Area (EEA).
It does not apply to EU countries which are not part of the Schengen area.
Amendments to existing agreements
An EU country wishing to conclude an agreement with another EU country must notify the European Commission of its intention to do so.
The Commission must then notify the EU country concerned within 90 days of receiving the notification of its intentions to open formal negotiations on an agreement.
If the envisaged agreement meets the conditions set out in Article 4 of the regulation, the Commission must give a reasoned decision on the application of that EU country authorising it to open negotiations on that agreement. The Commission may propose negotiating guidelines and may request the inclusion of particular clauses in the agreement. If necessary, the agreement may be replaced by a subsequent agreement on the same subject matter, or for a direct replacement of the relevant provisions of the agreement by the provisions of such as those of such subsequent agreement. It must also be kept fully informed throughout the different stages of the negotiations and be allowed to participate as an observer in the negotiations as regards those matters.
Notification to the Commission
The notification must be given by letter or by electronic means.
EU countries must inform the Commission of the date on which they intend to enter into formal negotiations with the other EU country.
Authorisation to conclude the agreement
The agreement must enter into force within 30 days of receipt of the Commission’s reasoned decision.
A reasoned decision must be made on the basis of a proposal for the conclusion of an agreement within 30 working days of the notification.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE INTO FORCE?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Agreement: a bilateral agreement between an EU or an EU-non-EU country and a third EU country on matters covered by the rules laid down by the Treaty on European Union.
* Civil and commercial agreement: an international agreement between the EU and a non-European Union country on the recognition and enforcement of judgments in civil matters and on the interpretation and application of those judgments.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratom and the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 December 2004 on the procedures for the signing and conclusion of agreements between Member States and third countries in respect of matters relating to areas of judicial cooperation (OJ L 183, 29.12.2004, pp. 1-8)

Summary 151:
Statistics on migration and asylum
Statistics on immigration and asylum (European Union statistics)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2008 — common rules for the collection and compilation of Community statistics on migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
   
It sets out common EU-wide rules for collecting and compiling EU statistics on:
immigration to and emigration from the EU territories, including flows from the territory of one EU country to that of another EU country and
the number of third-country nationals who have in fact left the territory.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Commission (Eurostat).
EU countries must supply to the European Union statistics office, Eurostat, statistics on the number of residence permits issued to persons who are non-EU nationals, disaggregated by the citizenship of the persons returned. They must provide Eurostat with the data within 12 months of the end of the reference year.
Eurostat must also provide data to Eurostat on the numbers of requests for taking back or taking charge of an asylum seeker, the provisions on which the requests are based, and the decisions taken in response to the requests.
The data must be provided in the form of:
statistics on the total number of persons returned to their country of origin or to their home country;
statistical data on the average number of asylum seekers who have left the country of their origin or have left their country following an administrative or judicial decision or act;
the numbers of transfers to which the decisions lead;
data on requests for information.
National statistics may be used to calculate alternative definitions of the terms ‘migration’ and ‘refugee’, ‘return’ or ‘asylum seeker’.
Data collection and transmission
The European Commission is responsible for collecting, compiling, analysing and disseminating the statistics it receives from EU countries. It must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) and the Committee on the Statistical Programmes of the European Communities of any changes to the data it receives.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Migration: the movement of people from one country to another by land, sea or air.
* Asylum seeker: a person who has been granted permission to reside in an EU country for a limited period of time and who is seeking to return to his or her home country.

Summary 152:
Strict requirements for statutory auditors and audit firms in the EU (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — statutory audits of public-interest entities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum requirements for the statutory audit* of publicly-listed companies in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to statutory audits* carried out by:
the statutory auditor or audit firm of an audited entity;
the audit firm responsible for the audit of the audited entities’ consolidated financial statements.
The statutory audit must be carried out on behalf of the audit firm.
It must be performed by an independent statutory auditor and audit firm, and must contain the following information:
a description of the statutory auditor’s role and responsibilities;
information on the independence of the auditor or the audit firms and on whether the audit was considered capable of detecting irregularities, including fraud.
An audit report must be prepared by the audit committee of the public-listed company. It must contain all the information required to be provided under Article 28 of Directive 2006/43/EC (see summary).
The audit report should in particular include:
details of the activities of the management of the company, including the composition of the board of directors and the number of employees;
a summary of the annual financial statements and the annual audited financial statements for the previous financial year and for the current financial year;
an estimate of the value of the assets and liabilities held by the company and of the liabilities of the group as a whole.
Auditors must be independent and must not provide non-audit services such as specific tax, consultancy and advisory services to the audit entity, to its parent undertaking and to its controlled undertakings within the EU.
EU countries must ensure that statutory audits are carried out in a way that does not undermine the independence and independence of statutory audit firms, and that the audit report contains all the necessary information to detect irregularities.
A statutory auditor may be referred to the European Systemically Important Financial Institutions Board (ESIB) if they fail to comply with the requirements of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/853 has applied since 1 January 2019. It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 852/2004 and its subsequent amendments.
BACKGROUND
The new European audit framework as established by this regulation and Directive 2014/56/EU of the European Parliament and the Council is designed to improve the quality of statutory audits.
For more information, see:
‘Strict statutory audits’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Statutory audit: an independent audit performed by a statutory auditor.
* Audit firm: an audit firm that performs an audit for a public-owned company.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 21 December 2017 on statutory audits (OJ L 347, 21.12.2017, pp. 1-18)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Eurat) No 952/2003 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 153:
Conservation of marine biological resources and fisheries
Convention of European Fisheries (CFP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 on the Conservation of Marine Biological Resources and the management of fisheries targeting them
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that sustainable fishing and aquaculture activities contribute to long-term environmental, economic, and social sustainability.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 2371/2002.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The scope of the CFP includes:
the conservation of the marine environment;
the management of sustainable fishing activities;
fishing in areas protected by environmental law.
EU countries must ensure that:
fisheries are conducted in a manner that is compatible with the conservation objectives of the Convention of the European Union (Cereals Directive);
fishers are responsible for minimising the negative impacts of their activities on the environment and the marine ecosystem.
Fisheries must comply with the minimum conservation reference size (MCR) and the landing obligation.
The MCR is set out in the EU’s multiannual fisheries management plans (MMPs), which are drawn up by EU countries and the European Commission. They are designed to:
ensure that the MMPs are in line with the MCPs’ conservation objectives;
set out the minimum MCR and the maximum MCR for each MMP;
define the maximum sustainable yield (MSY) and maximum mortality (MMC) for each MSY;
provide a framework for EU countries to share information on MMSs and MMCs;
establish a system for monitoring and reporting on the implementation of the MSPs.
National authorities must take the necessary measures to ensure the conservation and management of marine resources, including by:
monitoring and reporting the number and size of fishing fleets;
ensuring the protection of marine protected areas;
developing and implementing conservation measures and measures where marine resources occur in sea areas of different jurisdictional status and obligations to have due regard to other legitimate uses of the seas.
In addition, EU countries must cooperate at regional level in order to adopt joint recommendations and other instruments for the development and implementation of conservation measures or measures affecting fishing activity in areas of protected areas.
Management of fisheries
The Commission is responsible for the management and control of EU fisheries. It is assisted by the European Fisheries Management Agency (EFMA).
The CFP is implemented in a way that is consistent with other EU policies and, in particular, that takes into account interactions with EU actions in other maritime policy areas, such as the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA), the European Coast Guard, the European Border and Coast Guard and the International Maritime Organization.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION ENTER INTO FORCE?
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* MCR: the MRC is an international framework for the conservation of marine living resources and marine ecosystems.
* FISHING: the fishing of fish, crustaceans and other marine life for human consumption.

The common organisation of the markets in fishery and aquaculture products (CMO) (European Commission). The CMO is an integral part of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) and contributes to achieving the objectives of the CFP.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 of 26 February 2013 on the common organisation in the fields of fishery products and the fisheries and waters that they have historically exploited and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 104/2000
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out common rules for the production and marketing in the EU of fish and other marine products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the production, marketing and sale of fish, shellfish and crustaceans in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to imports of these products from non-EU countries.
It applies to fishery producer organisations (PIOs) and to producers of aquacultural products (POPs).
Eligibility for PIOs and POPs
Fishery producers must meet the following criteria:
fisheries must be managed in a way that minimises the impact of discards on the environment and human health;
they must have the necessary expertise to manage and manage fishery resources;
their products must be marketed in a manner that ensures the sustainability of their products;
the PIO must provide clear information on the ecological sustainability of such products and on the environmental impact of their activities;
pOPs must be open to small-scale producers.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The new regulation has applied since 26 February 2014.

Summary 155:
Securities settlement systems — prudential requirements for CSDs (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Securities settlements — prudentials for CSD (Regulations (EU, Euratom)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (ECB) No 552/2014 on prudential standards for securities settlement systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out prudential rules for the securities settlement system (CSD) operators in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that CSDs are subject to the same rules as market participants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all CSDs in the EU. It does not apply to CSDs operating in non-EU countries.
It applies to:
CSDs that provide services in an EU country other than the EU country where they are established, including through setting up a branch, including in the host EU country;
the competent authority of the host country that is mainly responsible for the supervision of that CSD.
EU countries must ensure that the CSDs comply with the requirements of the regulation by 31 December 2018.
The CSDs must:
ensure that:
they have the necessary financial resources to carry out their activities;
they comply with all the requirements set out in the regulation.
They must report to the competent authorities of their home EU country on the activities of their CSD and to the relevant authorities of the other EU countries;
when a CSD provides its services in another EU country, they must provide all information to the authorities of that is of relevance to the requesting authority.
A CSD is subject to certain requirements, such as:
a minimum level of capital requirements;
a maximum level of solvency requirements for the CSD’s core services;
an annual report on the operation of the settlement system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.

Summary 156:
Cohesion Fund for the environment and trans-European networks in the area of transport infrastructure (2013-2020)
Cohesion fund for the environmental and trans–European networks on the basis of Article 5 of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1316/2013 establishing the Cohesion Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Regional Development Fund (CDF) to provide financial support to projects in the fields of environment and transport infrastructure in the EU.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006 which established the European Social Fund (ESF).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The CDF is the main instrument of the European Union’s cohesion policy. It is designed to support projects that contribute to the sustainable development and energy of the EU and to the development of the trans-EU transport network in the areas of environment, energy, transport infrastructure and public health and social protection.
The funding is provided by the European Investment Bank (EIB) under the Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) and the European Commission.
Eligibility
The EDF is open to all EU countries and EU countries that are eligible for funding from the EDF.
EU countries are eligible to apply for EDF funding if they have:
investment in the environment, including areas related to sustainable development or energy which present environmental benefits;
transport infrastructure projects of European added value provided for in Regulation (EU countries’ national plans, in line with the guidelines adopted by the EEF;
the EDF guidelines adopted under Regulation (Euratom);
transportation infrastructure projects which are part of the EAF under the TEN-T programme, in compliance with the EEA guidelines adopted in 2013;
technical assistance.
Funding for transport infrastructure projects
The Cohesion fund provides €10.5 billion over the period 2013-2020 for projects of EU added value (EADV) of at least €10 million for each EU country. The EAFSPs are funded by EAFV and EDFSPs.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 157:
The European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the European Territorial Cooperation Fund (ETC)
The European regional development fund (ERGDF)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/849 on the European Regional development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE DECISION?
The ERDF is the EU’s main financial instrument for regions of the European Union (EU). It aims to support regions which are disadvantaged by natural or demographic handicaps and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions.
The regulation sets out the types of activities which can be supported by the ERDF, in order to contribute to the investment priorities within the thematic objectives.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The objectives of the EU regional development funds are to:
contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing their backwardness;
support regions which suffer from severe and permanent physical or social handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions;
provide financial support to regions with an average gross domestic product (GDP) of less than 75 % of the average GDP of the Member States;
promote the sustainable urban development of regions with a population density of below 20 000 inhabitants;
encourage the development of sustainable tourism, culture and natural heritage in the areas of the outermost regions, including the conversion of declining industrial regions, as well as the creation of new urban areas;
facilitate the exchange of information and communication technologies (ICTs);
support the sustainable development of rural areas with a high level of education and training;
improve the quality of life for people living in rural areas.
Eligibility criteria
The EU regions eligible for funding are those which have an average GDP per capita of at least €10,000 in the period 2007-2013 and an average GDP of €25,000 for the period 2009-2013.
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2014 sets out eligibility criteria for regions with GDP of €20,000 or less in the years 2007-2014.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The Regional Development Funds (ERFDs) are the main financial instruments for regions in the EU. They were established under Article 174 TFEU of the Treaty on European Union.
For more information, see:
‘Regional Development Fund’ on the EU's website.

Summary 158:
Cross-border and transnational cooperation programmes under the European Structural and Investment Funds (ERDF) (European Commission)
Cross-Border and cross-border cooperation programmes UNDER the European Social Fund (ESF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1301/2013 on the European Regional Development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the EU’s regional development fund, the European Strategic and Investment Fund (ERSDF).
It aims to support cooperation between EU countries and non-EU countries in order to reduce disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF supports cooperation between the EU countries’ regions with a view to:
fostering high employment that results in social and territorial cohesion, including through activities supporting sustainable tourism, culture and natural heritage as part of a territorial strategy aimed at achieving employment-friendly growth;
developing administrative capacity.
The scope of the ERDF is set out in the annex to the regulation.
Funding
The funding is divided into three main categories:
cross-border activities;
transnational cooperation;
regional cooperation.
EU countries are responsible for setting out the resources allocated to each of the different components of the European territorial cooperation goal, while maintaining a significant concentration on cross-bilateral cooperation, including each EU country's share of the global amounts for cross–border cooperation, the potential available to EU countries concerning flexibility between those components, and securing sufficient funding levels for outermost regions’ cooperation. In addition, the Commission is responsible for:
designing the list of regions that are to receive support under cross-based cooperation programmes in a simpler way, by cooperation programme.
selecting the operations
The selection of operations is based on a number of criteria, including:
the nature of the cooperation and the nature of its objectives;
the type of activities to be supported; and
the number of staff to be employed.
Financing
The EU countries have to ensure that the funding provided for in the regulation is:
contributed by the participating EU countries;
provided by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Development Fund (EDF);
provided in whole or in part by the EIB and the EDF; or
provided for by the EU country in which the cooperation is taking place.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The European Regional development Fund’ on the ESF's website.

Summary 159:
Trans-European energy infrastructure — priority projects for 2020 and beyond (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Trans- European energy infrastructure – priority projects to 2020 and further beyond (Commission Regulation (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 852/2018 — strategic energy infrastructure priorities for the period up to 2020
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the timely development and interoperability of energy infrastructures in order to achieve the energy policy objectives of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) to ensure the functioning of the internal energy market and security of supply in the EU, to promote energy efficiency and energy saving and the development of new and renewable forms of energy, and to promote the interconnection of energy networks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to energy infrastructure projects of common interest in the areas of electricity, transport and telecommunication. Projects must be part of the latest available 10-year network development plan for electricity and gas.
The Commission has identified 12 strategic trans-European infrastructure priorities by 2020, which are essential for the achievement of the EU’s energy and climate policy objectives. These priorities are:
1.
Ensuring a high level of safety and environmental protection of energy infrastructure;
ensuring that energy infrastructure is compatible with human health and the environment;
promoting energy efficiency;
increasing the share of renewable energy in final energy consumption by 20 % by 2020.
2.
Implementing the priorities
The priority projects must meet the following a selection process for the establishment of regional lists and for the setting up of the Union list.
3.
Projects are eligible for inclusion in the second and subsequent EU lists if they meet certain criteria, such as:
they meet the criteria set out in Directive 2008/114/EC on the identification and designation of European critical infrastructure and the assessment of the need to improve their protection;
they are in line with the objectives set out by the European Commission in the framework of the Europe 2020 strategy.
4.
Financing
The European Commission must ensure that the priority projects are financed according to the EU countries’ financial resources.
5.
EU countries must also ensure that:
the priority projects have the necessary financial resources to be financed;
the project promoters have the financial resources necessary to carry out the project and that the project is financed in a manner compatible with the EU budget;
all EU countries have the power to grant permits for the construction of the projects.
6.
Public procurement
The public procurement process must be transparent and transparent, and must be open to all interested parties, including the public and private sectors.
7.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2021.

Summary 160:
Alternative dispute resolution (ADR) — online out-of-court resolution of online sales or service contracts
Alternative disputeresolution (ADC) – online out of court resolution of electronic sales or services contracts
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/849 on alternative dispute resolution
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to provide consumers with an alternative way of resolving disputes arising from the sale of goods or the supply of services online.
It sets out rules for the use of the Online Dispute Resolution (ODR) platform to resolve disputes between traders and consumers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all online sales and service contracts between a consumer resident in the EU and a trader established in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
the sale or service contract between a trader and a consumer in another EU country;
the settlement of disputes concerning contractual obligations relating to the sale or the service contract.
The ODR platform is free of charge and allows traders to conduct the dispute resolution procedure with ADR entities listed in accordance with Article 20(2) of Directive 2013/11/EU.
ADR entities must:
provide an electronic complaint form for consumers to submit complaints against traders;
ensure that the complaint form contains all relevant information, including the trader’s name, contact details and contact details of the complainant;
set up a dispute resolution mechanism to resolve the dispute within 3 working days of the complaint being submitted.
Disputes which are initiated by traders or traders’ intermediaries and which are not initiated by consumers, but which involve a use of ADR procedures, are not covered by the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that traders who are covered by this regulation inform consumers about the ADR procedure by which those traders are covered and about whether or not they commit to use ADR to resolve their disputes with consumers. They must also inform consumers of the terms and conditions under which they are covered.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Online sales and services contract: a contract where the trader, or the trader's intermediary, has offered goods or services through a website or by other electronic means and the consumer has ordered those goods, services or goods on that website or on other e-commerce means.
* Dispute resolution: a procedure whereby the parties resolve a dispute by means of a court, arbitration or other legal means, unless the parties agree otherwise.

Summary 161:
Reforms of the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) and Regulation (EU) No 1306/2013 on the allocation of payment entitlements to farmers
Resolution of the Council and the European Parliament and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States on the re-introduction of the common agricultural fund for rural development and repealing Regulation (EC) No 73/2009
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition on the European Commission's website
Regulation
1.1.2014
-
OJ L 183 of 1.1 January 2014
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the allocation by EU countries of direct support to farmers in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Direct support is provided to farmers on the basis of a coefficient coefficient (i.e. the number of hectares of land that are eligible for direct support per hectare) of €2,000 for each hectare of land in each Member State.
Eligible areas
The regulation sets out a number of eligibility criteria, including the following:
the number of hectales of land which are eligible to receive direct support for each year;
the percentage of the eligible area of the holding for which support is claimed.
The maximum amount of the direct support is €1,500 for the following years:
1 hectare in each year for each farmer;
2 hectales in each calendar year for all farmers;
3 hectales for all small farmers; and for all medium-sized farmers.
Small farmers may receive more direct support than large farmers, provided that their agricultural activity is not marginal.
Large farmers may benefit from a higher level of support if they have an annual gross income of €10,000 or more.
EU countries may decide to reduce the amount of support granted to large farmers if they consider that they have sufficient income to cover the costs of their agricultural activities.
In addition, EU countries may reduce the level of payments to small farmers by up to 50 % of the average value of the annual direct support provided for by the EU’s basic payment scheme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULTRY ENTER INTO FORCE?
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Coefficient coefficient: the coefficient coefficient of the land area covered by direct support.
* Direct support: the total amount of financial support provided to a farmer by the end of each year.
